==PAGE:3==
 
II 
 
 
Abstract 
 
The aim of this thesis is to 1) identify and understand unsustainable behaviours in PSS, and 
2) develop a method to design for sustainable behaviour in product/service-systems (PSS) 
by identifying, consolidating, and improving methods and tools found within the field 
Design for Sustainable Behaviour (DfSB) to change unsustainable behaviours in PSS. This 
was carried out by identifying 19 unsustainable behaviours in PSS, which were classified 
according to four parameters linking to PSS: strategic characteristics, ownership dimensions, system 
characteristics and the institutional framework, chosen explicitly as literature suggests (Hüer et al., 
2018; Arnold Tukker, 2015) the transfer of ownership as the root to unsustainable 
behaviours in PSS and the parameters were found to represent that characteristic. The 
classification provides an in-depth understanding of the unsustainable behaviours in PSS.  
In the five most crucial steps for supporting a consolidated DfSB in PSS process, five 
methods from DfSB are selected, consolidated, and tested in a student workshop to evaluate 
their applicability for a PSS context deemed relevant. The methods are developed into a 
functional prototype linking the knowledge about unsustainable behaviours to the methods, 
thereby helping designers to identify, select and understand why the unsustainable 
behaviour occurs followed by selecting strategies and creating ideas to solve the 
unsustainable behaviour based on examples proposed from the findings. Lastly, the method 
shows to successfully live up to its goal in a scenario test and case study. However, it requires 
further testing with practitioners to increase usability before propagating it in the field.

IV 
 
 
 
Learning objectives 
 
Product/service-systems 
• Select a relevant PSS design process for integrating DfSB 
• Identify potential unsustainable behaviours in different types of PSSs in accordance 
with Tuckers PSS typologies 
 
Design for Sustainable Behaviour 
• Select the most applicable DfSB method to achieve sustainable behaviour in a PSS 
context 
• Investigate the field of behavioural design and draw on promising principles in 
customising the method  
 
Development of method 
• Evaluate application of DfSB method in a PSS context  
• Identify strengths and weaknesses of the DfSB method in PSS development  
• Customise a DfSB method targeted towards PSS development  
• Test the customised DfSB method on a case study  
• Identify opportunities for the improvement of the method  
• Critically reflect upon the applicability and limitations of the proposed method 
 
 
==PAGE:6==
 
V 
 
Terminology 
PRODUCT/SERVICE-SYSTEM (PSS): A set of tangible and intangible goods (products 
and services) that jointly  provide a function to meet customers’ needs (Goedkoop, 1999) 
and seeks to minimise environmental impacts 
 
CIRCULAR ECONOMY: A circular economy is based on the principles of designing out 
waste and pollution, keeping products and materials in use, and regenerating natural 
systems (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2017) 
 
SUSTAINABLE DEVELOPMENT: Development that meets the needs of present generations’ 
without compromising future generations’ ability to meet their needs  
 
BEHAVIOUR: An action or physical movement of a user determined by internal and 
external factors bringing an effect to its environment 
 
SUSTAINABLE BEHAVIOUR: An action performed by an individual that, when being 
compared to “best practice” has a similar or better sustainable performance 
 
UNSUSTAINABLE BEHAVIOUR: An action performed by an individual that, when being 
compared to “best practice” has a negative sustainable performance 
 
REBOUND EFFECTS: A behaviour that has unintended negative sustainable impacts on its 
surrounding or external systems despite sustainable efforts 
 
METHOD: A number of generalised steps and model(s) to work through to help designers 
achieve a desired goal 
 
TOOL: A means to aid individuals or groups in performing an action to obtain a goal more 
efficiently than without the tool 
 
PROCESS: A course of actions conducted following upon each other in order to progress 
towards a defined goal

==PAGE:9==
1. Introduction
==PAGE:10==
 
3 
 
The climate crisis is above us as our generation’s improved living standards have come with 
a consequence.  
 
The population is expanding and so is the global middle class which is projected to rise 
from 47% to 63%, making up for 5.3 billion of the population by 2030 (Kharas & Hamel, 
2018). Concurrently, our consumption patterns are accelerating, extending pollution and 
resource consumption to dimensions our planet cannot keep up with contributing 
drastically to global warming. At this rate, resource scarcity is only becoming more 
apparent (De Weck et al., 2011). In the meantime, waste generation is reaching new heights 
every year and is expected to increase by 70% in 2050 compared to the state of 2016 (Kaza 
et al., 2018). How do we keep up with this development and improved living standards that 
will ultimately result in self-destruct of our species? Continuing to do business as usual is 
not an option. We need to take these consequences seriously and start creating solutions 
that will work fast and last.  
 
Luckily, modern society is working hard to solve the climate crisis. Circular Economy is 
being highlighted by the Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2017) as one of the promising 
solutions as it is restorative and regenerative by design. Circular economy seeks to redefine 
growth by decoupling consumption from the exhaustion of finite resources. This is done by 
designing out waste and pollution, keeping products in use and regenerating natural systems 
(Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2017).  
 
Product/service-systems (PSS) are considered one of the most important means to facilitate 
circular economy (Dobbs et al., 2011; Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2013). PSS are based 
on new types of business models and offerings consisting of tangible products and intangible 
services to provide a somewhat more dematerialised value. Goedkoop (1999) defines PSS 
as “a marketable set of products and services, jointly capable of fulfilling a client's need”. By providing 
products as a service, the provider can optimise use, close material cycles by recirculating 
products, and ensure sustainable disposal. Hüer et al. (2018) found 18 articles describing 
the positive environmental effects that PSS can have on consumption, and Mont (2002), 
Professor in Sustainable Consumption Governance, mention that one of the essential goals 
of PSS “should be to minimise the environmental impact by [...] reducing consumption through alternative 
scenarios of product use”. Clearly, PSS can solve some of the most prominent challenges of our 
societies that contribute to the climate crisis.  
 
Although projections of PSS are promising, there is a flip side of the coin. Despite the fact 
that most PSS seek to mitigate consumption errors or tackle overconsumption, this is 
unfortunately not always the case. Several factors can negatively impact the output of PSS 
and sometimes even jeopardise their environmental benefits. These factors are most 
commonly classified under the term rebound effects. Rebound effects are systemic or 
behavioural responses that occur despite sustainable efforts and result in less impact than 
expected or ultimately higher negative impact (Hertwich, 2005). There are three types of 
rebound effects: direct, indirect, and economy-wide rebound effects (Sorrell, 2007). Direct 
rebound effects increase consumption of the product or service due to cost savings from 
higher efficiency of the product or service. In comparison, indirect increase consumption 
of other products or services due to cost savings from higher efficiency of the product or 
service. Economy-wide rebound effects increase the overall use of resources as efficiency, 
often energy efficiency, enables society-wide technological progression and production. An 
example many may recognise is the rise of electric scooters dispersed around many 
European metropolises within recent years. Users can rent e-scooters through their 
==PAGE:11==
 
4 
 
 
smartphone, pick up and drop them whenever and wherever offering flexible on-demand 
mobility. These e-scooters have created a higher demand as they are easily accessible and 
affordable, increasing consumption. Thus, the e-scooters are victim to direct rebound 
effects. However, there are other contributing factors to why they are not living up to their 
sustainable potential. E-scooters being littered around cityscapes, parked in the middle of 
sidewalks or thrown all over squares is a sight that is familiar to visitors and inhabitants of 
cities in which the e-scooters are offered. Ultimately, this could contribute to why the e-
scooters only have a lifetime of 29 days (Lindholdt, 2019). Considering both the increase in 
consumption and the less sustainable treatment of the e-scooters are, despite technologies 
interference, a result of human behaviour. From now on, these behaviours will be referred 
to as unsustainable behaviours as they lead to PSS performing less sustainably. Literature 
suggests that these unsustainable behaviours can be linked to the change in ownership as 
users are given access to products that they do not have ownership nor responsibility for 
(Hüer et al., 2018; Arnold Tukker, 2015). Thus, PSS create a space for less sustainable 
consumption in defiance of their sustainable potentials and intentions. Essentially a paradox 
as the change in ownership incentivises the providers to prolong the product lifetime and 
have products stay in circulation for longer. 
 
A wicked problem that calls for a solution. While there might be several angles from which 
the problem can be approached, approximately 80% of a product’s carbon footprint is 
determined during conceptualisation in the design phase (McAloone & Bey, 2009). Thus, 
focusing on methods and tools to help designers during this phase seems most impactful. 
 
A systematic literature review was conducted prior to this thesis to investigate design 
methods that mitigate unsustainable behaviour in PSS (Appendix I: Literature review). This 
study showed a clear gap in the field regarding methods that create sustainable behaviours 
in PSS. One of the most prominent findings was discovering the field Design for Sustainable 
Behaviour (DfSB), specifically working with behaviour change to achieve a more 
sustainable output. Selvefors & Renström (2018) defines: “Design for Sustainable Behaviour is a 
user- and use-centered design approach that can be applied to design products and services that create 
preconditions for a sustainable everyday life.” As it targets user behaviour, it has its core focus on 
the use of products. It has been widely applied to various products, in particular, to optimise 
the use of electronic products. The findings suggest that DfSB can accommodate some of 
the rebound effects and unsustainable behaviour in PSS, yet DfSB has never been applied 
in this context. Although products also exist in PSS, there are additional elements in PSS 
that seem to influence how products are used, which has not been tackled by DfSB before.  
 
1.1. 
Goal 
As the literature study discovered the lack of methods and tools helping designers design 
out unsustainable behaviours in PSS, this thesis aims to develop exactly that. Not only to 
ensure that PSS are used most optimally, but also to leverage their sustainable potential and 
harvest the systems’ environmental benefits. Based on the identified gap in the literature, 
the goal of this thesis is to consolidate, customize and evaluate DfSB methods and 
tools suitable for a PSS context to make the systems perform sustainably. 
 
Subgoals for the project are: 
• Understand the unsustainable user behaviours that occur in PSS 
• Review, consolidate and customize methods and tools from product and service 
design to achieve sustainable behaviour in the development of PSS 
==PAGE:12==
 
5 
 
• Test and evaluate the effects of implementing a consolidated design for sustainable 
behaviour method in PSS design and identify opportunities for improvement 
 
The measure of the goal is to help designers to design impactful behaviour change 
for sustainable behaviour. The goal will be sought achieved through following research 
questions:  
 
RQ1 What unsustainable behaviour exists across different PSS categories and how are 
they connected to the transfer of ownership? 
RQ2 How to support designers in addressing unsustainable behaviours and how to 
integrate it in the PSS framework? 
RQ3 How can behaviour intervention strategies mitigate unsustainable behaviours in 
PSS and achieve lasting sustainable change? 
RQ4 How can PSS development be affected by an increased focus on unsustainable 
behaviours? 
 
 
 
==PAGE:13==
2. Research 
methodology
==PAGE:14==
 
7 
 
2.1. 
Introduction 
This project aims to develop a new method, tool or theory for designers, why it is taking its 
methodological offset in the Design Research Methodology (Blessing & Chakrabarti, 2009). This 
is embodied in the study through the approach, including the four phases: research 
clarification, descriptive study I, prescriptive study and descriptive study II. Each phase will include 
smaller work packages, as presented in Figure 1. The figure also provides an overview of 
chapters in which work packages are conducted. Research clarification aims to find evidence 
of a research gap for methods to design for sustainable behaviour in PSS and define the 
research goal. Descriptive study I aims to identify unsustainable behaviours that occur in PSS 
through an empirical approach. The unsustainable behaviours will be further analysed to 
understand better what, when and why behaviours occur and by whom. The prescriptive study 
seeks to develop a method to avoid unsustainable behaviours in PSS. Lastly, the descriptive 
study II aims to test and evaluate the method, measure success and identify improvement 
opportunities. Due to the iterative nature of developing a method, the prescriptive study and 
descriptive study II will be conducted for the first conceptualisation of a method and then the 
improvement of the method. A more detailed description of the tasks to be performed in 
each work package is outlined in Appendix II: Gantt chart. 
 
 
Figure 1 Description of project process with employed research approach divided into eight work packages with indication of the five chapters in 
which work packages are conducted 
This study's approach will be comprehensive based on the sparse number of methods and 
tools tackling unsustainable behaviours in PSS. The limited research within the field calls 
for empirical studies to broaden and strengthen the general body of knowledge on why PSS 
performs as they do and how to tackle it. Therefore, a research type 7 (Blessing & 
Chakrabarti, 2009) will be carried out, meaning that the research clarification will be review-
based. The remaining descriptive study I, prescriptive study and descriptive study II will be 
comprehensive. 
 
==PAGE:15==
 
8 
 
 
2.2. 
Research clarification 
The goal of this phase is to clarify the research objective. The phase includes the activity to 
investigate how behaviour can be changed, building upon the preceding literature study to 
identify DfSB methods and tools to be used on PSS. Additional activities seek to establish a 
solid understanding of PSS and the mechanisms that operate within such systems; what 
makes them sustainable and what can jeopardise their impact, followed by seeking 
indications of the existence of unsustainable behaviour in PSS. Lastly, as the shift in 
ownership is assumed to be of significance for unsustainable behaviours, literature 
concerning this connection is particularly sought. During this phase, the research is found 
through literature searches, while the preceding literature study is a systematic review. This 
phase's outcomes are the research goal, research questions, and a research measure on 
which project success can be determined. 
 
2.3. 
Descriptive study I 
The goal of this phase is to identify and understand unsustainable behaviours in PSS and 
aims to answer RQ1. What unsustainable behaviour exists across different PSS categories and how are 
they connected to the transfer of ownership? It reaches for describing the unsustainable behaviours 
in a detailed enough manner to determine what to address in the following phases. The 
unsustainable behaviours are sought through literature search, including outreach to the 
most prominent researchers working within the field. Additionally, an empirical study is 
carried out to search for and validate the existence of unsustainable behaviours in various 
PSS. However, as the thesis has limited time resources, secondary sources are prioritised to 
support primary examples and observations. The phase seeks to describe and classify the 
different types of unsustainable behaviours that can be found. This is followed by an in-
depth investigation of what causes these behaviours to occur and whether that can be 
connected to specific characteristics within PSS, especially characteristics connected to 
ownership transfer. The expected outcome is an overview of unsustainable behaviours 
occurring in current PSS offerings supplemented by an understanding of why they occur 
and how they can be connected to the construct of PSS.  
 
2.4. 
Prescriptive study 
The goal of this phase is to develop a method to avoid unsustainable behaviours in PSS. 
The activities include reviewing, consolidating, and customising methods and tools from 
design to achieve sustainable behaviour in the development of PSS. The search for methods 
will take its offset within DfSB, and the systematic review conducted preceding this thesis. 
The research question this phase aims to answer is RQ2. How to support designers in addressing 
unsustainable behaviours and how to integrate it in the PSS framework? A supplementary search for 
DfSB methods and tools is carried out, followed by composing the desired method's 
requirements based on the findings from the descriptive study I. Next, a systematic evaluation 
and selection of methods and tools are carried out based on success factors and barriers 
identified in the systematic review, selecting the most suitable methods and tools. These are 
scrutinised for strengths and weaknesses to create a method supporting designers in 
mitigating unsustainable behaviours in PSS. Thereby, the outcome of this phase is a testable 
method that meets the identified requirements. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:16==
 
9 
 
2.5. 
Descriptive study II  
The goal of this phase is to test and evaluate the applicability and effects of implementing 
a design for sustainable behaviour method in PSS and verify whether it can help designers 
reach impactful behaviour change. It aims at answering RQ3. How can behaviour intervention 
strategies mitigate the unsustainable behaviours in PSS and achieve lasting sustainable change? The 
activities include conducting two tests, first with design students taking the PSS course at 
the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) and secondly with authors. These tests are 
carried out in a workshop format followed by participant evaluations. They seek to both 
investigate usability, applicability for PSS and the ability to reach the goal of designing 
sustainably impactful behaviour change. Lastly, a critical reflection of the applicability and 
limitations of the proposed method will be conducted. The reflection should answer RQ4. 
How can PSS development be affected by an increased focus on unsustainable behaviours? The desired 
outcome is a set of identified opportunities for improvement for the method alongside 
recommendations for further work. 
 
==PAGE:17==
3. Theoretical 
background
==PAGE:18==
 
11 
 
3.1. 
Introduction 
This chapter aims to establish a shared knowledge of the context in which the research takes 
place. This research draws upon three research fields: PSS design, behavioural design, and 
sustainable design (Figure 2). Firstly, an understanding of PSS is established as a point of 
reference. In the overlap between PSS design and sustainable design is sustainable PSS 
(S.PSS), which further explores the sustainability potential of PSS and how human 
behaviour can challenge this potential. Thus, the investigation of behavioural design seeks 
to understand human behaviour and how it can be changed through design. In the overlap 
between behavioural design and sustainable design is DfSB, which is examined to discover 
how to create sustainable behaviour. With an aim to stand on the shoulders of giants, 
especially these the two fields, S.PSS and DfSB, will be investigated as the overlap between 
the two is the target of this research. Thus, when diving into DfSB and S.PSS's literature, 
the aim is to understand what and how the two fields can be utilised to enhance sustainable 
behaviour in PSS. 
 
 
Figure 2 The overlap between the three research fields PSS design, behavioural design and sustainable design will be investigated 
 
3.2. 
Product/service-systems  
A PSS aims at decoupling economic growth from mere product sales by integrating services 
with products. The integration of services will develop new business opportunities for 
companies and aid in creating value for the customer.  
 
3.2.1. PSS definition 
Mont (2001) defines a PSS as “a system of products, services, networks of actors and supporting 
infrastructure that is developed to be competitive, satisfy customers and be more environmentally sound than 
traditional business models”. Based on this definition,  Mont (2004a) also introduces a 
framework for analysing PSS (Figure 3). The framework has four central PSS elements: 
product, service, infrastructure, and network. The element product refers to tangible objects. As 
products enter a PSS, it may require changes in the product design to support the other 
elements in the PSS, e.g. design for multiple use-cycles, prolonged lifetime, refurbishment, 
etc. Same applies for the element service being the intangible offerings. Services in a PSS 
have diverse characteristics, and can both be additional services to support the use of the 
product, a service providing access to a product or the result of the product. Regardless, the 
services should be designed to incentivise environmentally sound use and create customer 
==PAGE:19==
 
12 
 
 
satisfaction. Infrastructure refers to both collective and private infrastructures in society such 
as roads, waste collection systems, communication channels, etc. This element is a necessity 
in delivering a PSS and can both provide opportunities or constraints. Sometimes 
additional infrastructure needs to be implemented, e.g. charging stations for electric sharing 
vehicles. The final element is network and describes the actors needed to provide PSS. A PSS 
may require a more extensive network of actors to provide offering as more functions are 
managed by a PSS provider. For all the PSS elements, they must be designed to support 
each other cohesively.  
 
 
Figure 3 PSS framework adapted from (Mont, 2004b) 
Underlying the PSS elements is three criteria ensuring PSS feasibility: business viability, 
customer satisfaction and environmental soundness. PSS feasibility describes the performance of 
PSS, why the three criteria can be viewed as drivers. Business viability measures the 
profitability and competitiveness of the PSS. Business viability is closely coupled with 
customer satisfaction, which can increase competitiveness. PSS are challenged compared to 
traditional business models as users experience a higher level of uncertainty in this novel 
way of consuming. Thus, user involvement and education are essential. Lastly, the 
environmental soundness evaluates whether a PSS performs more environmentally sound than 
traditional business models. It can be argued that environmental soundness to a greater 
extent will impact business viability as lack hereof is a risk to the company if, e.g. carbon 
taxes are enforced. 
 
Finally, the PSS framework touches upon assessing the institutional framework: 
normative, cognitive, and regulatory structures as these may facilitate or hinder the broader 
acceptance of the PSS. The institutional framework builds upon the work of Powell & 
DiMaggio (1991) and Scott (1995). In the definition used by Mont, institutions are the 
”humanly devised constraints that shape human interaction”. Thus, institutions are used to describe 
how people's behaviour affects and is affected by institutions and how behaviour can 
become institutionalised. The normative structure describes society's values and norms that 
impact our behaviour and will take place if users feel it is socially unacceptable to behave 
differently. The cognitive structure is the learning processes helping users understand a given 
situation and includes decision-making regarding the use of products and services. 
However, this decision-making will occur instinctively as users will not be able to conceive 
other ways of acting. Thus, behaviours impacted by cognitive structures are closely 
connected to culture. Lastly, the regulatory structure touches upon the rules made by 
==PAGE:20==
 
13 
 
regulators and how these affect user behaviour. Behaviour impacted by regulatory 
structures happens as users fear punishment. Policy instruments can be effectively used to 
change behaviour such as carbon taxes, deductions for upgrading to energy-reducing 
technologies or clean energy sources. Thus, according to Mont, consumption patterns are 
highly influenced by both normative, cognitive and regulatory structures, especially for PSS 
providing sharing services where the ownership no longer lies with the user. 
 
3.2.2. PSS typologies 
As previously described, there are different ways of integrating services and products in a 
PSS. Tukker (2004) describes three different categories of PSS: product-oriented, use-
oriented, and result-oriented and his description of the three categories will be used 
throughout this section. These are further categorised into eight types of PSS (Figure 4). 
The gradual shift from value based in product content to service content aims to increase 
products' efficiency and reduce consumption. The services provided in a PSS should help 
run the product in a more efficient way. Thus, additional services such as free shipping will 
not transform a product into a PSS as the service does not increase the product's efficiency.  
 
 
Figure 4 PSS have three categories: product-oriented, use-oriented and result-oriented, where the content of service compared to product will 
increase, respectively (Tukker & Tischner, 2005) 
In the business model of product-oriented PSS, products are sold to customers with 
additional services, either product-related services or advice and consultancy services. The 
first type includes repair and maintenance contracts, take-back agreement, or supply of 
consumables for the product. The second type includes advice on using the product most 
efficiently, such as training of users, etc.  
 
In a use-oriented PSS, the business model delivers value by offering access to a product, 
and not through product sales. Thus, the ownership of the product is not transferred. Three 
different types of PSS can be used to provide use-oriented services. The provider is 
responsible for maintenance, repair, and control in all three types of use-oriented PSS. 
However, they differ in how the product is made accessible. In product lease, the third type 
of PSS, the lessee pays a fixed fee for using the product with unlimited or individual access 
to the leased product. In comparison, users must share the product with other users 
sequentially in product renting or sharing simultaneously in product pooling.  
 
==PAGE:21==
 
14 
 
 
Result-oriented PSS offer value by selling a result and ownership of products used to 
deliver the results stays with the provider. This category has three types of PSS. Activity 
management, where activities are outsourced to a PSS provider, e.g. cleaning. In pay per 
service unit, the provider sells the output of the product. Lastly, a provider can deliver a 
functional result. In this type of PSS, the products used to deliver the result are not inscribed 
in the offering as opposed to activity management. 
 
3.2.3. PSS characteristics 
The eight types of PSS across the three categories described by Tukker (2004) have different 
characteristics that influence the way PSS are used. Tan & McAloone (2006) identify seven 
different strategic characteristics that can be applied in PSS development: 
• Benefit orientation: A PSS can satisfy customer needs in different ways by 
having benefit oriented towards either selling a product, offering access to using a 
product, offering the result or a product, etc. The benefit orientation should be 
aligned with the customer objective of using the product. 
• Transfer of ownership: In a PSS, ownership of a product can be transferred to 
the customer permanently, transferred to the customer temporarily or remain with 
the provider throughout product lifetime. Installation of the product may require 
specialised knowledge, why ownership might remain with the provider until after 
installation. It can also be beneficial to have the provider keep ownership of the 
product if purchasing requires an investment that is not aligned with customer 
objective. 
• Responsibility during use: Products may require specialised knowledge to be 
installed, operated, maintained, etc. Thus, it can be beneficial to have the provider 
be responsible for the product during such activities. 
• Management of life cycle activities: Managing life cycle activities such as the 
correct use of the product, collection or disposal may be beneficial to outsource and 
have the provider performing these activities. 
• Availability of offering: Increasing the availability of a product by allowing users 
to see where products are placed, what number of products are available and how 
to reserve them can enable multiple users to share the same product. Offerings can 
be available at customers permanently, become available at the customer when 
needed or become available at the company when needed. 
• Expansion of benefits: Apart from providing core benefits oriented around 
activities surrounding the product (installation, maintenance, repair, etc.), a PSS can 
expand the benefits to include activities surrounding the customer such as 
supporting the core activities related to the use of the product e.g. adjusting a bicycle 
to fit the customer of a bicycle leasing service. The benefits can either be aggregated 
together or integrated with each other.  
• Economic value model: A PSS can construct the payment of their offering in 
various ways depending on what their economic value is based upon. Apart from 
the traditional one-time payment if ownership is transferred to the customer, a PSS 
provider might construct the payment based on the value per use, value of 
management activities, value of the result of use or other value models. 
 
==PAGE:22==
 
15 
 
 
Figure 5 Strategic characteristics of PSS (Tan & McAloone, 2006) with additional strategic characteristic, level of digitalisation, and 
indication of characteristics for the specific PSS categories from Appendix III: PSS morphology, where green is product-oriented, orange is use-
oriented and red is result-oriented PSS 
The strategic characteristic variations have been identified for the three PSS categories, 
product- use- and result-oriented in Figure 5. The characteristics are different across the 
categories, and thus, it seems appropriate to assume that different behaviours will exist in 
different PSS categories.  
 
3.2.4. Sustainable PSS 
Despite the definition of PSS by Mont (2001) includes environmental soundness, Haase et 
al. (2017) has investigated 34 different definitions of PSS, identifying reduction of 
environmental impact as the fourth most common characteristic of PSS occurring in 39% 
of the definitions. This indicates that there are several PSS definitions in which 
sustainability is not a key component. Thus, not all PSS is based on a sustainable 
foundation, and thereby not created with sustainable performance in mind. Though 
environmental sustainability is most popularly used, sustainability is defined as both 
environmental, social and economic sustainability and the balance between the three. In 
some cases, a PSS is simply created to build the most viable business, e.g. subscription 
models ensure continuous revenues for the business, but the environmental benefits are not 
taken into consideration. Another example is rental models where the product is 
transported to the customer resulting in fuel and emission costs, potentially decreasing the 
environmental and economic sustainability. Therefore, a term called sustainable 
product/service-systems (S.PSS) has arisen. This covers that “the design of the system of products 
and services that are together able to fulfil a particular customer demand (deliver a ‘unit of satisfaction’) 
based on the design of innovative interactions of the stakeholders (directly and indirectly linked to that 
‘satisfaction’ system) where the economic and competitive interest of the providers continuously seeks both 
environmentally and socio-ethically beneficial new solutions.” as defined by Vezzoli et al. (2017). 
Hereby pointing towards that the PSS structure will be constructed in such a way that the 
economic motivation will inherently encourage environmental and social sustainability. 
Below some of the environmental, social and economic benefits of S.PSS, some also referred 
to as benefits of PSS. 
 
==PAGE:23==
 
16 
 
 
Environmental benefits: 
• S.PSS are created to optimize the use of products. Resource consumption can be 
reduced by having more users per produced product posing additional potential 
environmental benefits through economies of scale by lowering the material and 
energy consumption in the production (Deckmyn et al., 2014; Carlo Vezzoli et al., 
2018). 
• The provider will be economically incentivized to decrease the product's downtime, 
calling for a more robust and long-lasting design. This will ultimately give higher-
value products encouraging leaner manufacturing (Deckmyn et al., 2014; Carlo 
Vezzoli et al., 2018). 
• As the provider is facilitating the use and in some S.PSS models is responsible for 
the consumption, it will be in their economic interest to lower the material and 
energy consumption of the product during use and to sell excess material (Deckmyn 
et al., 2014; Carlo Vezzoli et al., 2018).  
• Prolonging product life through the extension of benefits, life-prolonging activities 
giving products more professional care. This may also encourage upgradability to 
potentially more eco-efficient alternatives and design for disassembly, keeping the 
same products in a loop for as long as possible (Deckmyn et al., 2014). 
• Extending the provider responsibility enables a much easier closing of material 
cycles, ensuring highest value recirculation, recycling and proper discarding at end-
of-life (EOL) and increasing the rate of which these EOL products are utilised (Carlo 
Vezzoli et al., 2018).  
 
Social benefits: 
• Lower to middle-income groups are at times excluded from certain types of 
products due to the high ownership costs. By selling access-based consumption this 
increases the accessibility for these groups (Carlo Vezzoli et al., 2018). 
• Low to middle-income groups are given the option to buy “units of satisfaction” 
thereby given access to services such as maintenance, repair and upgrading they 
may not otherwise have access to (Carlo Vezzoli et al., 2018). 
• Low investment costs for the use of products gives better opportunities for new 
business start-ups and entrepreneurs (Carlo Vezzoli et al., 2018). 
 
Economic benefits: 
• Many S.PSS include services along the products' full lifetime, allowing a better and 
extended customer relationship (Carlo Vezzoli et al., 2018).  
• S.PSS often pose new and innovative business models may open up to new markets 
and strategic positioning for providers (Carlo Vezzoli et al., 2018). 
• Many PSS business models allow for a constant cash income and the possibility to 
provide increased value to the customers (Bocken et al., 2016). 
 
All these sustainable benefits are clear indicators for why S.PSS is a desirable part of the 
solution to current sustainability challenges. However, these benefits come with a flipside 
that is often referred to as rebound effects. 
 
3.2.5. Rebound effects  
Despite many PSS and especially S.PSS are developed to create a more sustainable 
consumption and overall sustainable performance, this is not always the case. The main 
reason for this is a range of circumstances and phenomena called rebound effects. Rebound 
effects are unforeseen side effects occurring as a result of how a PSS is constructed and 
==PAGE:24==
 
17 
 
consumed. Hertwich (2005, p. 85) defines it as “a behavioural or other systemic response to a measure 
taken to reduce environmental impacts that offsets the effect of the measure” springing from a traditional 
product context. They can occur as both positive and negative effects, yet they are mostly 
discussed as something undesired. Rebound effects are mostly separated into direct, indirect 
and economy-wide rebounds (Sorrell, 2007) and can be applied broadly. In this case, it is 
applied in the context of PSS. Direct rebound effects directly impact the PSS such as improved 
efficiency reducing the price and selling access to a product making initial investment cost 
much lower, resulting in increased consumption of the PSS. Indirect rebounds are where users' 
economic savings from utilising PSS results in using those savings on other goods and 
services, thereby offsetting the savings achieved and increasing their environmental 
footprint. Economy-wide rebounds are related to overall societal improvements, e.g. energy 
efficiency resulting in increased economic growth and increased electricity consumption. 
This improvement of the energy efficiency can, however, also decrease the price of products 
throughout the economy, skew the market where energy-efficient goods will be defeated by 
more energy-intensive ones and overall give a smaller return on investments.  
 
On top of this, rebound effects can be expressed through both time, monetary, 
technological and spatial use of resources (Weidema et al., 2009): 
• Time: when the improvements result in being more or less time-consuming 
compared to existing technology 
• Money: when the improvements affect the price to be more or less costly compared 
to existing technology 
• Space: when the improvements result in taking up more or less space compared to 
existing technology 
• Technology: when the improvements affect the availability of raw materials or 
specific technologies 
 
Again, this classification derives from the context of a purchased product, indicating that 
the field and knowledge about rebound effects are fairly researched. However, looking into 
rebound effects in the field of PSS is a different story. A quick search through Scopus and 
Web of Science returns only 141 and 32 results respectively when looking for the 
combination of rebound effects and PSS, indicating a lack of focus on these parameters that 
can potentially jeopardize the sustainable performance of PSS. 
 
3.2.5.1. Shift in ownership as a rebound effect 
Tukker (2015) suggests an additional parameter or driver to these rebounds directly linked 
to the PSS context on top of the above-mentioned rebound effects.  He highlights that 
“leased products tend to be used less carefully than products that are owned, and rented, leased or shared 
products may be returned earlier to the service provider in comparison to the lifetime of a product sold in the 
traditional manner”, indicating that the reduced ownership in these models results in a 
decreased sustainable performance of the PSS overall. This suggests that the rebound effects

in PSS span beyond Sorrell’s definition as these concern merely energy efficiency leading 
to overconsumption. In the article, Tukker identifies that, especially in business to consumer 
(B2C) contexts, the shift in ownership challenges the way products are used. He emphasises 
how consumers worship the intangible esteem value of ownership and control over their 
actual possession of new and fashionable products. Hüer, Hagen, Thomas and Pfisterer 
(2018) support the ownership dimension as something that potentially will negatively 
impact the sustainability of PSS. 
 
Demyttenaere, Dewit and Jacoby (2016) have investigated how the shift in ownership 
influences consumers and reports a set of ownership and system characteristics to describe 
the new relationship between products and users in PSS (Table 1). These dimensions should 
be considered as parameters that have an influence on the feeling of ownership for users. 
They conclude their article by recommending a more in-depth analysis of the dimensions 
applied to existing PSS case examples and developing a set of guidelines for designers to 
facilitate a sustainable PSS design process taking these dimension into account.

3.3. 
Behavioural design 
Behavioural design is a field that seeks to influence the behaviour of people through design. 
The design must create behaviour that is beneficial to either the individual, society or the 
environment. This can be achieved through nudging, persuading, or otherwise motivating 
people to behave beneficially (Khadilkar & Cash, 2020b). Behaviour change takes point of 
departure in understanding people, what they think and how they make decisions. Ideas 
for designs that change behaviour are be based on this understanding, and tested to 
demonstrate the behaviour before and after people have interacted with the design. Thus, 
behavioural design primarily draws on a combination of cognitive psychology3 and design 
thinking4. Many within the field base their understanding of behaviour on Kahneman 
(2011), where people's behaviour is mostly driven by unconscious decisions referred to as 
System 1 thinking. This type of thinking is not rational, but an automatic and intuitive 
decision-making process that determines an action. Behaviour can also be driven by 
conscious decisions referred to as System 2 thinking. This thinking is a reflective decision-
making process, which is slow and requires effort. System 1 thinking is much faster as people 
make mental shortcuts, called heuristics, to reduce decision-making efforts. Heuristics are 
rules-of-thumb of thinking and actions that enable people to make quick judgements. 
Research suggests that 95% of decisions are be based on System 1 thinking, and thus, 
behaviour is rarely rational. 
 
Khadilkar & Cash (Khadilkar & Cash, 2020a) defines behavioural design to consist of a 
goal and objects while drawing upon behavioural theories and user psychology, discovered 
through qualitative multi-case research. The goal is “the explicit, ethical design or positive 
behaviour desired by both the individual and society”. The primary object being the behaviours 
themselves and secondary objects as interventions and artefacts, proposing a central role 
for interventions and artefacts to achieve behaviour change. Both unconscious and 
conscious behaviour can be changed through interventions (Cash et al., 2017). Thus, 
behavioural design can be broadly applied. However, an immense contribution to 
behavioural design is DfSB (Khadilkar & Cash, 2020b), focusing solely on sustainability. 
 
3.3.1. Design for Sustainable Behaviour 
The design of a product, service or system influences the behaviour of users. Regardless of 
designers' effort to make sustainable products, how users interact with the product 
significantly impacts sustainability. DfSB seeks to ensure that users perform the desired 
behaviour by understanding how users interact with a product, service, or system and why 
they do so. 
 
Some of the first contributions to the field of DfSB is the research conducted by Lilley in 
her PhD thesis called Designing for Behavioural Change: Reducing the Social Impacts of Product Use 
Through Design (Lilley, 2007), which mostly focus on social sustainability. The unsustainable 
behaviours sought changed are the negative social impact of use, which she defines as: “Any 
action enacted or facilitated by the product or resulting from the behaviour of the user in the use of the product 
which diminishes the health, wellbeing, social equity or quality of life of others affected by the use of the 
 
 
3  Cognitive psychology revolves around mental processes that constitute, exert and regulate actions, 
behaviour and experience by investigating sensing, perception, conscience, thought processing, memory, 
problemsolving and creativity among others (Katzenelson, 2017).  
4 Design thinking is a creative, iterative process for problem solving that designers use to understand users, 
define problems, develop solutions, prototype and test (What Is Design Thinking?, n.d.). 
==PAGE:27==
 
20 
 
 
product.” In her research, she finds three design strategies to change behaviour: eco-feedback, 
behaviour steering and intelligence. Eco-feedback aims to enable users to make more informed 
decisions regarding their behaviour by providing them with adequate information about 
how their actions impact environmental and social sustainability. Behaviour steering use scripts 
to encourage users to perform the desired behaviour as intended by the designer, or by 
blocking undesired behaviour.  Lastly, Lilley suggests using the intelligence of products and 
systems (also referred to as persuasive technology) to control the user into performing the 
desired behaviour. These three strategies played an instrumental role in future research of 
DfSB as it suggested a scale of power in decision-making with either user in control of the 
behaviour, or products and systems in control (Figure 6). Furthermore, Lilley found that 
interventions should be prototyped and extensively tested on users to determine whether 
users will accept the intervention, but also evaluate their effectiveness in changing 
behaviour.  
 
 
Figure 6 Illustration of strategies distributed across power in decision-making adapted from Lilley (2009) 
Prior to Lilley's research, researchers within the design community had a growing interest 
in how the design of products impacted use behaviour and how users' behaviour impacted 
products such as the work by Rodriguez & Boks (2005). However, the interest mainly 
concerns consumer electronics to reduce electricity and water during use as supported by 
systematic review findings (Appendix I: Literature review) and thus, address environmental 
sustainability. Building upon Lilley's findings, Zachrisson & Boks (2010) suggest a model 
describing the effectiveness of the behaviour intervention strategies in relation to both 
control (equivalent to power in decision-making) and user’s attitude towards the desired 
behaviour to ensure acceptance. The model illustrated in Figure 7 aims to aid designers to 
select strategies as this had been identified as a gap in the field. 
 
Zachrisson & Boks place eight behaviour intervention strategies consolidated from the field 
on the scale of user in control and product in control. The strategies are information, feedback, 
enabling, encouraging, guiding, steering, forcing and automatic. User in control strategies 
requires the user to notice, interpret and accept, the intervention before deciding to change 
behaviour. Product in control strategies requires limited attention from the user to act upon 
the intervention as the product will dictate the behaviour. Zachrisson & Boks uses CADM 
(Klöckner & Blöbaum, 2010) to understand what influences sustainable behaviour, either 
situational, intentional or habitual and base the selection of strategies upon that understanding. 
The intentional influences distinguish between three user attitudes: positive, neutral or 
==PAGE:28==
 
21 
 
negative. Users with a positive attitude are willing to make an effort to act sustainably, while 
users with a neutral attitude will not be willing to make an effort, nor oppose sustainable 
behaviour change. Lastly, users with a negative attitude will not act sustainably due to, e.g. 
convenience or conflicting beliefs. It is found that users with a positive attitude towards the 
desired behaviour can be given high control. In contrast, users with a negative attitude need 
product in control strategies to obtain successful behaviour change. 
 
 
Figure 7 Relation of user attitude and distribution of control adapted from Zachrisson & Boks (2010). Positive behaviours represent the area 
of successful behaviour change, whereas negative behaviours represent unsustainable behaviours 
In 2012, Tang & Bhamra proposed their Design Behaviour Intervention Model to link the 
antecedents of behavioural or habitual change with seven design strategies, also placed on 
the user or product in control scale. They further add system or service to product in control as 
design categories. 
 
Although many authors within the field have adopted the distribution of control as a 
dimension to structure behaviour intervention strategies, alternative dimensions have been 
proposed. Lockton, Harrison & Stanton (2010) view the strategies to change behaviour 
through six lenses based on the function of strategies in their Design with Intent framework. 
The lenses aim at inspiring designers through different perspective or means in which to 
embody the behavioural interventions. In particular, one of the lenses, the cognitive lense, 
draws upon the findings of Kahneman, by proposing to affect user behaviour by 
understanding their biases and heuristics. Tromp, Hekkert, & Verbeek suggest two 
dimensions, hidden versus apparent salience and strong versus weak force, resulting in four 
categories to distribute their proposed 11 strategies. They describe their framework 
targeting socially responsible behaviours and highlight the importance of considering 
conflicts between individual and collective concerns. 
 
DfSB has shown to revolve mostly around products, yet in recent years, research has been 
utilised in the context of circular economy. In 2018, Daae, Chamberlin & Boks described 
how behaviour change strategies specific for a circular economy context had been 
addressed very limitedly. However, it is pointed out that these strategies entail new activities 
that users may not have been involved with previously, such as self-repair, returning or 
reselling of products or even purchasing pre-owned products and that these activities, 
therefore, necessitate a behaviour change amongst users. Their research is thus an

==PAGE:29==
 
22 
 
 
investigation of existing behaviour change strategies that have already been applied in 
circular solutions in order to inspire how to enhance such activities for future circular 
businesses or improvements of existing ones. The examples are mostly enhancing circular 
behaviours with consumers in contexts where the provider is not engaging in how the 
product is being used thereby not having a focus on PSS-related behaviours and even 
conclude that “the circular model of leasing or sharing as a replacement for ownership is another topic for 
future exploration”, thus, indicating a need for investigating DfSB in PSS further. However, it 
is important to distinguish circular economy from PSS. Although PSS is a mean to facilitate 
circular economy, it is not equal to PSS. Products and services can be designed into a 
circular economy, but they do not need to be integrated as with PSS. Many examples of 
circular economy can be found in producers upcycling, recycling or downcycling materials 
to produce products instead of using virgin materials.  
 
Also looking through the lense of circular economy, Wastling et al. (2018) propose a 
framework to design for circular behaviour with a focus on PSS. The framework is called 
Design for Circular Behaviour and draws on previous work within the field, especially 
Zachrisson & Boks (2012) and Michie et al. (2014). The framework suggests five key 
components in the process. The component business model, through which the intervention 
should be delivered, is novel. Wastling et al. (2018) contribute further by introducing the 
selection of desired circular behaviours as a prerequisite before the three phases. A strength 
of the framework is the integration of business model components. In PSS, the product 
design is not the only element through which behaviour can be changed, but also through 
financial levers, customer relationship, etc. Unfortunately, the framework has not been 
tested on any case studies, why it is difficult to determine its usability. It seems rather a high 
level and thus difficult for designers to apply. 
 
3.3.2. Ethics of behaviour change 
An important aspect to consider within the field of behavioural design is ethics. In the 
definition of behavioural design by Khadilkar & Cash (2020), it is inscribed that the design 
must be ethical or create positive behaviour desired by both the individual changing 
behaviour and society. However, it can be challenging for a designer to determine whether 
the targeted user group desires an intervention – and whether it is ethical. Berdichevsky & 
Neuenschwander (1999) have defined The Principles of Persuasive Technology to make designers, 
or persuaders as they term them, critically reflect upon the ethical issues of applying 
persuasive technology. As they describe it, persuasion is an intentional effort to change 
attitude or behaviour, and technology is the mean. Principles include that designers must 
consider predictable outcomes and ensure users' privacy to the same extent as designers 
prefer their own privacy, not misinform, etc. Last of eight principles is the golden rule of 
persuasion: “The creators of a persuasive technology should never seek to persuade a person or persons of 
something they themselves would not consent to be persuaded to do.” Although persuasive technology 
is only regarded as a behaviour intervention strategy within the DfSB, these principles are 
relevant for the entirety of behavioural design and DfSB as guidelines to ensure ethical 
design. Another interpretation of ethical design is proposed by Daae (2014) in the context 
of DfSB. He argues that designers have a moral obligation to reduce users' environmental 
impacts through design, and prioritising users freedom of choice over the consequences of 
climate change can be regarded as unethical. However, as Daae also states, designers should 
have good arguments for why enforcing behaviour change is crucial and how likely 
designers can achieve success. 
 
==PAGE:30==
 
23 
 
3.4. 
Concluding remarks 
PSS aims to decouple economic growth from resource consumption and reduce mere 
product sales by integrating services with products. A common understanding of PSS have 
been established with four elements consisting of three different categories based on the 
frameworks by Mont (2004a) and Tukker (2004). As we build our understanding on Mont, 
PSS will be considered with the same attributes as those of S.PSS despite the varying 
definitions in the field. It was found that rebound effects can occur in PSS as they can in 
many other technologies, yet the documentation for this is relatively sparse. Some research 
even points towards some of the parameters that differentiate PSS from linear products, 
e.g. ownership as the root of a type of rebound effect that spans beyond electricity 
overconsumption. 
 
Looking towards behavioural design, it seems that this field could explain some of these 
rebound effects as a result of irrational unconscious behaviour as the majority of how 
humans behave are driven by system 1. In behavioural design, especially DfSB is a 
prominent field with the potential to change some of the unsustainable behaviours that 
seem to take place in PSS. The investigation of DfSB shows several authors pointing 
towards control as a central dimension, where less consensus around additional dimensions 
is established in the field. Furthermore, DfSB has not been applied comprehensively in the 
context of PSS. This is supported by findings from the systematic review of methods and 
tools to design for sustainable behaviour in PSS, indicating a research gap (Appendix I: 
Literature review). Lastly, it is important to consider the ethical implications when changing 
behaviour. However, this responsibility lies with the designer, and thus, a method to design 
for sustainable behaviour in PSS will not limit which strategies to use to change behaviour. 
 
 
==PAGE:31==
4. Understand 
unsustainable 
behaviours 
==PAGE:32==
 
25 
 
4.1. 
Introduction 
This chapter seeks to investigate and understand unsustainable behaviours in PSS. This is 
carried out by defining unsustainable behaviours in PSS through literature and identifying 
unsustainable behaviours. An in-depth analysis of numerous PSS-related parameters and 
classification based on PSS category are carried out to investigate if the unsustainable 
behaviours occur due to the construct of PSS as previously suggested. Lastly, the analyses 
are collected in an overview based on each identified unsustainable behaviour and the 
parameters that affect them. 
 
4.2. 
Methodology  
To gain an in-depth understanding of unsustainable behaviours in PSS,  this work is carried 
out in a four-step approach visualised in Figure 8 with the overall goal of answering RQ1. 
What unsustainable behaviour exists across different PSS categories and how 
are they connected to the transfer of ownership? 
 
 
Figure 8 Method approach to understand unsustainable  behaviours in PSS 
The first step is the definition of unsustainable behaviour in PSS, a crucial starting point in 
identifying unsustainable behaviours. This is followed by analysing the behaviours on 
descriptive parameters according to PSS category and clustering similar behaviours. The 
third step is analysing when and why the unsustainable behaviours happen, focusing on 
identifying reasons anchored in the construct of PSS categories according to the user activity 
cycle, PSS  strategic characteristics and variations, ownership dimensions, system characteristics and 
institutional framework. These will collectively be termed PSS-related parameters. Lastly, an 
overview of each unsustainable behaviour and all the different parameters that affect the 
behaviour is presented to identify the most significant PSS-related parameters impacting 
unsustainable behaviours in PSS. 
 
4.2.1. Definition of unsustainable behaviours  
To create a definition of unsustainable behaviour, a literature search has been carried out. 
First, the term sustainability is investigated to gain an understanding of what is unsustainable. 
==PAGE:33==
 
26 
 
 
How PSS can enhance sustainability has previously been explored, and PSS showed to have 
a high impact potential. However, how to measure the actual sustainability of PSS have yet 
to be investigated. Thus, considering the measurability of unsustainable behaviour in PSS 
in the definition is sought. Finally, the term behaviour is investigated to create a cohesive 
definition. This will also draw upon the understanding of behaviour from behavioural 
design and other definitions found in the literature. The definition must enable the 
following identification of unsustainable behaviours. 
 
4.2.2. Identification of unsustainable behaviours 
within PSS 
An understanding of unsustainable behaviours was established through the identification of 
unsustainable behaviours by following an inductive approach. A brainstorm of PSS 
companies and potential unsustainable behaviours within these enabled a better overview 
of where to search for actual unsustainable behaviours. The search was further focused on 
PSS with B2C offerings as these were considered most exposed to unsustainable behaviours 
as also supported by Tukker (2015). Furthermore, the customer review pages Trustpilot.dk 
and Tripadvisor.com were scrutinised for known PSS company examples identified in the 
brainstorm. The identified behaviours were described with both observation (the visible 
consequence of a behaviour) and the unsustainable behaviour (the user’s action) to ease the 
identification. Lastly, the identified behaviours were clustered to more generic groups 
describing the result of the behaviour. 
 
4.2.3. Classification of unsustainable behaviours 
within PSS 
Users’ reasons for doing the unsustainable behaviours were analysed to gain a deeper 
understanding with the 5 whys method, originally developed by Sakichi Toyoda. A 
brainstorm was carried out for each unsustainable behaviour, investigating the potential 
reasons for why the behaviour occurs. As this is a hypothetical procedure, the approach was 
exhaustive, and all feasible reasons were noted. 
 
To gain a broader understanding of how these unsustainable behaviours are connected to 
the construct of PSS, five analyses were conducted on the behaviours based on their whys. 
These whys were translated into heuristics to describe behaviours in a more generalised 
manner. These will be termed reasons. Analysing the whys with different frameworks gave 
rise to more, and these were added to the list iteratively. 
 
4.2.3.1. User Activity Cycle 
The unsustainable behaviours were analysed according to when in the User Activity 
Cycle the behaviour occurs divided into the three phases: pre, during, and post (Finken et al., 
2013). Pre covers the time from signing up to a PSS and any preparational activities before 
using the main product or service through which PSS offering is delivered. During is covered 
by the time actually using the main product or service, while post covers the time from 
terminating the use of the main product and other activities happening after the PSS has 
delivered its main offering.  
 
The analysis attempted to steer away from the initial purchase behaviours, where the user 
decides whether or not to use the PSS the first time. Instead, the analysis focus on behaviour 
==PAGE:34==
 
27 
 
when using the PSS. However, decisions regarding the re-use of a PSS are included as these 
are influenced by previous use of PSS. 
 
4.2.3.2. PSS strategic characteristics 
A PSS offering can be described through eight strategic characteristics: benefit orientation, 
transfer of ownership, responsibility during use, management of life cycle activities, 
availability of offering, expansion of benefits, economic value model and level of 
digitalisation. Different PSS will have variations of the strategic characteristics, although 
most variations will be generic based on the PSS category, as shown in Figure 5. Every why 
of the unsustainable behaviours were sought connected to the strategic characteristic 
variation of the PSS that cause the why to occur, and thus, affect the unsustainable 
behaviour. As the strategic characteristic variations describe provider’s design decisions, the 
whys were connected if these decisions influenced the user’s reasons. This analysis aimed to 
identify unsustainable behaviours that occur because of a PSS strategic characteristic 
variation connected to the PSS category, and thus, happen for different reasons than 
those found in corresponding linear models. 
 
4.2.3.3. Ownership dimensions 
The unsustainable behaviours were analysed according to the ownership dimensions 
(Demyttenaere et al., 2016) to fully understand and investigate additional parameters in a 
PSS context. Additionally, it sought to enrich the analysis of the PSS strategic 
characteristics, especially the transfer of ownership and how this influences the user’s 
perception, which has been highlighted as a subject of interest and potential significance 
(Demyttenaere et al., 2016; Hüer et al., 2018; Arnold Tukker, 2015). These dimensions are 
described more in-depth in Table 1, where each dimension should be perceived as a scale 
from low to high. First, the PSS offerings were placed on each dimension depending on 
how users would perceive the offering in a generic use cycle. Then, each unsustainable 
behaviour was linked to the ownership dimensions based on the whys resulting in a 
description of reasons. Demyttenaere, Dewit and Jacoby characteristics (2016) describe 
the ownership dimensions from the users' perspective, and therefore the behaviours were 
analysed from the same perspective. 
 
4.2.3.4. System characteristics 
The behaviours were also analysed according to system characteristics. These are 
presented together with the ownership dimension (Demyttenaere et al., 2016), but 
separated as they describe the system rather than dimensions affecting the user’s perception 
of ownership. Here the unsustainable behaviours were scrutinised for connections to what 
functionality the PSS fulfils according to Maslow’s Hierachy of Needs (1943) or whether the 
PSS offered and option to ascribe special meaning to the PSS and added as reasons. 
 
4.2.3.5. Institutional framework 
Concluding the analysis of PSS-specific parameters, the institutional framework was 
used to investigate whether the three pillars, regulatory, normative and cognitive, shape users' 
decision-making, resulting in unsustainable behaviours. According to Mont (2004a), the 
institutionalisation of PSS is necessary to gain a broader acceptance of this novel way of 
consuming. The institutions seek to “provide stability and meaning to social life” (Scott, 2013). 
PSS can achieve stability by either engaging in institutional creation or disruption to 
institutionalise other ways of consuming (Zvolska et al., 2019). Further, PSS can be affected 
by existing institutions from traditional consumption practices (Mont, 2004a). For each 
==PAGE:35==
 
28 
 
 
unsustainable behaviour, it was evaluated whether users might behave out of expedience to 
avoid punishment (regulatory), a feeling of moral obligation (normative) or because the user 
could not conceive other ways of behaving (cognitive) (Scott, 2008) and find whether existing 
institutions drives behaviour or the institutionalisation of PSS does. These were added as 
reasons. Furthermore, the reason that a lack of institutions may cause the unsustainable 
behaviours were explored as Mont (2004a) argues that PSS are not institutionalised, and 
thus, in need of common practices of sharing. 
 
4.2.4. Key insights  
The description of unsustainable behaviour together with observation of behaviour, result of behaviour, 
phase in user activity cycle, PSS category and the reasons connected to PSS-related parameters 
were collected in a database.  The database enabled a holistic analysis of each unsustainable 
behaviour as opposed to previous analyses of PSS-related parameters, where the 
classification is based on PSS category. The analyses were consolidated according to what 
(unsustainable behaviour, the result of behaviour), when (user activity cycle), how (strategic 
characteristic), and why (ownership dimension, system characteristic, institutional 
framework). 
 
The holistic analysis provided an overview of how unsustainable behaviour is affected by 
specific parameters. Both the number of unsustainable behaviours affected by a specific 
parameter and amount of parameters affecting a specific unsustainable behaviour were 
investigated. First, it is used to identify which PSS-related parameters mostly give rise to 
unsustainable behaviour, and second to identify which behaviours are mostly influenced by 
PSS-related parameters. Taking point of departure in the unsustainable behaviours, further 
enabled another understanding of the unsustainable behaviours based on the result of the 
behaviour as these describe the behaviours more generically. 
 
4.3. 
Results 
4.3.1. Definition of unsustainable behaviours 
To understand what unsustainable behaviour is, it is important to understand both what is 
sustainable and what is behaviour. The most common definition of sustainability comes from 
the report Our Common Future by the Brundtland Commission under the United Nations in 
1987. Here, sustainable development is described as: “Development that meets the needs of the 
present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs” and must consider 
both the environment, society and economy. However, products will require materials and 
energy throughout their lifecycle from production to disposal. Thus, it is difficult for 
products to be described as sustainable, why Andreasen et al. (1996) suggest another 
definition: “When a product complete a lifecycle with fewer or lower environmental impacts than another 
product with the same performance, it is more environmentally desirable.” A product cannot become 
sustainable. Instead, it can be said to be more sustainable than another product. As 
previously described, PSS seeks to enable sustainable consumption by seeking both 
environmentally and socio-ethically beneficial solutions. Here the same argument can be 
made that the PSS cannot become sustainable, but be more sustainable than alternatives. 
 
According to A Dictionary of Psychology, a behaviour is: “The physical activity of an organism, 
including overt bodily movements and internal glandular and other physiological processes, constituting the 
sum total of the organism's physical responses to its environment. The term also denotes the specific physical 
==PAGE:36==
 
29 
 
responses of an organism to particular stimuli or classes of stimuli.” Thus, the actions of a user towards 
a product is a behaviour. 
 
Measuring whether a behaviour is sustainable or not is a rather complicated matter. 
Behaviours do not have specific impacts that can be quantified. Yet, they can lead to a series 
of different more or less sustainable scenarios depending on what impacts are brought into 
consideration. They can be compared to situations leading to the same function. Take the 
decision to eat a banana for instance. Eating the banana in itself has an impact and may be 
different than eating a steak. Additionally, whether the banana derives from Portugal or 
South Africa influences the freight emissions, what pesticides may have been used during 
the cultivation impacts on the local environment and whether the peel is thrown in nature, 
send to incineration or a biogas plant has different outcomes and measures of sustainability. 
All of these scenarios influence sustainability and is a result of decisions and human 
behaviour. As seen in this example, sustainability is much more tangible when discussed in 
a comparative manner and not an absolute, but also here complexity arise. What impact 
categories are investigated (global warming potential, resource scarcity, acidification, ozone 
depletion, etc.), how likely is the alternative scenario, and what about social and economic 
sustainability? Where to draw the line? 
 
To tackle some of the complexity, this thesis mainly considers environmental sustainability, 
focusing on resource and energy consumption during use, handling of product, and how 
that may lead to more or less production of goods. As the focal point is consumer behaviour, 
the qualification of a behaviour’s sustainability will mostly be evaluated based on whether 
the consumer can influence the behaviour's sustainability, such as turning off the switch 
rather than selecting the types of components used in a product. Comparisons are made 
towards the intended scenarios assuming that the PSS are created with sustainable 
intentions. Often these correspond with what happens in the same use-context yet with a 
non-PSS product. Based on this, a definition is formulated as:  
 
A behaviour is unsustainable if a certain conscious or unconscious action or decision made by the user leads 
to less sustainable performance of the PSS compared to best practice examples seen in the system. 
 
Thereby our definition proposes an internal reference system or a PSS optimization scope 
(Kjaer et al., 2018). Kjaer et al. (2018) assess how to measure the environmental impact of 
PSS and develop a set of guidelines for life cycle assessments (LCA) to reduce the risk of 
biases in sustainability evaluations due to, e.g. rebound effects. Others have looked into how 
to measure the impacts of behavioural effects (Polizzi di Sorrentino et al., 2016; Takase et 
al., 2005). Common for all is that they propose a comparative comprehensive quantitative 
approach. Therefore, our definition is comparative by design, though with a more 
straightforward approach looking at behaviours relative to scenarios, where the user uses 
the PSS more sustainably, and most often as intended by the provider. The evaluation of 
behaviours is thereby not quantitative, as it merely aims at identifying whether a behaviour 
is desirable or not. 
 
4.3.2. Identification of unsustainable behaviours 
within PSS 
The selection of PSS case companies exemplifying the unsustainable behaviours is shown 
in Table 2, with a description of their offering as some companies have several types of 
products and services. Some companies are included to exemplify a service and have not 
==PAGE:37==
 
30 
 
 
necessarily experienced any unsustainable behaviours. These are marked “Example only”. 
Additionally, although a behaviour is happening in one company, they may very well occur 
in other similar offerings.

Car-sharing services are offered around many cities of the world to supply flexible 
mobility. Many of such services offer users to pre-book cars, sometimes for a fee 
often without customers having to consider fuel costs, parking fees, maintenance 
etc. Additionally, many car-sharing services include insurance for the users, in 
order for it to be as carefree for the users as possible.

other users need it. The requirement to refuel when low.  
 
Stroller leasing  
• 
Bugaboo 
Bugaboo is a high-end stroller company offering leasing of strollers for six months 
to three years for a monthly fee and an initial deposit of €199 in a pilot project. 
Customers could swap strollers once a year and accessories for their strollers 
anytime for a fee delivered directly to the customers. 
 
Bike-sharing 
• 
Mobike 
Mobike is a sharing service for dockless bicycles, offering a bicycle anywhere in 
the city at 69p/30 min in the UK and an initial £1 deposit. Users are given a top-
up account with a pay-per-minute model and can take and drop the bicycle 
anywhere. 
  
Electric scooters 
• 
Voi 
Voi offers renting of electronic scooters in cities around Northern Europe. Users 
can pick up and park the scooters anywhere and pay for their rides for 1.5 
DKK/min through the app with an unlocking fee of 10 DKK.

Taxi service 
• 
Uber 
 
 
Uber is a taxi-service-platform offering user trips from A to B with a pre-

provides an estimated arrival time and ratings of the chauffeur. 
 
All the identified unsustainable behaviours are described in Table 3. The behaviours are 
classified according to PSS category from the description of PSS offering. Furthermore, a 
description of why they are unsustainable based on the definition is evaluated compared to 
alternative scenarios and by looking for environmental, social, and economic sustainability.  
 
In total, 19 unsustainable behaviours were identified. Three overlap slightly as they 
all concern the choice of a less sustainable means of transport. 11 unsustainable behaviours 
occur in PSS offering transportation means.  However, the majority of PSS’s selected for 
analysis (Table 2) are offering mobility which can explain this finding. 
 
Looking at the identified behaviours in relation to PSS category, it becomes apparent that 
a considerable amount of unsustainable behaviours occur in use-oriented PSS with 16 
out of 19 identified behaviours, while two occur in product-oriented and one in result-
oriented. This is also highly impacted by the amount of PSS known and identified in the 
two other categories. However, in the process of identifying behaviours, it was found that 
some of the known result-oriented PSS would not be exposed to as many unsustainable 
behaviours as the users have little control or interaction with the product to behave 
unsustainably. E.g. Phillips’ pay-per-lux service providing light takes full responsibility of 
when the light is on and off, manages repairs, etc., leaving little room for the users to use it 
wrongly.  
 
The unsustainable behaviours were further generalised into four different results of the 
behaviours: 
 
• Overconsume PSS offerings: This category covers the increased consumption 
of either products or services provided in the PSS offering which essentially will lead 
to unnecessary consumption of resources 
• Treat products in PSS carelessly: Some products are treated carelessly which 
essentially leads to a decreased lifetime of the products or a higher demand for 
maintenance and repair 
• Misuse products in PSS: This category covers the behaviours that are outside 
common practice around PSS products and executed despite the fact that they are 
knowingly harmful 
• Deselect PSS offerings: This category covers behaviours that is a result of some 
condition in the PSS that makes some users cancel or stop using the PSS again

The result of the behaviours show that eight behaviours relate to overconsuming the PSS 
offering, seven treating the products in PSS carelessly, three misuse of products in PSS, and 
one deselecting the PSS offering. Deselection of PSS offering includes unsustainable 
behaviours caused by using the PSS and not the initial purchase decision. The behaviour 
users expect new looking and aesthetic products result in deselection of PSS offering. This behaviour 
can result from either careless, misuse or overconsumption of PSS, yet it results in the 
provider taking preventive actions that decrease the sustainability.  
 
4.3.3. Classification of unsustainable behaviours 
within PSS 
As previously described, the unsustainable behaviours were classified based on an 
exhaustive brainstorm for reasons giving rise to the behaviour. Thus, the description of 
reasons for behaviour across the four classifications is formulated as reasons that might 
occur. The actual reasons will differ depending on the user. Some of the reasons have later 
been confirmed in literature and reviews (Eckhardt, 2012; Appendix IV: Customer 
reviews). An overview of the full table can be found in Appendix VI: 5 whys analysis of 
unsustainable behaviours. 
 
4.3.3.1. When do unsustainable behaviours occur? 
The behaviours were classified according to when in the use phase the behaviour occurs as 
illustrated in Figure 9. The results show that six behaviours occurred in the Pre phase, four 
behaviours in the During phase, and ten behaviours in Post phase. The results show an 
indication of an even distribution of when the behaviour occurs.  
 
Figure 9 Unsustainable behaviours placed in User Activity Cycle showing that six behaviours occur in the pre phase, four in the during phase, 
and ten in the post phase

to cover situations where providers have the main responsibility with some contractual 
exceptions in use-oriented PSS.
