The identified reasons for unsustainable behaviours led to several findings. For one, it 
became clear that there could easily be a mismatch between what the users expected and 
what the provider actually intended to offer. Seven reasons across 11 behaviours 
indicated a mismatch in expectations as a result of communication-related issues 
and how users intuitively perceived PSS offering (marked in beige). However, 14 reasons 
across 15 unsustainable behaviours could not be linked to misalignment in expectations, 
and despite miscommunication being an influential factor, other more fundamental reasons 
may be contributing to how the PSS is used. The analysis indicates that 
unsustainable behaviours occur due to the nature of the PSS offering's 
strategic characteristics with most variations in use-oriented PSS giving rise to the 
behaviours. 
 
Numerous whys were found for each unsustainable behaviour. However, not all whys could 
be connected to the strategic characteristics showing that reasons for unsustainable 
behaviours also lie outside the scope of PSS. Also, for some behaviours, none of the whys 
was connected, implying that the reasons for the unsustainable behaviour could not be 
found in the construct of the PSS. These included “Xerox: Customers print without 
concern of consumption” and “Mobike: Dockless bikes were thrown into canals”. Despite 
that behaviours do not seem to originate from the construct of PSS, there is still a potential 
for these to be solved by a future behaviour change tool, why they are kept within the 
analysis.  
 
4.3.3.3. Classifying ownership dimension 
The analysis of each of the whys and their connection to ownership dimensions are 
summarised in Table 5, which shows reasons for unsustainable behaviour across the 
different PSS categories, where five out of the six ownership dimensions give rise 
to 18 unsustainable behaviours. Only the ownership dimension user participation could 
not be connected to any reasons for behaviour.

The strategic characteristic transfer of ownership is only related to whether there is an actual 
transfer of ownership. Instead, the psychological ownership looks into the perception of the 
ownership. Findings from the analysis suggest that when users do not feel psychological 
ownership it causes unsustainable behaviours if these are more convenient. Again, it becomes 
apparent that there can be a mismatch with the perception of the ownership compared to 
the actual characteristic of the PSS. E.g. if users do not feel ownership over a product, and 
thereby park recklessly although they are responsible for that action. There are some 
differences in the ownership perception depending on the PSS category as the ownership 
relation in product- and use-oriented PSS are different. The product-oriented PSS can lead 
to unsustainable behaviours because the users feel it is within their right to treat the product 
==PAGE:47==
 
 
 
40 
 
 
 
as they want. In the use-oriented PSS the ownership perception is more related to the 
consideration and feeling of responsibility towards others. 
 
Anonymity and temporality are widely represented in this analysis with several variations of the 
two, especially evident for the use-oriented PSS. Demyttenaere et al. (2016) describe several 
dimension within each, yet they are compiled as the two for simplicity. Temporality only has 
use-oriented linkages with contributing dimensions including: when products frequently 
shift between users, a short period of ownership, or if only used once, as users will not feel 
responsible for the product nor connected to it. Also, having the PSS in possession for long 
or using the product frequently can create habits or make users forget that others will use it 
afterwards, which leads to unsustainable behaviours. A long ownership can lead to an 
increased desire to renew the product. In product-oriented PSS, anonymity can create 
unsustainable behaviours if the behaviour is generally accepted. The bad norms are handed 
on from user to user such as printing without needing it. In use-oriented PSS, social 
norms can be somewhat blurred, making users switch off their rationality if 
not being confronted by other users, sharing the PSS in such a large group, lack of 
connection, or even using the product in safe environments. This can make users feel 
somehow detached from the product and lead to unsustainable behaviours. 
 
Responsibility life cycle and life cycle cost are partly represented in the strategic characteristics. 
What differentiates responsibility in this analysis compared to the strategic characteristic 
responsibility during use is that it addresses the feeling of responsibility and the economic 
responsibilities compared to actual obligation. Here, it shows that all three PSS categories 
can lead to unsustainable behaviours due to lack of responsibility for overconsumption and 
treating products carelessly. 
 
Overall the analysis concludes that the perception of ownership and dimensions 
influencing that perception can explain why unsustainable behaviours occur.  
 
4.3.3.4. Classifying system characteristics 
The analysis of system characteristics shows that only use-oriented PSS have connections 
between system characteristics and the reasons for unsustainable behaviour (Table 6). The 
analysis solely found connections to three behaviours, and thus, it cannot be ruled out 
that system characteristics provide a reason for unsustainable behaviours. 
The analysis indicates that if a PSS only fulfils a basic need in relation to Maslow’s 
Hierarchy of Needs (1943), it can lead to unsustainable behaviours as users will feel less 
attached to the product. Additionally, when the PSS offerings add special meaning to the 
PSS, it can result in overconsumption as it is the case for swapping and buying accessories 
for Bugaboo strollers.

4.3.3.5. Classifying institutional framework 
The analysis showed that institutions might have influenced nine out of 21 unsustainable 
behaviours. As displayed in Table 7, six behaviours are influenced by the cognitive 
institutions followed by normative with three, and regulatory with one reasons for the 
behaviour. 14 behaviours were connected to a lack of institutions prescribing how 
to behave the desired way. Furthermore, mostly use-oriented PSS seemed to have reasons 
connected to the institutions (excluding lack of institution).

The descriptions in Table 7 show that the behaviours result from existing institutions from 
traditional consumption practices (marked in beige) and attempts to create new institutions 
that either purposely or accidentally influence users. Behaviours found in DriveNow and 
Bugaboo are influenced by an existing normative institution that especially exists in the Western 
world, where consumer culture has created a need for new-looking products - both in terms 
of a need for products to look unused and a need for new products to obtain social status. 
The behaviour is grounded in a social obligation and strengthen the feeling of honour (or 
==PAGE:49==
 
 
 
42 
 
 
 
avoid the feeling of shame) when showcasing products in a social context. Bugaboo even 
intentionally exploits this need to make the offering attractive enough to increase adoption 
of the PSS by including these additional benefits of providing access to swapping strollers 
and accessories. Car-sharing and communal laundry services also increase user experience 
by offering additional benefits such as reservation services. However, this can lead to users 
making unnecessary reservations as they cannot conceive other ways of conveniently 
gaining access to the product influenced by the cognitive institution. Thus, the strategic 
characteristic, expansion of benefits, seems to be connected to the attempt of reducing adoption 
barriers or increasing user experience by PSS providers.  
 
A new cognitive institution is also found to influence behaviour seen in the mobility services 
such as DriveNow, Green Mobility, Zipcar, Voi and Uber, where users increase their use 
of the services by replacing more sustainable means of transportation. Taking an Uber can 
be a taken-for-granted option, where users simply cannot conceive other ways of 
conveniently getting to the airport. 
 
Regulatory institutions can also be used to achieve stability in the system. To avoid that users 
damage the car or otherwise will be held accountable, DriveNow requires them to check 
and report damages on the car. However, this may result in users deselecting the PSS 
offering in fear of being held responsible for having missed damages made by others. 
Furthermore, offering convenience while requiring time-consuming inspections may 
conflict and contribute to the deselection. 
 
For many behaviours, the lack of institutions prescribing the desired behaviour may provide 
the reason for the unsustainable behaviour. In Zipcar, it is prohibited to smoke in the cars, 
but still, some users do it as they do not feel responsible. Similarly, other car-sharing services 
such as DriveNow and GreenMobility experience users leaving trash in the cars and Voi 
experience that users park their e-scooters recklessly. This is despite rules condemning the 
behaviour, which should create a regulatory institution. Nevertheless, if the user does not know, 
believe or fear they will be punished, the attempts to create the institutions fail. Same applies 
for overconsuming or deselecting PSS offerings, which might occur due to the lack of 
institutions. The common laundry provider, Saniva Facility, have implemented a physical 
restriction that machines can only be reserved once a day, as they previously experienced 
that users will reserve unnecessarily and leave machines unavailable for others. This is an 
example of a PSS provider strengthening the regulatory institution to avoid a particular 
behaviour. Findings suggest that the certain practices within the PSS are not 
institutionalised, however,  the general use of some PSS have been institutionalised as 
seen with mobility offerings. 
 
4.3.4. Key insights  
In Table 8, consolidations of the five analyses are gathered describing each identified 
unsustainable behaviour according to what (unsustainable behaviour, the result of the 
behaviour), when (user activity cycle), how (strategic characteristic), and why (ownership 
dimension, system characteristic, institutional framework) (Table 8). It displays the key 
insights into the understanding of unsustainable behaviours in PSS and allows relational 
assessment of what behaviour towards all the previously analysed parameters.  
 
Firstly, looking at when shows that deselect PSS offering only occurs before use, evidently as 
the deselection of an offering based on a bad experience happens before initiating a new 
==PAGE:50==
 
 
 
43 
 
use cycle. Overconsumption of PSS occurs during the entire use cycle, as it both relates to the 
reservation of PSS, using the PSS more than needed and swapping the product to a new 
model. Treating product carelessly and misuse of the product in PSS occurs both during and post 
use, evidently not before use as these behaviours require an interaction with the products. 
This analysis shows a pattern of when specific types of unsustainable behaviours can occur. 
Despite the limited data size, this distribution is considered conclusive in relation to the result 
of the unsustainable behaviour as their presence and absence in each phase can be logically 
explained. As supported by previous analysis, looking across the unsustainable behaviours, 
they are considered evenly distributed across the three phases, although most occur in post 
phase. 
 
Looking towards how the behaviour happens it becomes apparent that the strategic 
characteristics that dominantly lead to unsustainable behaviours are availability of offering, 
economic value model, and responsibility during use whilst management of life cycle activities and benefit 
orientation  are not found to cause unsustainable behaviour. Careless and misuse behaviours do 
not seem to be influenced by the expansion of benefits nor level of digitalisation, meaning 
expanding offerings in PSS to go beyond the core activity is not a reason to treat a PSS 
wrongly. Neither would the level of digitalisation. Availability of offering is the most influencing 
characteristic giving rise to overconsumption, which is an expected finding. Expansion of benefits 
is only linked to overconsumption behaviours, and the functionalities they refer to are all 
interpreted as implemented to increase the customer base. The strategic characteristic, 
digitalisation, is embedded into other characteristics such as availability and expansion of benefits 
as digitalisation is a mean to provide those offerings much more conveniently. This explains 
why digitalisation is mainly linked to overconsumption as it can be seen as an enabler to increase 
consumption. On general terms, it is concluded that across the four results of unsustainable 
behaviour, certain strategic characteristic will more likely give rise to unsustainable 
behaviour, but generally, none can be pinpointed as most seem to affect the behaviours.  
 
Inspecting why unsustainable behaviours occur, it becomes apparent that few are 
influenced by system characteristics. Functionality parameters only affect behaviours in misuse 
and careless behaviours. This reflects that when the PSS offers a low functionality according 
to Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs (1943) users are more reluctant to treat the PSS wrongly. 
Special meaning is only connected to one unustainable behaviour, and its influence on 
unsustainable behaviours is therefore considered inconclusive. 
 
Looking further into why focusing on ownership dimensions, especially psychological ownership, 
temporality, anonymity and responsibility life cycle dominate the influence on unsustainable 
behaviours, whereas the dimension user participation did not influence unsustainable 
behaviours. Apart from two instances (temporality in deselect, and responsibility life cycle cost in 
misuse) all dimensions cause unsustainable behaviours across every result of behaviour. 
Careless and deselect behaviours are most influenced by ownership dimensions. Both 
responsibility and psychological ownership are present, indicating that lack of attachment 
can lead to both deselection and careless treatment of products. For behaviours related to 
overconsumption, responsibility also has influence, which here is explained by the lowered 
responsibility making it so favourable for users that they will overconsume it. Temporality is one 
dimension where both long and short periods of possession can lead to unsustainable 
behaviours. Anonymity is mainly explained by users not being confronted by a rationally 
inappropriate behaviour within the norms. This finding corresponds well with the 
overweight of behaviours being potentially being explained by the lack of institutions. 
 
==PAGE:51==
 
 
 
44 
 
 
 
Mostly misuse and careless behaviours in PSS occur due to the lack of institutions, while 
overconsumption of PSS mostly occurs due to institutions. However, this finding is quite 
evident and synergises directly with the responsibility life cycle dimension. Institutionalisation 
is seeking stability, and when the system is stable, it might become so favourable for users 
that they overconsume the offering. As Scott (2008) writes regarding the regulatory, normative, 
and cognitive institutions “[…] each is properly seen as providing or contributing to an institutionalized 
social order: all support and sustain stable behavior.” Behaviours not connected to institutions can 
be described as actions by users that destabilise the system. Interestingly, seemingly stable 
systems can create unsustainable behaviours embodied in overconsumption. However, for 
some providers such as car-sharing services and e-scooters services, this behaviour will be 
desired as it also results in increased revenue, while for others such as Bugaboo, it will 
increase the cost of the offering.  
 
The behaviours that show to be mostly influenced by the parameters sought for, are for 
careless behaviours:  damage car, leave trash in car, use strollers inexpediently and parking 
e-scooters recklessly. Additionally, the deselect behaviour of high expectations to the offering, 
and smoking in cars being a misuse behaviour are all affected by nine to ten parameters. 
Overconsume behaviours are maximum affected by seven parameters, yet as some categories 
overlap the four types of result of behaviour are considered to be highly affected by PSS-
related parameters.  
 
Lastly, the 16 unsustainable behaviours are collected and described in a generalised form 
behaviour by behaviour to view them from a different perspective. This is not found to be 
further incorporated in the later works of the thesis. Nevertheless, they are referred here to 
potentially enable further research of the behaviours or a redesign of the proposed method 
(Appendix VIII: Analysed unsustainable behaviours).

4.4. 
Discussion 
By identifying unsustainable behaviours and exploring reasons exhaustively, it was shown 
that depending on the PSS category and result of behaviour, certain PSS-related 
parameters could give rise to the behaviours, especially in use-oriented PSS. The significant 
PSS-related parameters are, amongst others, the responsibility distribution between user 
and provider, user’s perception of what to expect, availability of offering, temporality of 
user’s “ownership”, anonymity of user during “ownership”, existing institutions from 
traditional consumption practices and the lack institutions supporting sustainable 
behaviour.  
 
It was also found that some PSS successfully create institutions as it was the case for Saniva 
Facility. This finding is supported by Zvolska et al. (2019) who state that “many [urban sharing 
organisations] engage in purposeful institutional work with a clear goal to disrupt or create institutions. On 
the other hand, their institutional work also takes place as a result of less purposeful actions, which are more 
of a side effect or an unintended consequence of their practices” and thus, stating the providers' ability 
in having a central role in avoiding the unsustainable behaviours. Findings suggest that the 
availability of offering could be a causal reason for the overconsumption of PSS, which is 
connected to a disruption of existing institutions. Tunn (2020) finds that certain strategies 
can be used to overcome adoption barriers for access-based PSS, mentioning that if users 
have high digital confidence, it will ease PSS adoption, especially in short-term PSS 
offerings. Interestingly, our findings suggest that level of digitalisation give rise to 
overconsumption of the PSS offering, indicating that strategies to create institutions (and 
ease adoption) can lead to unsustainable behaviour, however, this is yet to be investigated  
 
Investigating the strategic characteristics indicated seven reasons for behaviour that were 
influenced by a mismatch in the user's expectations, stemming from the users' intuitive 
perception of the PSS. This suggests that investigating the use of affordances (Norman, 
2013) could be used to overcome these behaviours. However, PSS expert Marina Peroni 
also mentioned that users’ expectations and perceptions of the PSS's value are among the 
main entry barriers for consumers, suggesting a slightly different issue. Perhaps even a 
different solution. Interestingly, a literature review of barriers to consumer adoption of 
access-based PSS by Tunn (2020) identified “Perception of high price or lack of financial advantage” 
and “Concerns regarding low quality product either because of low end brands or because of increased 
utilisation” as barriers. These are also identified as reasons for the identified unsustainable 
behaviours, suggesting a connection between why consumers do not adopt PSS and reasons 
for performing an unsustainable behaviour. An investigation of rental models for high-end 
fashion brands carried out by Continued Fashion also found adoption barriers. However, 
their studies indicate that users are afraid to be held responsible for damages they may 
impose upon the garments - a regulatory institution keeping these business models' growth 
from accelerating to its potential.  
 
Our findings do not say that all these parameters always lead to unsustainable behaviours 
as they are most often implemented with good intentions. Convenience has shown to be a 
dominant determinator. It is especially relevant for PSS as it is also very often a key selling 
point for converting to a PSS solution rather than conventional consumption practices. This 
finding has also been highlighted by an industry specialist. Pia Huusfelt, Global Business 
Leader for IKEA, have spent two years making exhaustive interviews focusing on the good 
life across different consumer markets. In an interview, we discussed how to create 
sustainable behaviour. She explains: “What drives consumer behaviour is first and foremost 
==PAGE:54==
 
 
 
47 
 
convenience. If the product is also sustainable, it will be an additional good. This will change in the future.”  
This indicates that not only can convenience be a driver for unsustainable behaviour, but 
to this day also a driver to choose a PSS in the first place. However, a shift in priorities may 
be on their way. 
 
4.4.1. Sharing is caring – or is it? 
But do the identified unsustainable behaviours mean that use-oriented PSS is not 
sustainable? No. However, it does mean that use-oriented PSS does not necessarily create 
sustainable consumption – but some do. Co-founder of Vigga, Peter Svensson, talks about 
their experiences with user behaviour in their previous business, now known as Circos. The 
word exemplary is used to describe the user culture. Vigga rent out baby clothes for a 
monthly payment, ensuring that the clothes fit as the baby grows and clothes that no longer 
fit is circulated to another. Going to the market, they expected a 15% waste margin. 
Surprisingly, the actual number was closer to 1%. He further elaborates that neither the 
quality nor user’s perception of items caused any problems. This suggests that not all use-
oriented PSS experience unsustainable behaviour, and maybe that something can be 
learned from how other practitioners have designed PSS. So instead of asking whether 
sharing is caring, the question instead becomes: How can sharing become caring? Peter 
Svensson point towards community culture: “Can only guess, but maybe it's all about culture - 
understanding this is of great importance. How you meet the world and how it meets you. Most meaningful 
relationships are not broken when beneficial to each other.”  The use of community can create both 
a shared understanding between users and a sense of obligation towards others in PSS 
(Briceno & Stagl, 2006). Vigga has also used their community to gain traction by 
encouraging using its hashtag on social media. However, Vigga is also supported by an 
existing sharing culture and hand-me-downs of baby clothes between family and friends. 
Thus, building communities may not help to avoid unsustainable behaviour for all PSS. 
Studies made by Pia Huusfelt lead her to conclude: “You can use sub-culture to change behaviour 
by having others lead the way. But most consumers will not be affected by what others think of them regarding 
sustainable products. That is why sustainable solutions must be attractive not just for first movers.”  Further 
exploration of using communities to avoid unsustainable behaviour should be conducted. 
 
In addition to culture community, Peter Svensson also says that “to be in service can be 
restrictive but generally rules and ease of part taking is not a problem if done right - and taking care 
that products meet the customer expectations is key”. Another parallel can be drawn to one 
of the findings as our analysis of strategic characteristics found that sometimes 
unsustainable behaviour can be connected to a misalignment of expectations to the 
PSS.  
 
These best practice examples give slight indications that sustainable behaviours can be 
created through the establishment of institutions, for instance, by increasing community 
feeling. This, however, does not necessarily make users want to buy into PSS. As the 
expansion of benefits also leads to unsustainable behaviours, community building and 
expectations alignment may not be the only way. Instead, behaviours related to the 
expansion of benefits could possibly be solved by avoiding to create an unnecessary need 
and focusing on benefits that revolve around the core function of the PSS. The feeling of 
ownership has also shown to be a significant motive for unsustainable behaviours, why it 
should be investigated whether increasing PSS attachment could solve these.  
 
==PAGE:55==
 
 
 
48 
 
 
 
We have seen in relation to the problem that it is not exclusively grounded in institutions 
or lack thereof. There are also general issues in the construct of PSS and the change in 
ownership and perception hereof. But how do we solve it? It is important to point out that 
the problem space does not necessarily correspond to the solution space, meaning that there 
may even be solutions to these problems that lie far outside the sphere of PSS design. Also, 
the study of unsustainable behaviours and their whys has led to reasons for the behaviours 
spanning beyond the PSS domain.  However, this project has a presumption that DfSB can 
solve the problems that lie in PSS, and perhaps also those beyond. Whether DfSB is 
applicable and how it will be carried out is to be discussed in the following chapters. 
 
4.5. 
Limitations 
4.5.1. Some behaviours are driven by culture 
The analysis of unsustainable behaviours has its limitations. Not only by data size as 
previously discussed but also the geographical representativeness. Data is primarily 
collected for Danish, American and British users. All of which is placed within the Western 
part of the world. As much behaviour, especially behaviour being affected by others, is 
highly influenced by cultural background and surroundings, this is a clear limitation. Both 
in terms of the comparability between some of the behaviours, and whether all of them are 
representative for all data points in the population. With Western world domination in the 
data, some norms will likely be the same – but not all. In an interview, a representative from 
Nextbike (a German competitor to the Chinese Mobike) said: “Sadly, the UK compared to the 
rest of mainland Europe has a lot less respect for shared resources. Our parent company in Germany honestly 
find it hard to get their head around the generic levels of vandalism and theft that occur in the UK” (Pidd, 
2018), thus, suggesting that the unsustainable behaviour of vandalism can be connected to 
norms and that these are different in the UK than other parts of Europe. This is supported 
by the study of IKEA, which concluded that behaviour is highly dependent on culture. 
Specifically, they found that maintenance performed by users is seen at a much greater 
extent in countries were borrowing or renting items from each other is very common. This 
is amongst others seen in countries such as Poland and other Eastern European countries 
where “you do not disappoint others” phrased by Pia Huusfelt. “The Northern countries are highly 
consuming societies and differ from this borrowing culture quite extensively.” Therefore, the 
unsustainable behaviours identified through this study will not be universal.  
 
This also means that the analysis of why users conduct unsustainable behaviours has its 
limitations, especially as many unsustainable behaviours could be related to the institutional 
framework, which is built upon culture. Although it was attempted to identify as many 
different reasons for the behaviour, the results were limited by our own experiences and 
knowledge as these form our comprehension of a given situation – or behaviour in this case. 
The scientific approach would be to base our findings upon user studies. However, this 
would also have limitations as users may very well not know why they do a certain 
behaviour, or even have biases of their own influencing their understanding of why. Due to 
these limitations, our analyses cannot credibly be used to prove that PSS can create 
unsustainable behaviours across all cultures. There are, however, strong indications that 
PSS create unsustainable behaviours within Western cultures.  
 
4.5.2. Are mobility PSS the reason for unsustainable 
behaviours? 
==PAGE:56==
 
 
 
49 
 
Apart from cultural differences, the data was also limited to specific types of PSS – also 
regarding the product offering. Many of the unsustainable behaviours identified were from 
access-based transportation means. However, user behaviour towards specific products will 
perhaps vary as one might be more prone to treat a bike with less care than a laptop – 
regardless of whether it is in a PSS or not. Tunn (2020) makes a similar argument in relation 
to product care in access-based PSS that users will take less care of products that mainly 
serve a functional purpose due to less attachment. This indicates that the system 
characteristic, functionality, may be an important parameter although our findings were 
inconclusive. 
 
As previously discussed, unsustainable behaviour does not occur in all PSS, e.g. Vigga (now 
called Circos), who offers access to baby clothes. Therefore, the limited variation in product 
type may decrease the relevance of our results to other types of PSS. However, this could 
simply be a consequence of the successful construction of a PSS in which the mobility PSS 
can draw learnings. Since the investigation has also found unsustainable behaviours in 
electronics and home appliances, it could also be based on the fact that the majority of 
established PSS are related to mobility. The relevance to other PSS product types is yet to 
be tested. 
 
4.6. 
Summary 
To enable the identification of unsustainable behaviours, a definition of such was adapted 
through literature search: A behaviour is unsustainable if a certain conscious or unconscious action or 
decision made by the user leads to less sustainable performance of the PSS compared to best practice examples 
seen in the system. Based on this definition, 19 unsustainable behaviours distributed across 
eight different types of PSS offerings were identified. 16 unsustainable behaviours were 
found in use-oriented PSS, while two occur in product-oriented and one in result-oriented 
PSS. The 19 behaviours were clustered into four different results of unsustainable 
behaviours through an inductive approach being: overconsume PSS offering, deselect PSS offering, 
misuse products in PSS and treat products in the PSS carelessly. The 19 behaviours were later 
analysed and classified based on when in the use cycle they occur, how according to 
strategic characteristics, and why according to ownership dimensions, system 
characteristics and the institutional framework. Lastly, all behaviours and their connections 
to all the PSS-related parameters analysed for are gathered in an overview to display areas 
that are more dominating for unsustainable behaviours in PSS including: use-oriented 
PSS compared to result-, and product-oriented; an even distribution is considered 
between the pre, during, and post phase; strategic characteristics in particular 
availability of offering, economic value model and responsibility during use; 
ownership 
dimensions 
especially 
psychological 
ownership, 
temporality, 
anonymity and responsibility life cycle; and in the institutional framework mostly 
cognitive and lack of institutions influence unsustainable behaviours. Across the four 
proposed results of behaviours all showed to include behaviours that were highly affected 
by the parameters. Overconsume behaviours are mostly influenced by availability of offering and 
cognitive institutions, while careless behaviours showed to be mostly influenced by economic value 
model, anonymity and responsibility. As only one deselect and three misuse behaviours were 
discovered the most influential parameters for these behaviours were inconclusive. Overall, 
there are not one parameter, use cycle phase, nor strategic characteristic that gives reason 
to unsustainable behaviours. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:57==
5. Conceptualise 
a method
==PAGE:58==
 
 
 
51 
 
5.1. 
Introduction 
This chapter seeks to investigate how to avoid unsustainable behaviours in PSS through 
design. It takes on an MVP (minimum viable product) approach to investigate if existing 
DfSB methods, tools and design processes can be used to solve unsustainable behaviour in 
PSS. This includes identifying the crucial steps in a DfSB design process followed by setting 
criteria for a suitable MVP method. Existing methods and tools are sought for, and the 
most promising ones are consolidated into an MVP method. This method is tested with a 
group of students to evaluate the suitability for solving unsustainable behaviours in PSS 
with the current contributions. Lastly, opportunities for improvement are identified. 
 
5.2. 
Methodology 
The aim is to identify and test a suitable MVP method for designing out unsustainable 
behaviours in PSS. It seeks to answer the research questions: RQ2. How to support 
designers in addressing unsustainable behaviours and how to integrate it in 
the PSS framework? And RQ3. How can behaviour intervention strategies 
mitigate the identified unsustainable behaviours in PSS and achieve lasting 
sustainable change? These are sought answered through a structured three-step 
approach. First, DfSB design processes are identified through literature, to identify when 
designers are most in need of support and key steps are chosen as a focus for the MVP 
method. To determine what designers need, specifications for the selection of the DfSB 
methods and tools were developed based on barriers and success factors from Appendix I 
Literature review. Second, specifications were utilised in a systematic selection of methods 
and tools found in Appendix IX: DfSB methods and tools databaseand consolidated into a 
method. Third, the conceptualised MVP method is tested and evaluated through a 
workshop with students, and opportunities for improvement are identified. 
 
5.2.1. Understanding what needs to be developed 
The literature study carried out prior to this thesis (Appendix I) resulted in a database of 
methods, tools, processes, etc. within the field of DfSB (Appendix VII). The database, 
containing 45 methods was scrutinised for reference models, design processes and other 
methods describing a DfSB design process, to understand the crucial steps that have to be 
carried out to design successful sustainable behaviour change. An online search 
supplemented this to accommodate if some processes had fallen outside the literature 
study's scope. The processes were arranged according to the purpose of the steps so that 
they could be compared. The identified steps were consolidated into a design process 
containing necessary steps. Finally, the steps most unique for DfSB were identified to scope 
the process steps that would be part of the conceptualised MVP method, determining when 
designers are most in need of support. Boks (2012) points out that selecting target behaviour is 
one of the biggest challenges of DfSB research, which also informed the selection of steps. 
 
To define what designers need support with, a set of requirements and criteria were 
composed to solve the unsustainable behaviours through design methods. The literature 
study had, apart from methods, sought for success factors and barriers for DfSB methods, 
which served as a basis for developing the criteria and requirements for the selection of 
tools. A brainstorm supplemented this. The specifications were sorted into requirements 
and criteria, which were categorised to create an overview of the different aspects sought 
included. 
 
==PAGE:59==
 
 
 
52 
 
 
 
5.2.2. Developing the MVP method 
5.2.2.1. Selecting methods and tools 
Through screening of the database (Appendix VII), a rough sorting was conducted. Here, 
the defined requirements were used. Thus, only DfSB methods and tools targeting the 
selected steps in the design process made it through the cut. This required the methods and 
tools to be classified according to which steps in the DfSB process they targeted to sort out 
the methods that did not target the steps in focus. 
 
Afterwards, the weighted objectives method (WOM) (Cross, 2000) was used. Firstly, the 
criteria were evaluated comparatively, giving a ranking and a weight for each criterion. All 
methods and tools that met the requirements were evaluated on each criterion on a scale 
from one to five, with five being the highest score indicating the best attainment of a 
criterion. This enabled selecting the methods with the highest score within each step of the 
process.  
 
5.2.2.2. Consolidating and tweaking the MVP method 
After selecting the highest rated methods and tools for each step, some steps required a 
more in-depth comparative evaluation of the methods and tools, which was carried out by 
identifying pros and cons. This enabled both the selection and points for improvements to 
be incorporated prior to testing the MVP method on a PSS case study. Next, the methods 
and tools were either selected or consolidated in each of the key steps within the design 
process with minor adjustments to adapt them to suit a PSS context. However, adjustments 
were delicate to test the tools in an MVP format. 
 
5.2.3. Testing and evaluating the MVP method 
To test the method, a new set of requirements and criteria was developed to evaluate each 
tool's performance and the consolidated MVP method's overall performance. The criteria 
were based on Daae (2014), who has conducted an extensive literature study on important 
measures for design methods. This was supplemented by key parameters important to the 
PSS context. For each of the performance requirements and criteria, a measurable and 
desired performance target was set together with a description of how to measure it.  
 
All the tools were gathered together on the online collaborative platform Miro to test it with 
students currently enrolled in the course Development and operation of product/service-systems 
offered at the Technical University of Denmark as part of the engineering design master 
programme. The test target group was selected due to their knowledge on PSS development 
and possession of a PSS concept. This year (fall 2020), the teams developed PSS solutions 
to solve societal challenges. However, only one student signed up from this year’s course, 
accompanied by a student from last year (fall 2019). The project from this year’s student 
served as a case for the workshop, which sought to facilitate a take-back system for fast 
fashion brands in a B2B context, but with B2C touchpoints. The participant and designer 
of the PSS describe the concept: “Cloop a unified clothing take-back system where we handle the take-
back process of clothing and facilitate responsible recycling of clothing if our partners do not have established 
recycling partners/infrastructure.” 
 
The aim was to test the applicability of both the individual tools and overall MVP method 
in a PSS context and identify if the distinctive types of unsustainable behaviour could be 
solved by using the MVP method. The applicability and usability was evaluated through 
==PAGE:60==
 
 
 
53 
 
participant self-evaluation questionnaire, alongside observations from the workshop and an 
analysis of the participant's outputs from the workshop. 
 
5.3. 
Results 
5.3.1. Understanding what needs to be developed 
From a literature search, four design processes and reference models were found in DfSB 
(Table 10). The four processes were consolidated into a design process suitable for PSSS. 
The approach suggested by Selvefors et al. (2011) has six steps: choose resource consumption 
situation, user study, identify wasteful resource consumption, choose focus, identify behavioural intervention 
strategies, and idea generation. Other steps found in the table were integrated from the regular 
product development processes. The aim of the approach is to enable designers to reduce 
resource consumption in product usage by employing user-centered design approach in 
combination with behavioural intervention strategies and thus, change the resource-
intensive behaviours of users. The design process proposed by Daae (2014) is quite similar 
to that of Selvefors et al., but include another step after identifying what behaviour to 
change, which is to identify what affects that behaviour. His design process aims to support 
designers in understanding the user and the context in which the user interacts with a 
product, enabling the designers to make informed decisions about how to design products 
that make users interact with the products most sustainably. Drawing upon this work from 
Daae, Wastling et al. (2018) present a process containing four key components as seen in 
Table 10. The focus is different from the previous authors as they seek to design products 
and services to encourage circular behaviours. Lastly, a decision support diagram by De 
Medeiros et al. (2018) has been included. The diagram has been developed by assessing 
and combining approaches within DfSB into a single diagram to induce sustainable 
behaviour. 
 
Figure 10 Design process to design for sustainable behaviour in product/service-systems consisting of 11 steps in total. Step 1 to 5 highlighted 
in yellow are chosen to be the scope of the MVP method henceforth 
The four design processes and approaches were consolidated into a design process to design 
for sustainable behaviour in PSS and consist of 11 steps. Five steps are selected as the scope 
of the MVP method, as illustrated in Figure 10. The steps within scope are: 1) Identify 
unsustainable behaviours, 2) Select target unsustainable behaviour, 3) 
Identify what affects the unsustainable behaviour, 4) Select strategies to 
create desired behaviour, and 5) Develop ideas. Steps such as design brief, evaluate and 
select ideas, develop a concept and user testing require methods and tools considered known to 
designers and are thus not necessary to look further into, why these have been excluded 
==PAGE:61==
 
 
 
54 
 
 
 
from the scope. For a similar reason, the steps understand consumption practices and user study 
have been excluded, although these could potentially be relevant to look into from a PSS 
perspective in further work. Thereby the chosen steps in focus outline when designers need 
most support.  
 
Specifying what designers need, concrete challenges and enablers are investigaed. 
Through the systematic review (Appendix I), eight success factors and ten barriers had been 
identified for DfSB methods and served as indicators of what designers need. Amongst 
these, the following were deemed relevant as specifications for an MVP method. 
Success factors: 
• 
Need for user inclusion 
• 
Resource intensive 
• 
User satisfaction 
Barriers: 
• 
Time and resources 
• 
Oversimplification of users 
• 
Differences in large consumer groups 
• 
Knowledge gap 
• 
Specialist knowledge 
The requirements and criteria are gathered in Table 9. The criteria is least resource intensive 
and is most exhaustive are important to balance against each other as both criteria are 
important while being each other's opposites. The MVP method must imply a proper 
understanding of users to achieve successful behaviour change, yet at the same time, it is 
important to reach this in a resource-efficient manner. The measure for the criterion is the 
most applicable in a PSS context is formulated how many behaviour intervention strategies are used in the 
method and can seem like a rough translation. This was, however, necessary to be able to 
evaluate the given tool on such parameter. The logic is, that the more behaviour 
intervention strategies are included, the higher the possibility of targeting PSS-related 
behaviours. Additional to the criteria below, a given tool must increase the sustainable 
performance of the PSS. However, this will not be possible to evaluate merely by reading 
about the tool if authors do not themselves assess it, why it is not included in the table.

5.3.2. Developing the MVP method

As seen in Table 12, evaluating the 22 methods gave five methods that scored with a total 
above 100, in which at least one method showed to represent each of the desired steps. The 
five methods were evaluated more in-depth to determine which methods showed to be best 
for the five steps. Some steps needed further exploration to decide between them. Next is 
an overview of the five top-rated methods and the steps for which they are found relevant 
followed by a thorough description and evaluation of pros and cons.  
 
Step 1: Identify unsustainable behaviour 
• Principles of design for sustainable behaviour (Method ID 28, Appendix VII) 
• Design for sustainable consumption behaviour (Method ID 122, Appendix VII) 
 
Step 2: Select target unsustainable behaviour 
• Principles of design for sustainable (Method ID 28, Appendix VII) 
• Design for sustainable consumption behaviour (Method ID 122, Appendix VII) 
 
Step 3: Identify what affects the unsustainable behaviour 
• Behavioural heuristics (Method ID 85, Appendix VII) 
 
Step 4: Select strategies to create desired behaviour 
• Principles of design for sustainable behaviour (Method ID 28, Appendix VII) 
 
Step 5: Create ideas 
• Dimensions of behaviour change (Method ID 30, Appendix VII) 
• Behaviour model matrix (Method ID 138, Appendix VII) 
 
5.3.2.1. Step 1 and 2: Identify and select target 
unsustainable behaviours 
The goal of the first and the second step in focus is to identify unsustainable behaviours occurring 
in the PSS alongside selecting which behaviours to change. These two steps are found represented 
as closely linked in the chosen methods why they here are presented together. These steps 
can contain quite different approaches depending on whether the focus lies on redesigning 
existing or developing new PSS. A redesign allows for direct contextual investigations of 
behaviours and designing a new PSS needs a broader perspective when conducting 
contextual inquiries. As the steps in focus are scoped down, some preceding steps are 
considered prerequisites to these, especially regarding user insights. 
==PAGE:65==
 
 
 
58 
 
 
 
 
Despite both the methods selected in the WOM have a clear focus on energy reducing 
behaviours, they are considered to accommodate unsustainable behaviours that go beyond 
energy consumption.  
 
5.3.2.1.1. 
Design for sustainable consumption behaviour  
Design for sustainable consumption behaviour (DSCB) (Selvefors et al., 2011) is a design 
process containing several steps to obtain a more sustainable consumption of products. For 
step 1 and 2, Identify wasteful resource consumption and Choose focus and target behaviour are 
considered. The method prescribes to analyse critical resource-intensive behaviours where 
consumption could be reduced. This is done by asking the following questions: 
 
• Is it an active or passive consumption?  
• Is the consumption due to a habit or due to an active action based on an actual 
need?  
• What prevents the user to end the consumption? 
 
Based on the answers, the behaviours are mapped in a cycle of consumption (Figure 11). 
As this cycle of consumption has already been filled out with an expected course of action 
for the user in previous tasks, the actual behaviours are compared to these. The method 
points out that high potential savings can be achieved when discrepancies between the 
assumed and actual behaviour occur.   
 
Figure 11 Cycle of consumption adapted from Selvefors et al. ( 2011) 
Lastly, the behaviour(s) to keep in focus is chosen based upon company objectives that 
embody potential resource savings during consumption. Additionally, the designer should 
decide whether to influence a current behaviour or replace it by evoking a less resource-
intensive one.  
 
5.3.2.1.2. 
Principles of design for sustainable behaviour   
Principles of design for sustainable behaviour (Daae & Boks, 2013) contain an entire design 
process to design for creating or maintaining sustainable behaviour change. For step 1 and 
2, the step study and measure base-line practice and identify which behaviour is to be changed is 
considered. Study and measure base-line describes important user research that should be 
carried out before selecting behaviour. Daae & Boks point out, that there are numerous 
ways to obtain user insights, where the understanding of the user, the context, and what 
affects the behaviour is important to keep in mind. 
==PAGE:66==
 
 
 
59 
 
The selection of the behaviour to change should be based upon quantifying the potential 
reduction in environmental impact that could be achieved through each behaviour. 
However, it is mentioned that ranking all behaviours that have an impact on sustainability 
can be of a rather complicated character. It is pointed out that several factors should be 
taken into account, such as what behaviours designers are able to change alongside the 
situational context's complexity. Additionally, it suggests that if it is too complex to evaluate 
a quantified environmental impact of the behaviours, these could be evaluated relative to 
each other.  
 
ID 122. Design for sustainable 
consumption behaviour – Identify wasteful 
resource consumption and Choose focus and 
target behaviour 
ID 28. Principles of design for 
sustainable behaviour – Study and 
measure base-line practice and Identify which 
behaviour is to be changed  
Pros 
 
 
- 
Method targeting both the identification 
and selection of the unsustainable 
behaviour in focus 
-

- 
Includes a visual model to structure the 
identification 
 
Cons 
-

description of how to select the behaviour

most impactful behaviour

Cons 
-

one silver bullet nor clear tasks

numerous ways to identify unsustainable 
behaviours yet with no specific description 
or recommendations 
 
 
 
Step 1: Identify unsustainable behaviours 
 
Design for sustainable consumption (method ID 122) is selected as it best describes an 
approach to identifying unsustainable behaviours. Furthermore, it also includes a visual 
tool which is deemed highly relevant.  
 
The visual tool was consolidated with the user activity cycle to target the methods more 
towards a PSS development. Additionally, visual examples of some of the unsustainable 
behaviours identified in PSS were added for inspirational purposes. 
 
Step 2: Select target unsustainable behaviour 
 
Principles of design for sustainable behaviour (method ID 28) are primarily selected for this 
step. It suggests a pragmatic approach and considers concrete measures when selecting 
which behaviour to focus on. 
 
It is supplemented with some principles of Design for sustainable consumption behaviour 
(method ID 122), specifically the consideration of selecting whether to influence a 
current behaviour or replace it by evoking a less resource-intensive one. Additionally, it 
is being structured by collecting the measures in a table to comparatively evaluate each 
unsustainable behaviour. 
==PAGE:67==
 
 
 
60 
 
 
 
 
5.3.2.2. Step 3: Identify what affects the unsustainable 
behaviour  
The purpose of the third step is to identify what affects the selected behaviour. Both 
internal factors within the user and their motivations alongside external factors should be 
investigated in this step. Lastly, it is important to have a general understanding of the user 
and context to better predict the effect of implementing behaviour-changing strategies in 
later steps. 
 
5.3.2.2.1. 
Behavioural heuristics  
In the article Exploring Problem-framing through Behavioural Heuristics, Lockton et al. (2013) 
suggest a problem-framing procedure using a heuristics approach to obtain behaviour 
change. Heuristics are simple rules of thumb simplifying what happens in situations of 
interest and are identified through contextual inquiries by asking users simple “If … then 
…” statements to understand specific behavioural actions relevant for the challenge. The 
task of particular interest to discover what affects the behaviour is called 5 Whys and 
Further Abstraction. This is carried out by asking users a heuristic statement through a 5 whys 
approach of why they perform a certain action. It is stressed that it is not strictly necessary 
to reach a full series of whys. This results in a tree diagram of whys exemplified in Figure 
12. 
 
Figure 12 Example of a 5 whys chain adapted from Lockton et al. (2013) 
ID 85. Behavioural heuristics – 5 whys 
Pros 
 
 
- 
Based on user interviews building an in-
depth understanding of why the 
behaviour occurs 
- 
Useful to achieve an exhaustive result 
 
Cons 
- 
Resource-intensive to interview users of 
their whys after having decided on a 
target behaviour 
- 
Not all users may be truthfully aware of 
why they have certain behaviours 
 
Step 3: Identify what affects the unsustainable behaviour 
 
Behavioural heuristics (method ID 85) is chosen to identify what affects the behaviour 
through the 5 whys method, although it can be resource-intensive constructing the whys 
==PAGE:68==
 
 
 
61 
 
with the additional user studies. However, this is considered insignificant as it seems 
possible to utilise the tool based on existing user studies, desktop research or an 
exhaustive hypothetical approach (as already proven in 4.3.3 Classification of 
unsustainable behaviours within PSS). As pointed out, not all users are aware of why 
they behave as they do, yet this is considered to be within the designer's skills to 
overcome. 
 
5.3.2.3. Step 4: Select strategies to create the desired 
behaviour  
Step four aims to select the behaviour intervention strategies best suitable for the problem at hand 
and the target user. The selection of intervention strategies can be based on several different 
factors, while the key is to select the strategies with the highest potential for successful and 
sustained behaviour change. 
 
5.3.2.3.1. 
Principles of design for sustainable behaviour 
Numerous behaviour intervention strategies can be utilised to change behaviour. However, 
depending on why a user acts a certain way, the strategies will influence users differently. 
Thus, it is important to select strategies that will help change behaviour effectively and not 
result in users disregarding the intervention or rejecting the PSS. Daae & Boks (2013) 
developed a guideline to help select behaviour intervention strategies – or as they call it, 
principles of behaviour change. The guidelines were developed during a research project 
and was further iterated in the thesis Informing Design for Sustainable Behaviour by  Daae  
(2014), which is chosen for the consolidation of the MVP method. 
 
Generally, Daae finds three groups of principles: informing, persuading, and determining that 
contain nine principles (Table 13) and were identified through a literature review 
(Zachrisson & Boks, 2012). 
 
Table 13 Overview of behaviour intervention strategies adapted from (Daae, 2014)  
Informing 
Information 
Feedback 
Persuading 
Enabling 
Encouraging 
Guiding 
Seducing 
Steering 
Determining 
Forcing 
Automatic 
 
The principles provide either user or product with different levels of control, which is a 
dimension found to be important for distinguishing between principles together with 
==PAGE:69==
 
 
 
62 
 
 
 
obtrusiveness. Obtrusiveness describes whether intervention should be subtle or forceful in the 
amount of attention required by the user. Other dimensions identified are empathy, meaning, 
timing, frequency. However, control and obtrusiveness form the basis for categorising and 
choosing between the principles as seen in the landscape depicted in Figure 13. 
 
 
Figure 13 Landscape of control and obtrusiveness adapted from Daae & Boks (2013) 
To identify which principles to use, the designer must be able to answer a set of questions 
as seen in Figure 14. The questions should be answered based on insights from user 
research, and personas can be useful to generalise users within the target group. The 
answers will suggest suitable areas for principles, both in terms of control and obtrusiveness, but 
also show unsuitable areas, where users are likely to not accept the intervention. Unsuitable 
areas are only applicable for negative responses to the questions. The areas identified 
through the five questions are then consolidated, by placing the five marked areas given 
from the replies on top of each other in the same landscape. If the intervention should be 
designed for more than one persona, this exercise should be done for all and consolidated 
again to show the suitable and unsuitable areas. The tool to select principles can both be 
used before ideation to delimit the solution space or after to select between ideas by placing 
the ideas within the landscape.  
 
ID 28. Principles of design for sustainable behaviour – Select strategies 
Pros 
 
 
- 
Extensively tested in five iterations with 
both design students and practitioners 
- 
Quite easy and quick to use without further 
need of resources 
Cons 
- 
If used before ideation, it can limit 
creativity 
- 
Difficult to identify which principles (or 
ideas) will be most effective, and only help 
limit solution space 
- 
Only takes two dimensions into account 
 
 
==PAGE:70==
 
 
 
63 
 
 
Figure 14 Tool to select principles to achieve desired behaviour adapted from Daae (2014). The outlined boxes indicate the area with suitable 
principles based on the answer to relevant question. Boxes with dotted line indicate areas with unsuitable principles, when the answer lies within 
the red-dashed outlined boxes 
==PAGE:71==
 
 
 
64 
 
 
 
Step 4: Select strategies to create the desired behaviour 
 
Principles of design for sustainable behaviour (method ID 28) is selected to determine strategies 
to create the desired behaviour as it achieved the highest ranking. Not many 
adjustments are made to this method, yet a thorough description is prioritised due to its 
complicated procedure. 
 
5.3.2.4. Step 5: Create ideas 
The fifth step aims to develop ideas based on selected behaviour intervention strategies. 
The goal is to develop ideas for how to change an unsustainable behaviour, either by 
changing the existing behaviour or by replacing it with a new one. It is desired to create 
ideas that solve the issue with the highest certainty of behaviour change. Two methods with 
the highest rating were selected for this step. 
 
5.3.2.4.1. 
Dimensions of behaviour change 
The method is based on investigating literature to understand what can affect behaviour 
while being supplemented with newly discovered perspectives. These are all embodied as 
dimensions in a tool to create behaviours called Dimensions of behaviour change, which aims to 
help designers consider the different ways product design can affect users’ behaviour. 
Despite the tool being targeting product design, “there is every reason to believe that the tool would 
be equally suitable to support other types of behaviour changes as well” (Daae & Boks, 2014).  
 
The tool consists of 11 physical cards. The first card is a visual representation of the 
progression of using the tool. It presents 16 design principles described in relation to how 
much control user has in the given situation followed by an introductory card describing 
how to use the tool.  
 
 
 
The first card to be in actual use when utilising the tool is the control card, on which the 
decision of which design principle to work from based on the amount of control that is 
suitable for the user in the specific context is selected. Following is presented eight, two-
sided cards representing each of the eight dimensions (Table 14) displaying an axis between 
two extremes and different examples of interventions with pictures and descriptions of 
existing products. Furthermore, pros and cons for choosing interventions located in 
different places of the spectrum are shown.  
 
==PAGE:72==
 
 
 
65 
 
Table 14 Short description of the cards displaying nine dimensions 
Control 
• 
Users in control 
• 
Shared control 
• 
Product in control 
Obtrusiveness 
• 
Demand attention 
• 
Medium obtrusive 
• 
Unobtrusive 
Encouragement 
• 
Promoting 
• 
Neutral 
• 
Discouraging 
Meaning 
• 
If it is fun 
• 
If it makes sense 
Direction 
• 
If the behaviour is in line 
• 
If it is not in line and not opposing 
• 
If the behaviour is opposing 
Empathy 
• 
Principles focusing on the users 
• 
Principles focusing on others 
Importance 
• 
Users finding it important 
• 
Users finding it unimportant 
Timing 
• 
Seeing before 
• 
Seeing during behaviour 
• 
Seeing after 
Exposure 
• 
Users encountering it rarely 
• 
Users encountering it often 
 
5.3.2.4.2. 
Behaviour model matrix  
Srivastava & Shu (2015) investigated how to address the diversity of users and their 
motivations for changing behaviour. They develop a behaviour model addressing three 
motives: egoistic, sociocultural, and altruistic (Table 15). 
 
Table 15 Description of the egoistic, sociocultural, and altruistic motives (Srivastava & Shu, 2015) 
Egoistic motives 
Users act based on own self-interest. Generally, this includes a desire for 
increased convenience, simplicity, and productivity (Burton & Easingwood, 
2006). Reducing time, cost and effort will thus be motivating for the user 
Sociocultural motives 
Users act to be perceived positively by social groups such as family and 
peers. Fitting into social groups will thus be motivating for the user 
Altruistic motives 
Users act out of concern for others and the environment.  Taking care of 
the environment or others will thus be motivating for the user 
 
A tool is developed to incorporate the three motives to generate ideas across seven 
behaviour intervention strategies. The range of strategies reflect whether the user is in control 
or product is in control. Furthermore, the tool suggests to both generate positive and negative 
interventions for each strategy and motive, where the positive rewards a desired behaviour 
and the negative punishes an undesired behaviour. Thus, the tool incorporates three different 
perspectives to understand the problem and develop solutions: motivation, control, and 
encouragement. The tool has been used to generate ideas to reduce the use of elevators through 
behaviour change in Figure 15. 
 
==PAGE:73==
 
 
 
66 
 
 
 
 
Figure 15 Tool to develop ideas based on motive, level of control and negative or positive encouragement adapted from Srivastava & Shu 
(2015) 
 
ID 30. Dimensions of behaviour change  
ID 138. Behaviour model matrix  
Pros 
 
 
- 
Considers several dimensions influencing 
the effectiveness of interventions 
- 
Inspires through examples and pictures 
- 
Tested through several workshops 
 
Cons 
- 
The examples for inspiration are mainly 
product interventions 
- 
May be challenging to understand the 
coupling between principles and 
dimensions 
Pros 
- 
The motives appear to synergise very well 
with the institutional framework 
- 
Very simplistic 
 
 
Cons 
- 
Considers few perspectives influencing 
behaviour change 
- 
PSS related parameters, apart from the 
institutional framework, is weakly 
represented in the tool  
 
 
Step 5: Create ideas 
 
Dimensions of behaviour change (method ID 30) is selected as the primary tool to create ideas. 
It broader targets the parameters affecting behaviours in PSS. Additionally, the tool is 
presented in an inspirational format, potentially enhancing creativity. As Behaviour model 
matrix (method ID 138) appears to have a more explicit representation of institutions, 
the motives are incorporated in the empathy card. Also, the dimensions of behaviour 
change cards are found to be very product-oriented, thus, PSS-specific examples are 
added to a few of the cards. 
 
Overall, methods and tools were selected with some minor adjustments to fit into a PSS 
context based on the pros and cons. Furthermore, the process was considered to make a 
smooth transition between the methods. 
==PAGE:74==
 
 
 
67 
 
The workshop was set up in a digital format in Miro (Figure 16). An additional step of 
creating a persona (step 0) was added to collect the crucial knowledge obtained in the 
previous user studies about the targeted user group in a simple format for testing purposes. 
 
 
Figure 16 Overview of MVP method in workshop format

5.3.3. Testing and evaluating the MVP method 
The MVP method and the tools it contains are evaluated based on a set of requirements 
and performance criteria shown in Table 16, where test method, target and result can  be found. 
The test methods include self-assessment by participants, observations by authors during 
the workshop, analysis of reasons for unsustainable behaviour, analysis of ideas according 
to PSS elements (Mont, 2004b) and analysis of ideas based on control/obtrusiveness 
landscape (Daae & Boks, 2013). Results are found in Appendix X: Conducted workshop, 
Appendix XI: Participant self-assessment questionnaire, Appendix XII: Workshop analysis 
of observations, Appendix XIII: Workshop analysis of 5 whys, Appendix XIV: Workshop 
analysis of ideas according to PSS elements, and Appendix XV: Workshop analysis of ideas 
in control/obtrusiveness landscape, respectively. Five of six requirements are met. The 
requirement, Help designers understand which behaviour intervention strategies they should apply to 
change the behaviour of their target group, was not sufficiently met as one of the participants found 
the tool to only “maybe” help understand which strategies to use. Objectively, the tool 
suggests behaviour intervention strategies, and the response must indicate that the tool to 
select strategies or the results hereof was not clearly communicated. Regarding 
performance criteria, only five of nine criteria met their target. The four criteria that did 
not meet their target are: Should guide designers towards interventions with the highest sustainable 
impact potential, Should be possible to understand how to use tools in 15 minutes, Should be experienced as 
suggesting rather than dictating, and Should remind the designers of the aspects of a PSS that affect the way 
users interact with it.

Appendix

Overall, the MVP method is successful in creating ideas to change unsustainable behaviours 
in PSS. A strength is that the method is collaborative and sparked many interesting 
discussions among the participants (Appendix XII). Furthermore, all tools achieved their 
purpose except the User Acitivity cycle, that did not sufficiently identify unsustainable 
behaviours (Appendix X). The unsustainable behaviour selected by participants was: 
“Makes the illusion that fast fashion is sustainable. Easier to choose over actually sustainable clothing”. It 
does not describe an action by a user directly, which is the tool's purpose but instead 
describe the PSS. This resulted in problems for the coming steps, and the authors needed 
to interrupt and guide the designers further. An example of how the unsustainable 
behaviour could have been formulated is that “Users buy more clothes”. This would lead to 
reasons for behaviour such as “I recycle through ClooBin”, “ClooBin is a sustainable choice”, etc. 
 
 
5 A completely even distribution between ideas would equal 16% of ideas in each of the six categories. The target standard 
deviation is set to <8 indicating that the average dispersion from the mean should be below half of the 16%, which is 
regarded as an estimated even distribution 
==PAGE:77==
 
 
 
70 
 
 
 
Thus, a weakness of the MVP method is that it is not clear how some tools 
should be used. When selecting target behaviours, having a tangible table to compare 
the unsustainable behaviours showed to be a strength as it gave structure to discuss and 
select from. However, some less significant behaviours concerning environmental impact 
(e.g “people hold on to clothes that they are no longer using[…]”) rated almost as high as the selected 
behaviour due to equal evaluation between severity of environmental impact, the occurrence of 
behaviour, and ease of changing behaviour, why participants suggested weighting them. Another 
weakness identified through observations suggested that participants did not 
broadly explore the problem space. The few whys (three out of ten) connected to 
strategic characteristics, institutions, system characteristics and ownership dimensions could explain this 
(Appendix XIII). However, it may also be that PSS-related parameters do not cause the 
unsustainable behaviour. Lastly, participants were able to generate 26 ideas to change 
behaviour. The ideas were generated across all cards except for the direction card proving 
the cards to successfully reach their goal to generate ideas (Appendix X), which 
is a strength. However, the last cards the participants went through had fewer ideas, which 
indicates a drop in creativity after being presented with many cards.  
 
Figure 17 Mont analysis with the distribution of ideas (Appendix XIV). Product: 12%, service: 38%, network: 4%, infrastructure: 8%, 
feasibility: 4% and institutions: 27%. Three ideas are not categorised, and two ideas are counted double as they are placed between categories. 
Looking at the placement of ideas in Mont’s framework (2004b) showed that ideas did not 
include interventions to different PSS elements equally. Apart from one idea in customer 
satisfaction, no other concerned the PSS feasibility making up for 4% of the ideas, which 
also was the case for the PSS element, network. Ideas mostly included additions to service 
offering with 38% of ideas, 12% for product interventions, 8% for infrastructure and 27% 
for the institutional framework. This summed to a standard deviation of 12.7 showing a 
relatively high dispersion of the number of ideas within each category, indicating a 
weakness with an uneven distribution of ideas and narrow exploration of the 
solution space within PSS. 
 
Based on the strengths and weaknesses, suggestions are made regarding how the MVP 
method should be improved in each design process step. 
 
Step 1: Identify unsustainable behaviour 
• Provide more relevant PSS-specific examples connected to the PSS category to 
enable easier identification of unsustainable behaviours and help designers best 
identify user behaviours that could result from the PSS.  
 
Step 2: Select target unsustainable behaviour
