==PAGE:78==
 
 
 
71 
 
• The severity of the environmental impact of the behaviour must be weighted higher 
than the occurrence of unsustainable behaviour and ease of changing behaviour as 
an equal evaluation of the three could undermine the highest environmental impact 
potential.  
 
Step 3: Identify what affects the unsustainable behaviour 
• Provide relevant PSS-specific examples of reasons for unsustainable behaviour 
connected to the unsustainable behaviour identified by a designer to make designers 
explore a broader and more PSS-related problem space. 
 
Step 4: Select strategies to create desired behaviour 
• Make a clear connection between the reasons for the unsustainable behaviour and 
relevant strategies by linking the specific problems found in reasons to specific 
solutions reflected in dimensions of behaviour change to ensure designers create 
ideas in the suitable area (criteria 1). 
• Make a clear division of when the unsustainable behaviour is in focus and when the 
desired behaviour is in focus as participants had difficulties distinguishing between 
the two (Appendix XII). 
 
Step 5: Create ideas 
• Provide more relevant PSS-specific examples across the different aspects of the PSS 
that can be redesigned to allow designers to explore a broader and more PSS-related 
solution space. 
• Only show the cards relevant to create the desired behaviour to optimise the design 
process and avoid tiring out creativity. 
• Integrate questions on the cards into the previous step to create more coherence 
and less repetition to ease the creative process and avoid tiring out creativity. 
 
Overall, the method should be easier to use by either automating some activities or 
providing more examples to help designers understand how activities should be performed. 
The examples should be PSS-related both regarding the unsustainable behaviours, reasons 
for doing unsustainable behaviour and strategies to create the desired behaviour. 
 
5.4. 
Summary 
This chapter has identified the five main steps necessary in a design process to DfSB in PSS: 
1) Identify unsustainable behaviours, 2) Select target unsustainable behaviours, 3) Identify 
what affects the unsustainable behaviour, 4) Select strategies to create desired behaviour, 
and 5) Create ideas. These were selected due to their uniqueness for behaviour change 
approaches and not found in state-of-the-art PSS and product development. Afterwards, 
the most promising seven methods were selected as most feasible to reach the five steps' 
purpose through a structured selection process. These were then consolidated into a five-
step process and were slightly adjusted to suit the PSS context in order for it to be tested in 
an MVP format. A test was conducted with a team of two design students to identify the 
MVP method’s applicability and ability to change behaviour in PSS, which showed to be 
confirmed. Additionally, the method was evaluated based on performance requirements 
and criteria. This clearly showed strengths of the method, yet also weaknesses and need for 
improvements. Specifically, automation and exemplification need further improvement to 
better guide designers towards the most effective behaviour change with the highest 
environmental potential, which will be sought solved in the following chapter.  
==PAGE:79==
6. Improve the 
method 
==PAGE:80==
 
 
 
73 
 
6.1. 
Introduction 
The goal of this chapter is to improve the MVP method to design out unsustainable 
behaviours in PSS based on identified improvement opportunities. As it has been 
discovered that certain PSS-related parameters constituting the very construct of PSS give 
rise to unsustainable behaviours in these systems, this knowledge is sought implemented in 
the method. The improved method will be tested and evaluated by the authors to identify 
improvement opportunities and further work. Further, a critical reflection and limitations 
of the improved method and its applicability and usability for designers developing PSS will 
be carried out. 
 
6.2. 
Methodology 
This chapter seeks to answer the research question RQ4. How can PSS development 
be affected by an increased focus on unsustainable behaviours, while further 
investigating research questions initiated for discovery in the previous chapter. First, a step-
by-step redesign approach is taken on to implement the opportunities for improvement in 
the method. Second, the method is tested through scenario tests and a case study to 
reevaluate the test criteria that were not previously met and targets replying R2. and R3. 
Lastly, opportunities for improvement are constructed based on the test findings and the 
scenario tests, and critical reflection informs the limitations and further work. This will 
further aid in replying RQ4. 
 
6.2.1. Improving the method 
Based on suggestions for improvement identified in section 5.3.3, each step of the method 
was redesigned. For each step, a brainstorm was conducted to investigate how we best 
might utilise the knowledge acquired on unsustainable behaviours in PSS. How to couple 
the unsustainable behaviours to an actual behaviour change was in focus. 
 
While the extent of the data on unsustainable behaviours enriches the knowledge base, it is 
of high importance to develop solutions that would not make designers drown in data. With 
this in mind, the tool development was split into two parts: the back-end tool containing 
the construction and logic of how the data is being utilised to best support the designer, and 
front-end tool demonstrating the user experience for designers.  
 
The back-end logic was built in Excel as a natural result of the unsustainable behaviours 
database being in Excel ( 
 
 
==PAGE:81==
 
 
 
74 
 
 
 
Appendix VII). The front-end tool was created using the open-source programme Figma 
to enable a prototype interface and rethinking the format slightly, especially in terms of 
visual model and illustrations. 
 
The tools supporting step 1 to 4 of the method were customised in parallel, linking database 
knowledge to each of the critical steps based on the opportunities for improvement 
identified in the workshop (section 5.2.3). Step 5 required extra attention as this step 
includes the synthesis of translating problems to solutions - unsustainable behaviours to 
behaviour change. 
 
6.2.1.1. Step 5: Create ideas 
As the purpose of step five is to inspire designers to develop effective, sustained behaviour 
change, it is of high importance to keep the user that performs the unsustainable behaviour 
in mind and the knowledge about why that behaviour happens. The database has already 
collected numerous reasons for why unsustainable behaviours occur and formulated them 
in a generalized manner, as heuristics, why these remain connected to how that might be 
solved.  
 
All the different types of reasons identified to cause unsustainable behaviours in PSS (strategic 
characteristics, ownership dimensions, system characteristics, institutions and lack of institutions) were all 
gathered in a table to identify if a given parameter could be directly found represented in 
the existing dimensions of behaviour change cards from Daae (2014). If they were not directly 
represented, it was recorded whether existing cards could be slightly adjusted to grasp the 
parameter or if a new card was needed. This was done for each problem within a parameter 
(descriptions found in Table 4, Table 5, Table 6 and Table 7) by selecting the most obvious 
solution or way to solve the behaviour with a moderate level of abstraction. It implied not 
dictating a specific or concrete solution but rather a way to solve the problem which could 
be embodied in many ways. This connection is drawn based on the hypothesis that the sum 
of the identified obvious solutions makes up a complete solution space.  
 
 
Figure 18 Approach to connect problem space with solution space – unsustainable behaviours with behaviour change 
Having connected the parameters to the dimensions of behavior change cards (Daae, 2014), 
an overview of what needed to be incorporated in each card was created. This enabled a 
redesign of each card, incorporating the parameters through examples or probing questions 
for the tool's front-end prototype. As a general aim, PSS-specific examples were sought 
implemented in each card.  If the parameters entailed a focus in a specific direction of the 
card, it was registered in the back-end tool. 
 
==PAGE:82==
 
 
 
75 
 
The dimensions that were not found suitable to be incorporated into the existing cards were 
gathered in an affinity diagram (Holtzblatt & Beyer, 2017) to create categories for new PSS-
specific cards and corresponding scales. 
 
6.2.2. Testing and evaluating the method 
Both limitations of the back-end prototype and the improved method's usability were tested 
through a case study to evaluate its applicability. 
 
6.2.2.1. Scenario tests of back-end prototype 
As the back-end prototype uses the database of unsustainable behaviours, it was tested using 
different scenarios to gain insights into the limitations to its applicability. The scenario test 
was conducted by setting up scenarios with both product-oriented, use-oriented, and result-
oriented PSS. Each type of PSS was tested with different results of unsustainable behaviour and 
a phase in which they occur. A total of 12 scenarios was run: deselect PSS offering in the 
pre-phase, overconsume PSS offering in the during-phase, treat products in PSS carelessly 
in the during-phase, and misuse products in PSS in the post-phase to spread out the results 
without having to run 36 tests. The scenarios were tested for: 
 
• How many examples of unsustainable behaviours are suggested for each scenario?  
• How many examples of reasons for unsustainable behaviour are suggested in each 
scenario? 
• How many answers are suggested to select strategies? 
• How many cards were found relevant in each scenario? 
 
In step 1, each type of PSS's strategic characteristics were chosen based on Appendix III: 
PSS morphology. The morphology does not necessarily prescribe a variation to the strategic 
characteristics transfer of ownership, availability of offering, expansion of benefits, and level 
of digitalisation. The options in each characteristic were selected alternately. The result of 
the unsustainable behaviour for the scenario was selected as a possibility, and an “identified” 
unsustainable behaviour, Behaviour A, was classified by the result of the behaviour and designated 
phase, predefined for the respective scenario. Then, Behaviour A was selected in step 2. In step 
3, all suggested reasons were selected to affect Behaviour A in the PSS and in step 4, only the 
suggested answers were kept, and nothing was added or selected in the other questions. 
Nothing was done in step 5. 
 
6.2.2.2. Test and evaluation of case study 
The authors tested the improved method on a PSS concept that offers shared showers to 
dorm rooms in Copenhagen. The concept was developed in the course Development and 
operation of product/service-systems in the autumn of 2018 attended by the authors. The  PSS 
provider is a company producing high-end showers that can reduce water usage up to 85% 
and energy usage up to 75% by circulating water in the shower and purifying it through a 
filter. 
 
The test was conducted using the back-end prototype. The design process was simplified as 
both authors were familiar with the case study in terms of the business model and user 
group from previous interviews during course participation. Thus, the test started with step 
1 and the identification of unsustainable behaviours. Before starting step 3, a persona was 
created to understand the targeted user group performing the unsustainable behaviour 
before identifying what affects it. The test was concluded with step 5. 
==PAGE:83==
 
 
 
76 
 
 
 
 
The method was evaluated based on the requirement and performance criteria, where 
targets that were not met previously in the MVP method were tested. All apart from the 
criteria Should be possible to understand how to use tools in 15 minutes and Should be experienced as 
suggesting rather than dictating as the authors cannot objectively evaluate these. 
 
6.3. 
Results 
The MVP method is redesigned to accommodate the identified opportunities for 
improvement of the method. The redesign results in an improved method to DfSB in PSS, 
which is tested and evaluated by the authors. Lastly, opportunities for improvement of the 
method is identified.  
 
 
6.3.1. The improved method 
The method consists of five steps: 1) Identify unsustainable behaviours, 2) Select target 
unsustainable behaviour, 3) Identify what affects the unsustainable behaviour, 4) Select 
strategies to create the desired behaviour, and 5) Create ideas. As previously described in 
Figure 10, the method only targets five out of 11 steps in the design process to create 
sustainable behaviour in PSS. Knowledge collected through the steps before the method is 
a prerequisite to use the method. Same applies for the steps after, that are necessary to 
create sustainable behaviour effectively. Considering the placement of the design process 
in the larger context of developing new PSS’s, the method requires a PSS concept and can 
be used to iterate in the solution space and mitigate potential challenges before they arise. 
If used on an existing PSS, it can be used as a stand-alone method for redesigning the PSS. 
 
In the redesign of the method, the database of identified unsustainable behaviour in PSS is 
utilised by providing examples relevant to their PSS with the purpose of helping designers 
understand how to use the method. Different data points are used throughout the method. 
The improved method is prototyped with both a front-end and a back-end. The back-end 
is crafted as a functional prototype in Excel, and the front-end is created to give an 
impression of how a visual tool might work. Below a description of the integrated solution 
is found explaining each step with the activities to be conducted by the designer and the 
logic behind the workings of the prototype.  
 
6.3.1.1. Step 1: Identify unsustainable behaviours 
Step 1 aims to identify unsustainable behaviours that occur in the PSS, which requires 
three activities. In the first activity, the designer must define the PSS based on the 
strategic characteristics, whereafter the PSS category, according to Tukker (2004), can be found. 
The second activity will present four questions to answer. The questions are regarding 
the plausibility of either overconsumption of PSS, deselecting PSS, treating products in PSS carelessly 
or misusing products in PSS to occur. These are the four different categories describing the 
result of unsustainable behaviours. Depending on which of these four behaviours that are 
selected as being likely to occur, examples of unsustainable behaviours seen in other PSS 
will be generated for the third activity. In the prototype, a User Activity Cycle will appear 
with relevant examples of unsustainable behaviours from other PSS together with a 
description of the PSS divided in pre, during and post phase to inspire the designer. The 
examples will be generated from the database, where the unsustainable behaviours must 
match the criteria regarding the result of behaviours, while the User Activity Cycle can be 
==PAGE:84==
 
 
 
77 
 
used as a boundary object for the designer to identify unsustainable behaviours in their PSS. 
The unsustainable behaviours should be formulated as an action by the user. For each 
unsustainable behaviour added, the designer must select when the behaviour occurs and 
the result of the behaviour. 
 
 
 
 
Figure 19 Clipping of User Activity Cycle with examples of other unsustainable behaviours in use-oriented PSS experiencing users that 
overconsume PSS and treat products in PSS carelessly in front-end prototype, and one unsustainable behaviour added by designer 
 
6.3.1.2. Step 2: Select target unsustainable behaviour 
In the first of two activities during this step, the designer must evaluate the 
unsustainable behaviours identified in the previous step. The aim of step 2 is to choose 
which target behaviour to change. Each unsustainable behaviour identified is evaluated 
based on the environmental impact of the behaviour, occurrence of the behaviour, and 
plausibility of behaviour change. The designer must evaluate based on the following three 
questions: 
 
• How severe is the environmental impact? 
• How often does the behaviour occur? 
• How easy is the behaviour to change?  
 
The behaviours must be evaluated relative to each other and receive a score from 1 (low) 
to 5 (high). The score of environmental impact is weighted twice as high as occurrence and 
plausibility to ensure that it cannot be overruled as the most unsustainable behaviour should 
be targeted. The severity of environmental impact should be evaluated compared to a best-
practice alternative, which can both be hypothetical or actual. In the prototype, the 
==PAGE:85==
 
 
 
78 
 
 
 
unsustainable behaviours will be directly transferred from the previous step and 
automatically sum scores. If one behaviour has the highest score, this behaviour will 
automatically be suggested as the one that should be selected. However, the selection can 
be overruled by the designer in the second activity, where the unsustainable behaviour 
to be targeted is chosen. 
 
 
Figure 20 Clipping of evaluation scheme with unsustainable behaviours identified in the previous step by designer 
 
6.3.1.3. Step 3: Identify what affects the unsustainable 
behaviour 
The selected unsustainable behaviour may occur for different reasons depending on who 
the user is. The aim of step 3 is to understand what affects the behaviour of the targeted 
user group. First, the designer must answer whether the behaviour is habitual. Then, the designer 
should try to answer why this unsustainable behaviour occurs. The following questions will be 
displayed to aid the designer in identifying reasons caused by the PSS: 
 
• Why does the transfer of ownership from user to provider cause the behaviour? 
• Why does the user's level of responsibility during use cause the behaviour? 
• Why does the availability of the offering cause the behaviour? 
• Why does additional benefits offered to the user cause the behaviour? 
• Why does the economic value model (what the users pay for) cause the behaviour?  
• Why does the level of digitalisation in the offering cause the behaviour? 
• Why does psychological ownership (whether the user regard the product as their 
own or not) cause the behaviour? 
• Why does level of anonymity in the offering cause the behaviour? 
• Why does temporality (period of ownership, frequency of use and frequency of 
ownership) cause the behaviour? Why does temporality (period of ownership, 
frequency of use and frequency of ownership) cause the behaviour? 
• Why does the feeling of responsibility (or lack hereof) for the product life cycle cause 
the behaviour? 
==PAGE:86==
 
 
 
79 
 
• Why does the economic responsibility (or lack hereof) for the product life cycle cause 
the behaviour? 
• Why does the type of function offered through the product cause the behaviour? 
• Why does the option to add special meaning to the PSS cause the behaviour? 
 
Based on the selected unsustainable behaviour, suggestions for how different PSS-related 
parameters might affect the unsustainable behaviour are shown as an extension to the 
questions. In the prototype, these are generated from the database based on three criteria: 
1. the result of the selected behaviour, 2. when the behaviours occur and 3. the strategic 
characteristics of the PSS. The designer can select these suggestions or add other whys for 
the behaviour that is not connected to the questions. For each reason or why identified, the 
designer must try to answer why this reason causes the behaviour. Again, suggestions will be 
generated based on the database and the aforementioned criteria with the reason selected. 
If adding other reasons to a reason, the designer will be presented with a new question 
about whether rules of the society (institutions) might cause the behaviour. Behaviours affected by 
institutions are described as followed: 
 
• Normative institution: Behaviour occurs because users feel it is socially unacceptable 
to behave differently 
• Cognitive institution: Behaviours occurs because users will not be able to conceive 
other ways of acting 
• Regulatory institution: Behaviours occurs because users fear punishment 
• A lack of institutions that prescribe how to behave because the PSS introduces a 
novel consumption practice unknown to users 
 
Reasons for reasons can be added to the extent the designer pleases, and a tree diagram 
will emerge with all the reasons why unsustainable behaviour occurs. 
 
 
 
Figure 21 Clipping of reasons for why the unsustainable behaviour targeted occurs illustrated in a tree diagram, where four reasons are added 
by the designer 
 
==PAGE:87==
 
 
 
80 
 
 
 
6.3.1.4. Step 4: Select strategies to create desired 
behaviour 
The aim of step 4 is to identify what behaviour intervention strategies are most likely to 
change the behaviour of the user, but also when and how strategies should appeal to the 
users through dimensions. The step includes two activities. First, the designer must 
clarify the desired behaviour sought created. This should like the unsustainable behaviour 
be formulated as an action by the user. The second activity seeks to guide designers in 
the right direction concerning behaviour intervention strategies by answering the following 
questions about the desired behaviour: 
 
• Do users wish to behave the desired way? 
• Have you considered if users find the desired behaviour important or unimportant? 
• Have you considered if the desired behaviour is in line or opposing the norms and 
values of the users? 
• Is it crucial to demand attention of the users or crucial not to disturb? 
• Have you considered whether the desired behaviour should be promoted or 
unsustainable behaviour discouraged? 
• Have you considered if the users will wish to behave desired way simply because it 
evokes an emotional response or because it makes sense? 
• Have you considered if the users are focusing on themselves, the common good or 
on others and what others think? 
• Have you considered if you should target the users before, during or after the 
behaviour? 
• Have you considered how often the users will encounter the behaviour intervention? 
• Have you considered whether the users should be made responsible either 
economically or by evoking a sense of responsibility? 
• Are you creating a new behaviour, tweaking a current behaviour or copying an 
existing behaviour? 
 
The previous step and the reasons identified to affect the unsustainable behaviour provides 
an understanding of the user that can be utilised to answer the questions above. If a PSS-
related parameter is found to affect the unsustainable behaviour, a suggestion for an answer 
will be generated based on the connections found in Appendix XXI: Connections between 
PSS-related parameters and cards. The designer can also pick another option, and 
sometimes must if several or none of the answers are found to be connected.  
 
==PAGE:88==
 
 
 
81 
 
 
Figure 22 Clipping of questions about the desired behaviour sought created by the designer, where two questions have suggested answers based 
on reasons identified for the unsustainable behaviour 
 
6.3.1.5. Step 5: Create ideas 
Step 5 consists of 12 behaviour change cards: control on two cards and obtrusiveness, 
encouragement, meaning, direction, empathy, importance, responsibility, expectations, timing and exposure 
on one card each. All cards are shown in Appendix XX: Behaviour change cards. The 
behaviour change cards aim to help designers generate ideas to induce the desired 
behaviour. One of the control cards displays 16 behaviour intervention strategies placed on 
a scale depending on whether they give the PSS or the user control of the behaviour. The 
remaining 11 cards provide examples of how to create obtrusiveness, encouragement, etc. Most 
of the examples stem from other PSS. These 11 cards also display a scale similar to that of 
the control card with different ways in which each card can change behaviour in the given 
dimension, e.g. the designer can create encouragement by either promoting the desired 
behaviour, discouraging unsustainable behaviour or encourage neutrally. These are 
displayed together with a list of pros and cons that should enlighten the designer. All cards 
highlight one end of the scale to guide the designer in creating ideas with the most effective 
behaviour change. However, the way in which differs depending on the card. 
==PAGE:89==
 
 
 
82 
 
 
 
 
Figure 23 The two control cards, first of which is presenting the 16 behaviour intervention strategies, while the second presents three different 
ways in which control can be used to change behaviour: PSS in control, shared control or user in control supplemented with examples of how 
strategy can be applied and pros and cons. PSS in control is the suggested strategy 
The control and obtrusiveness card highlight one end of the scale based on answers to the first 
question in step 3 and first four questions in step 4: 
 
• Is the unsustainable behaviour habitual? 
• Do users wish to behave the desired way? 
• Have you considered if users find the desired behaviour important or 
unimportant? 
• Have you considered if the desired behaviour is in line or opposing the norms 
and values of the user? 
• Is it crucial to demand attention of the user or crucial not to disturb? 
 
Depending on the answers to the five abovementioned questions, a matrix generate 
whether strategies are suitable, not suitable or neutral depending on their level of 
obtrusiveness and control. Each of the 16 strategies has three options of obtrusiveness: 
unobtrusive, medium obtrusive and obtrusive. The suitability of these 48 possibilities is thus 
evaluated. The matrix is based on the tool to select strategies by Daae (2014) shown in 
Figure 14 and consolidates the five matrices generated by each question into one. The 
strategies are placed on the control scale based on whether they give the user control in one 
end, shared control in the middle or PSS in control in the other end of the scale. The 
strategies that have rated most possibilities suitable across obtrusiveness are recommended 
and highlight the corresponding end of the scale. In the same manner, the level of 
obtrusiveness with most suitable possibilities across strategies is recommended. However, 
some level of obtrusiveness can be found unsuitable. If this is the case, this part of the card 
is dimmed. 
 
==PAGE:90==
 
 
 
83 
 
Regarding the remaining behaviour change cards, the scale is highlighted based on the 
answers given in step 4, where each of the last seven questions will be representative for a 
card. As not all cards are equally relevant to change the behaviour, the tool also shows the 
cards found relevant based on the same logic that suggests answers in step 4. Instead of 
searching for a specific part of the scale connected to the reasons why unsustainable 
behaviour occurs, it looks for if a card (any part of the scale) is connected (Appendix XXI). 
The cards that could not be connected to any of the reasons why unsustainable behaviour 
occurs are placed lastly and greyed out. However, control and obtrusiveness cards are always 
relevant and shown as the first cards.

Lack of institutions 
- 
- 
· Creation of institutions 
(normative, cognitive or 
regulatory) to 
communicate and 
stabilise the right way to 
behave 
 
The reasons found to affect the unsustainable behaviour that required a new card are sorted 
in an affinity diagram (Appendix XVI: Affinity diagram for new behaviour change cards), 
which result in two new cards: responsibility and expectations. The responsibility card seeks to 
increase the responsibility towards of use of PSS by the user as this was found to cause 
unsustainable behaviours. The scale of the responsibility card suggests to either create a 
feeling of responsibility or actual responsibility. Feeling of responsibility is exemplified with 
Vigga and Patek Phillipe who both enhance the feeling of responsibility towards others. As 
discovered previously (section 4.4.1), Vigga managed to create a strong community 
especially enabled through social media which is a very socially oriented responsibility 
feeling. Patek Phillip appeals to a more ego-centred feeling of responsibility towards their 
next of kin promoting that “you never really own a Patek Phillipe – you simply take care of 
it for the next generation”.  Actual responsibility is commonly known from classic rental 
contexts where users pay a deposit in case of accidents, etc. They are thereby given the 
==PAGE:93==
 
 
 
86 
 
 
 
responsibility for a product in a given timespan. The expectations card seeks to align the 
user’s expectations to the PSS with the provider’s. Here, it is important to align with users 
whether the PSS offering seeks to copy existing practices or create new ones. Depending on 
which is the case, designers may use different means to enhance this similarity or distinction, 
such as the service design, affordances in the product, marketing and communication. 
When creating new behaviours or offerings, it can be beneficial to make use of exclusivity 
as exemplified with Ganni Repeat, while the example of copying an existing behaviour, a 
deposit system for cups in Tivoli makes use of affordances in the design of the cup deposit.  
 
Figure 24 Responsibility and expectations behaviour change cards 
The functional prototype can be used as a stand-alone tool if it is supplemented with the 
behaviour change cards. A poster explaining how to use the tool, a link to the printed files, 
alongside a video demonstration of the front-end interface can be found in Appendix XVII: 
Back-end prototype, Appendix XVIII: Poster guide for back-end prototype and Appendix 
XIX: Front-end prototype.  
 
 
 
6.3.2. Test and evaluation of the method 
The improved method's back-end prototype was tested in 12 scenarios of PSS and a case 
study with a PSS concept offering shared showers for dorm rooms. 
 
6.3.2.1. Scenario test of back-end prototype 
The scenario tests conducted with the back-end tool provide an overview of the different 
outcomes across the PSS categories and unsustainable behaviours that designers might 
meet when using the tool (Table 18). Thus, the scenario tests indicate which situations the 
tool might be most and least helpful for the designer.

6.3.2.1.1. 
Step 1: Identify unsustainable behaviours 
Most data on unsustainable behaviours are found in use-oriented PSS, especially in the 
during phase, why suggestions are highly concentrated around those scenarios. If no 
unsustainable behaviours are suggested in step 1, there are no aids or suggestions later on 
in the tool, assuming no new reasons that affect the behaviour are typed in by the designer. 
In situations where few unsustainable behaviours are being suggested, suggestions for 
answers or cards are not limited as long as there are many reasons connected to the 
behaviours deemed similar. As of now, the inspirational examples of unsustainable 
behaviours are unevenly distributed across the four possibilities. However, that might not 
be an issue of significance as it is also a result of few offerings within the product- and result-
oriented PSS categories.  
 
==PAGE:95==
 
 
 
88 
 
 
 
6.3.2.1.2. 
Step 3: Identify what affects the unsustainable behaviour 
Scenario 3, 6 and 10 show situations where only few reasons for the behaviours are 
presented despite suggested unsustainable behaviours. Especially in scenario 6, only two 
reasons are suggested with eight potentially similar unsustainable behaviours. This has to 
do with how the tool identifies relevant reasons, which is also constrained by when the 
behaviour occurs on top of the strategic characteristics of the PSS. The function is sorting 
out reasons connected to behaviours in other phases than the one selected, to avoid 
duplicates in the system and an overwhelming number of suggested reasons. Excluding 
reasons for when they occur is not based on reality as reasons for behaviours to occur are 
widely spread regardless of when they happen (Table 8). However, this structural limitation 
could be solved in future iterations by conditioning this limitation to the situations where 
only a few reasons are suggested.   
 
6.3.2.1.3. 
Step 4: Select strategies to create desired behaviour 
Maximum two suggested answers are given for recommended traits of the behaviour that 
designers should aim for creating. This is because the logic only proposes a reply when a 
singular direction of the card is recommended. The limitation could be improved in future 
iterations by suggesting the card's direction when both the middle option and one of the 
extremes are relevant.  
 
6.3.2.1.4. 
Step 5: Create ideas 
For all the scenarios where cards are suggested, meaning, empathy, responsibility and expectations 
cards are recommended to use. However, the last three cards have many connections to 
the PSS-related parameters. Meaning is not as broadly connected, yet it is connected to the 
PSS-related characteristics concerning responsibility, often affecting unsustainable 
behaviour. 
 
There is an indication of the direction card not being suggested as relevant in any scenarios. 
This is because it is only connected to the PSS-related parameters, expansion of benefits 
and normative, which more rarely affect behaviours. Additionally, the importance card only 
occurs once in the scenario suggestions, which also is due to the dimension only being 
related to functionality-linked behaviours.  
 
6.3.2.2. Test and evaluation of case study 
The test of the improved method on the case study can be found in Appendix XIX: Front-
end prototype and in Appendix XVII: Back-end prototype, which shows the prototypes 
filled in by the authors, respectively. Before the test, a persona is created to represent a 
targeted user group. The persona describes a young woman who recently moved from 
home into a dorm room. She likes pampering, and thus, showers are part of her daily 
routine to her father’s dismay. She does not consider sustainability in her general decision-
making, but she views it as an additional good. In step 1, 20 unsustainable behaviours 
experienced in other PSS are shown as inspiration. Many of these examples are repetitive 
as identical unsustainable behaviours were shown for different PSS companies. Five 
unsustainable behaviours are identified in this step for the case study. The evaluation of the 
behaviours relative to each other results in three behaviours rated 9, and two rated 11 in 
step 2. The close ratings suggest that further improvements to this step may be required 
to support the designer better. The unsustainable behaviour Take long showers was chosen. 
In step 3, four reasons are suggested, of which one are correct for this behaviour. However, 
looking through the questions concerning PSS-related parameters, three other reasons are 
==PAGE:96==
 
 
 
89 
 
found. One reason is found without a connection to parameters. As five out of six reasons 
are connected, the first target is met for the performance criterion Should remind the 
designers of the aspects of a PSS that affect the way users interact with it.  Having all PSS-related 
parameters displayed is advantageous when suggested reasons do not fully support the 
unsustainable behaviour at hand. In the next step, answers suggest “unimportant” for the 
question Have you considered if users find the desired behaviour important or unimportant? and “reason” 
for Have you considered if the user will wish to behave desired way simply because it evokes an emotional 
response or because it makes sense? Both of which are correct for the desired behaviour to Take 
short showers. All behaviour change cards except direction and exposure card are 
recommended to use, and a high level of obtrusiveness discouraged in step 5.  
 
In the test, it is found that 35 out of the 37 ideas are within the area of suitable strategies, 
although the area with unsuitable and neutral strategies is limited to very obtrusive for all 
16 behaviour intervention strategies and unobtrusive for information, feedback and 
enabling strategies, being a small area in the obtrusiveness/control landscape (Appendix 
XXIII: Case study analysis of ideas in control/obtrusiveness landscape). Thus, the area of 
suitable strategies is relatively larger than the one found in the workshop testing the MVP 
method, why these results are not comparable. However, the target remains the same and 
thus, the performance criteria Should guide designers towards interventions with the highest sustainable 
impact potential is met above target with 92% of ideas in the suitable area as shown in 
Table 20.  
 
Figure 25 Mont analysis of ideas from case study test (Appendix XXII). 10 ideas belong in two categories and are counted double but only 
presented in one on this figure. Product: 22%, service: 22%, network: 8%, infrastructure: 5%, feasibility: 22% and institutions: 41% 
Furthermore, the test results in eight ideas suggesting product interventions, eight for 
service, two for infrastructure, three for network. Regarding PSS feasibility, four ideas were 
suggested to increase customer satisfaction, three for business model, and one for 
environmental soundness (all ideas regard environmental soundness; however, this idea 
could not be placed elsewhere). Finally, 15 ideas were placed within the institutional 
framework with nine in normative, four in cognitive and three in the regulatory institution. 
The distribution of results gives a standard deviation of 11.4 for the ideas, which as for the 
MVP method does not meet the second target for the criterion, Should remind the 
designers of the aspects of a PSS that affect the way users interact with it. As shown in Figure 25, it is 
evident that there is a broad variety of ideas, yet infrastructure and network are very 
sparsely represented, which are part of the reason for the unmet target. Also, the large 
representation in the cognitive framework has an impact. Comparing the Mont analysis of 
==PAGE:97==
 
 
 
90 
 
 
 
ideas from the MVP method and the improved method (Table 19) shows as the standard 
deviation indicated a slightly more even distribution of ideas in the improved method, 
especially for product, service and feasibility. However, where the MVP test showed to give 
a majority of ideas within service, the improved method had an overweight of ideas 
connected to the institutional framework, which could be a result of authors being highly 
influenced by the knowledge gained around institutions and lack thereof in previous 
chapters 3 and 4. It becomes apparent that the network element is sparsely targeted in both 
tests, which could indicate that network may not be suitable for creating behaviour change. 
However, looking at the standard deviation subtracting the network ideas results in 12.5 
and 11.1 respectively, thus an insignificant change. As both the MVP and improved method 
show to have sparked ideas within all elements of the Mont star, indicating search for 
solutions in all PSS elements, it could be questioned whether the standard deviation is the 
right measure for the criterion. Therefore, we argue that the criterion is met as all elements 
are present in the solutions which must be an indication of the designers being reminded of 
PSS elements that affect behaviour.

The overall goal of the process to DfSB in PSS is to help designers change behaviour. As 
the method does not conclude with a prototyped concept that can be tested on users, it 
cannot be evaluated whether the users will change behaviour. However, the outcome of the 
tested method is 35 suitable ideas to change the behaviour of users across different 
elements in the PSS, and the method does therefore help the designers towards a behaviour 
change of users in a PSS. 
 
6.3.3. Opportunities for improvement of method 
The evaluation of the method leads to identify opportunities for further improvement of 
the method for each step. 
 
Step 1: Identify unsustainable behaviours 
• Instead of returning all examples of unsustainable behaviours that are sorted based 
on PSS category and the result of behaviour, it should also filter these examples to 
disregard unsustainable behaviours that occur in several PSS companies not to have 
repetitive examples. 
 
Step 2: Select target unsustainable behaviour 
• It can be challenging to evaluate the environmental impact of unsustainable 
behaviours relative to each other without an in-depth understanding. A qualitative 
approach was chosen as it is time-consuming and resource-intensive to assess this 
with a quantitative approach such as LCA. However, an approach that increases 
designers understanding of environmental impacts for different unsustainable 
behaviours and provides additional support should be further explored. 
 
Step 3: Identify what affects the unsustainable behaviour 
• Some of the identified reasons for unsustainable behaviour are overlapping across 
PSS-related parameters. A suggestion for how strategic characteristics and system 
characteristics are correlated with ownership dimensions can be found in Appendix 
VII: Unsustainable behaviours database. However, further consolidation and 
redefinition of PSS-related parameters would increase simplicity and designer’s 
understanding during this step instead of each parameter being presented with their 
own string of reasons. 
 
Step 4: Select strategies to create desired behaviour 
• Few answers were suggested in the back-end prototype to the questions helping 
designers in selecting strategies. More answers should be suggested by optimising 
the logics behind Appendix XXI: Connections between PSS-related parameters 
and cards. A suggestion is to return the answer based on the highest number of 
occurrences and indicate if the middle and extreme option of a dimension are both 
relevant. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:99==
 
 
 
92 
 
 
 
Step 5: Create ideas 
• The logic behind what cards are shown as relevant is not highly sophisticated as it 
is purely based on what PSS-related parameters that are selected and how they are 
linked to the dimension. It could be re-iterated to return fewer cards by weighing 
the importance of reasons and integrate these in the logic to recommend relevant 
cards, meanwhile balancing the implication of increasing the number of 
considerations for the designer. However, it is still unknown whether the number of 
cards is overwhelming for designers to utilise.  
• Integrating more examples to the cards that show interventions on all PSS elements, 
especially infrastructure should be investigated further.  
 
6.4. 
Discussion 
The improved method to design for sustainable behaviour in PSS is found to support 
designers in achieving behaviour change as it lives up to its criteria and requirements. One 
of the main contributions is the two additional cards added to the method to ensure a 
solution space capable of solving the unsustainable behaviours found in PSS. The premise 
of this addition is that the specific solutions (the behaviour change cards) identified for each 
specific problem (the unsustainable behaviour) collectively create a solution space 
containing the most relevant behaviour intervention strategies. However, the specific 
solutions were identified as the most evident. These are not necessarily the most effective to 
change behaviour – neither for unsustainable behaviours found in the database nor those 
identified by the designer when using the method. Take the example of leaving trash in 
shared cars caused by the user not feeling responsible. Would the best solution be to make 
the user feel more responsible towards the shared car or the other users in the system, or 
could it more effectively be solved by giving the user instructions when reaching the 
destination or disabling to end the ride when trash is left in the car? As discovered from 
behavioural design, 95% of human behaviour is irrational (section 3.3). Can the rational 
solution solve these? We argue that they can. All three solutions could be ideated from 
behaviour change cards. Also, the reasons identified for unsustainable behaviours include 
seemingly irrational reasons. Thus, the underlying assumptions for the method should not 
limit its applicability, however, it may not always guide towards the most effective strategies 
to change behaviour. 
 
Critically speaking, the method, in its current state, only suggests behaviour intervention 
strategies, and how and when these should appeal to users through different dimensions. In 
itself, this does not create impactful sustainable change. It does guide designers in selecting 
the most effective behaviour intervention strategies, but not towards the ideas with the 
highest potential for success. This is why the entire design process is critical to achieve 
successful behaviour change, and testing and evaluating interventions on users in an 
iterative process is key.  
 
The behaviour intervention strategies stem from methods and tools found within the DfSB 
field. In selecting tools to consolidate a method to DfSB in PSS, it is assumed that they 
sufficiently draw upon behavioural sciences in the context of products and services and thus 
applicable for a combination of such in PSS. The difference is that PSS provide the designer 
with more elements to change, such as the infrastructure and network, which is sought 
implemented in the behaviour change cards through examples. A limitation of our tool is, 
however, the incorporation of the business model. The behaviour intervention strategies 
are quite broad and could give rise to interventions within this area, but the dimensions 
==PAGE:100==
 
 
 
93 
 
that should suggest how and when different strategies should appeal to users do not 
necessarily lead towards such interventions, especially regarding the entire system's 
economic incentive structure. 
 
Another limitation of the method is the database utilised to help the designer by giving 
examples. The limitations of the database are also the limitations of the method regarding 
data size, cultural biases, etc. These limitations have been previously discussed in section 
4.5. The back-end prototype can, however, include more examples if data is added. 
Especially for product- and result-oriented PSS, the method’s support will be limited. This 
is accommodated by, e.g. giving the option to add reasons for unsustainable behaviour 
manually, and probing questions in each step of the guide. Furthermore, the behaviour 
change cards can be used as a stand-alone tool to ideate, although without guiding towards 
most effective behaviour change. 
Another advantage of having the behaviour change cards in a stand-alone format is that 
they are used with the desired behaviour as a starting point. Thus, the tool can also be 
utilised to create new behaviours, and not only change existing, supporting the potential of 
using DfSB to create circular behaviours such as repair, reuse, recycle, etc. (Wastling et al., 
2018). This was also supported by the test of the MVP method, where the desired behaviour 
sought created was Ensuring long use instead of premature disposal, which speaks into circular 
behaviours. 
The flexibility will allow the method to be easily adapted and potentially be useful for a 
range of PSS and the unsustainable behaviours experienced. 
 
Looking into the applicability of the method to DfSB in PSS in a broader perspective, it 
can potentially increase the focus and awareness of unsustainable behaviours that occur in 
PSS and thus, enable PSS companies to deliver on their sustainability targets and ambitions. 
However, an undesired side-effect would be if the focus on unsustainable behaviours could 
increase the scepticism of transitioning or creating PSS instead of viewing it as a tool for 
reducing the risk. Hopefully, this research and the proposed method can enable PSS 
practitioners to create sustained behaviour change and contribute to the overall 
institutionalisation of PSS in our societies. Climate change requires behaviour change. 
 
6.4.1. Further work 
The method requires further work beyond incremental changes to the tool as identified in 
improvement opportunities. 
 
• As only the authors tested the final iteration of the method, further work includes 
testing with PSS practitioners across different PSS types (both regarding type of 
offerings, PSS categories and type of product) to evaluate the applicability and 
usability more objectively and in real-life scenarios 
• The method mainly builds upon DfSB, but it would be interesting to look into 
behavioural economics as this field might be relevant for finding behaviour change 
principles regarding the economic incentive structure in PSS, why this should be 
investigated further  
• The database of unsustainable behaviours builds upon 19 identified behaviours. 
Further work includes collecting additional data that can enable higher applicability 
of the method 
 
==PAGE:101==
 
 
 
94 
 
 
 
6.5. 
Summary 
The improvement of MVP method resulted in a method to design for sustainable behaviour 
in PSS. The main contributions are to support the designer by integrating the knowledge 
database of what, how and why unsustainable occur in PSS and creating two new behaviour 
change cards, with the dimensions responsibility and expectations, to change the unsustainable 
behaviours found in PSS. The method was prototyped as a functional back-end tool and a 
mock-up of the front-end, both of which can be improved in terms of usability. To test if 
the proposed improvements would better the method the remaining requirement and 
criteria: Help designers understand which behaviour intervention strategies they should apply to change the 
behaviour of their target group, Should guide designers towards interventions with the highest sustainable 
impact potential and Should remind the designers of the aspects of a PSS that affect the way users interact 
with it, not previously met where tested. The method was found applicable to create 
ideas for effective behaviour change through case study test and evaluation as it showed to 
spark 92% of ideas within the suitable area for the user. Additionally, it was found to remind 
designers of what PSS aspects affect the way users interact with it as 83% of the whys could 
be connected to PSS-related reasons. Furthermore, scenariotests were carried out and 
found that, not surprisingly, scenarios with use-oriented PSS would provide the most 
support for designers. Thereby the method showed to live up to all the predefined 
requirements and criteria while it remains to be tested with design practitioners s in 
its final state. Lastly a set of improvement opportunities are presented mainly regarding 
details to improve the method and making it run more smoothly.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:102==
7. Conclusion
==PAGE:103==
 
 
 
96 
 
 
 
This thesis was initiated with research clarification, finding that PSS can give rise to 
unsustainable use, and thus, experience rebound effects despite their otherwise sustainable 
intentions. Literature suggested that this could be linked to the transfer of ownership. 
Therefore, a literature review for potential mitigators, methods and tools to solve 
unsustainable behaviours existing in PSS was conducted, yet no such method was found. 
The search discovered DfSB as a promising field to potentially tackle the unsustainable 
behaviours and thereby set the goal to consolidate, customize and evaluate Design for Sustainable 
Behaviour methods and tools suitable for a product/service-system context to make the systems perform 
sustainably. The goal was sought achieved by answering four research questions. 
 
RQ1 What unsustainable behaviour exists across different PSS categories and how are 
they connected to the transfer of ownership? 
 
This was investigated through a variety of PSS companies discovering unsustainable 
behaviours documented through literature and online customer reviews of different PSS 
offerings. 19 unsustainable behaviours across the three PSS categories were found, with two 
behaviours found in product-oriented PSS, 16 in use-oriented and one in result-oriented 
PSS. Additionally, four types of unsustainable behaviours were proposed through an 
inductive approach according to whether the behaviours result in: overconsume PSS offering 
covering increased consumption of the product or service, treat products in PSS carelessly 
covering careless treatment of products, misuse products in PSS covering knowingly harmful 
behaviours, and deselect PSS offering covering when users stop using the PSS due to its 
conditions. 
 
Understanding how the transfer of ownership is connected to these behaviours was carried 
out by analysing and classifying the unsustainable behaviours according to different 
parameters signifying PSS: strategic characteristics, system characteristics, ownership dimensions and 
the institutional framework. The parameters that mostly influence unsustainable behaviours 
showed to be the lack of established institutions in PSS, lowered responsibility, higher anonymity, higher 
availability of offering, long and short term temporality, and economic value model, although many other 
parameters representing the very construct of PSS also contribute. First of all, this shows 
that PSS are not yet an established institution in society, why there, in some situations, are 
no clear prescriptions of how to behave around them. Especially when users are offered a 
lowered responsibility, it can very likely lead to unsustainable behaviours as users do not 
understand how to behave and what to expect. The same can be said for economic value model 
were, as for many other parameters, the misalignment of expectations is evident. Anonymity 
and temporality parameters are considered to be contributing factors to the lowered feeling 
of responsibility and ownership. They are often a reflection of the absence of not least 
normative, but perhaps also regulatory institutions affecting the behaviour. Lastly, the 
availability of offering is a key driver for unsustainable behaviours as it not only encourages 
the overconsumption of offerings but also enables products being used more spontaneously 
or even recklessly. Additionally, it was found that the unsustainable behaviours occur in all 
three phases of the user activity cycle, and no dominating phase could be determined 
showing a broad span of unsustainable behaviours in PSS. 
 
The analysis and classifications led to the conclusion that unsustainable behaviours can be 
connected to the transfer of ownership, and even more, parameters such as lowered 
responsibility, increased availability, and the not yet established institutions in PSS that 
should prescribe how to behave were found to give rise to unsustainable behaviours. 
 
==PAGE:104==
 
 
 
97 
 
RQ2 How to support designers in addressing unsustainable behaviours and how to 
integrate it in the PSS framework? 
 
The investigation of how to support designers in addressing unsustainable behaviours led 
to a set of requirements and criteria for design methods based in literature. Five steps were 
identified as the most crucial to support designers: 1. identify unsustainable behaviours, 2. select 
target unsustainable behaviour, 3. identify what affects the unsustainable behaviour, 4. select strategies to 
create desired behaviour and 5. create ideas while preceding and following steps are required to 
obtain a behaviour change. Five methods were consolidated, tested and reiterated to best 
support designers in overcoming unsustainable behaviours in PSS. Important measures 
showed to be usability for designers, providing in-depth knowledge and understanding of 
users, while being best suitable for a PSS context. 
 
Supporting designers in addressing unsustainable behaviours was solved by an automated 
method that helps designers through the crucial steps by providing examples and insights 
regarding the unsustainable behaviours and behaviour intervention strategies: 
 
1. Providing designers with examples of PSS specific unsustainable behaviours, based 
on PSS type and what results of unsustainable behaviour could be found in their 
offering, to help identify unsustainable behaviours.  
2. Giving a structured approach to compare and select between identified 
unsustainable behaviours by addressing the environmental impact, occurrence and 
the ease of changing the behaviour. 
3. Proposing specific reasons for designers to determine why the selected unsustainable 
behaviour occurs regarding strategic characteristics, ownership dimensions, system dimension 
and institutions based on the PSS’s category, strategic  characteristics, the behaviour 
sought to change and when it occurs.  
4. Asking questions regarding what desired behaviour designers wish to create based 
on the different dimensions that influence behaviour in PSS, while suggesting replies 
based on reasons for unsustainable behaviour. 
5. Suggesting design aspects from DfSB and PSS to most optimally create the desired 
behaviour based on knowledge about the user and the unsustainable behaviour.  
 
The proposed method is recommended to be integrated and used in the PSS framework 
when either experiencing unsustainable behaviours or as risk management having 
developed a conceptual PSS.  
 
RQ3 How can behaviour intervention strategies mitigate unsustainable behaviours in 
PSS and achieve lasting sustainable change? 
 
Many different intervention strategies to develop sustainable behaviour exist. This project 
found 16 behaviour intervention strategies through literature suitable to solve unsustainable 
behaviours in PSS: information, feedback, enabling, reminding, increasing motivation, creating goals, 
rewarding, threatening, persuading, punishing, guiding, making easier, making difficult, disabling, forcing, 
and automatic. Furthermore, dimensions for when and how strategies should appeal to users: 
meaning, empathy, timing, obtrusiveness, control, exposure, direction, importance, and encouragement,  
while suggesting two new categories responsibility and expectations to tackle some of the 
prominent challenges in PSS such as lowered responsibility and misalignment in expectations. These 
have shown to provide promising ideas investigating a broad solution space to create 
sustainable behaviours in PSS. We argue, that as the method suggests user tests before 
==PAGE:105==
 
 
 
98 
 
 
 
implementing the intervention and thereby ensures that the desired behaviour can be 
achieved, the proposed method enables reaching lasting sustainable behaviour change.  
 
RQ4 How can PSS development be affected by an increased focus on unsustainable 
behaviours? 
 
Having developed a method for DfSB in PSS will likely affect PSS development. With an 
increased focus on unsustainable behaviours and the fact that the construction of PSS can 
be part of why unsustainable behaviours occur, it can perhaps have a limiting effect on the 
companies daring to make the transition. However, it should mostly serve as an enabler for 
more prosperous PSS to arise. With an increased focus and knowledge about the challenges, 
it is possible to design out, steer around or tweak the parameters that give rise to 
unsustainable behaviours in a way that does not allow for them to exist. We propose that 
the behaviour change strategies can be used to design out unsustainable behaviours from 
PSS and thereby be part of the solution to establish strong institutions within PSS. 
 
 
Having replied the four research questions, only the measure for success remains: Help 
designers to design impactful behaviour change for sustainable behaviour. As the method has shown to 
develop 92% suitable ideas and broadly investigates the solution space, the measure is 
deemed successfully reached.  
 
By developing a method to design for sustainable behaviour in PSS, we have taken the first 
steps towards helping designers create impactful behaviour change for sustainable 
behaviour in PSS. 
 
 
==PAGE:106==
 
 
 
99 
 
Process reflection 
 
Due to the time limitations for this study, the global pandemic, and the ambitions to end 
up with a tested method or tool, the opportunities for contextual investigations to identify 
unsustainable behaviours was limited. Also, one could end up searching near and far as not 
all PSS have unsustainable behaviours. The insights were sought gained by reaching out to 
PSS providers yet without much luck. Searching through literature and the web did not 
either provide much insight. This may be due to the fact that the field of PSS is still a rather 
new solution to our climate challenges, and there is still a limited amount of PSS. It could 
also explain why much research focuses on onboarding more customers to PSS 
programmes (purchase behaviour). Lastly, PSS companies may not be very interested in 
sharing or posting unsustainable behaviours as the sustainability of their systems often 
functions as a key selling point. 
 
Unfortunately, we were only able to recruit two students for our workshop to test MVP 
method. The aim was to test the our method with 5-8 PSS practitioners (students currently 
developing PSS through course). Luckily the participants were highly engaged and 
provided plenty of feedback and outputs to analyse. 
 
Lastly, our process has been constrained by our prior knowledge going into this project. 
Neither of us had conducted research before, or developed a design method, which has 
been challenging, but also educational. However, much time has been spent in the cloud 
in search of new and ground-breaking findings.

Appendix I: Literature review 
 
 
 
ICED 
1 
INTRODUCTION 
COLPaWH FKaQJH UHPaLQV RQH RI WKH ELJJHVW WKUHaWV WR RXU ZRUOG¶V VRFLHWLHV aQG ZH QHHG WR GUaVWLFaOO\ 
innovate to come up with solutions to target our emissions and overconsumption. Product/service-
systems (PSS) has formerly been highlighted as a promising part of the solution as it allows more users 
per produced product, is projected to take better advantage of products and even prolong their lifespan 
thereby reducing their environmental impact (Manzini & Vezzoli, 2002). This is however not always 
the case despite their ambitious intentions (Kjaer et al., 2019). As the ownership of products in PSS are 
transferred or shared between users and suppliers, the products are treated differently by the users. One 
example is the electric scooters that can be rented by the minute in several European metropolises. They 
only operate for 29 days before they are scrapped (Lindholdt, 2019), which is partially caused by users 
not handling the scooters correctly. Similarly, the baby stroller manufacturer, Bugaboo, found that by 
offering their products as a PSS, one third of all strollers were highly damaged and needed refurbishment 
after only one use-cycle (Sumter, Bakker, & Balkenende, 2018). This conflicted with the expected 
durability by Bugaboo, which suggest a change in user behaviour in a PSS compared to purchased 
products. Thereby the behaviour of the users within these systems jeopardize the environmental impact 
of PSS and their potential is overshadowed by negligent consumer behaviour. 
This study was initiated with the aim to investigate potential mitigations or design choices that could 
make users treat PSS more sustainably and thereby improve their environmental performance. This was 
done through a systematic review with the following two objectives: (1) Systematise methods and tools 
for the development of product/service-systems based RQ ³GHVLJQ IRU VXVWaLQaEOH EHKaYLRXU´ (DISB); 
(2) Identify barriers and success factors in design for sustainable behaviour within product/service-
systems. The ultimate aim is to support designers to create or redesign PSS that can trigger a more 
sustainable behaviour. 
2 
BACKGROUND: DESIGN FOR SUSTAINABLE BEHAVIOUR 
Design for Sustainable Behaviour is a design approach that aims at improving the sustainability 
performance of products, services and systems during their use phase (Selvefors & Renström, 2018). 
DfSB is an ever-expanding field that continues to gain interest due to its increased relevance both in 
relation to user-oriented design (correct utilization of products) and later sustainable design (sustainable 
use of products). Within the field some of the most well know frameworks or methods are presented 
here, as they form the foundation of the field of DfSB. 
Lilley (2009) applies three behaviour intervention strategies in the design: eco-feedback to guide change 
(i.e. tangible signs as reminders to inform users of resource use), behaviour steering to maintain change 
(i.e. encourage users to behave sustainably based on embedded affordances and constraints), and 
persuasive technology to ensure change (i.e. methods to influence what people think or do). The 
strategies are placed in relation to power in decision-making, whether the user or the product have power 
to change the behaviour. Zachrisson & Boks (2010) connects the effectiveness of behaviour intervention 
VWUaWHJLHV WR WKH XVHU¶V aWWLWXGH ZLWK WKH aLP WR KHOS GHVLJQHUV GHFLGH ZKLFK EHKaYLRXU LQWHUYHQWLRQ 
strategy to apply. Informative behaviour intervention strategies require a willingness to change 
behaviour, thus, a positive attitude towards the intended behaviour. 
In 2009, Lockton, Harrison and Stanton present their proposed framework Design with Intent. This 
design tool is created for designers to be able to influence the behaviour of users in a general manner 
(including and going beyond sustainable behaviour). The goal of the tool is to redesign a behaviour 
towards a particular desired behaviour through the use of six ³OHQVHV´: (1) error-proofing lens (deviations 
from target behaviour are treated as "errors"). (2) persuasive lens (use systems with interfaces to 
persuade users with information). (3) visual lens (use forms, smells, sounds, textures, etc. to influence 
users´ behaviour). (4) security lens (use countermeasures to deter and/or prevent undesired behaviours). 
(5) cognitive lens (influence users´ behaviour by understanding how they make decisions). (6) 
architectural lens (use the structure of systems to influence users´ behaviour). The lenses are based on 
different behaviour strategies that help the designer approach a solution from different perspectives. The 
lenses can be used loosely as an inspiration for concept development or in a more structured way by 
breaking target behaviours down to interactions in a prescription mode which serves as a prescribed 
design process in the development phase.  
==PAGE:114==
 
 
 
107 
 
 
ICED 
Tromp, Hekkert, & Verbeek (2011) describe a framework for socially responsible behaviour that 
explains the relationship between the product, human behaviour and its implications. It consists of 11 
behaviour strategies that are split into the four categories: decisive, coercive, seductive and persuasive 
influences. These 11 strategies are intended to inspire the generation of ideas when designing for a 
certain behaviour. It is argued that a design may be categorised differently according to the specific user 
utilizing the design. Tromp et al. (2011) argues that there is a need to investigate the social implications 
that come with the framework presented by Lockton et al. in 2009, which this framework is a response 
to. Once selecting a strategy, the designer should take into account whether there are conflicts between 
individual and collective concerns, which should then inform the choice of strategy.   
Selvefors & Renström (2018) presents a holistic approach of how to create sustainable behaviour within 
different layers of design, ranging from what activity to enable users to perform to how different 
functions are communicated to the user.  Selvefors & Renström mention that a variety of different 
behaviour strategies can be used to affect behaviour within different layers of design, which they 
suggest, can be sorted into five categories: Enlighten, spur, steer, force and match (see Figure 5). 
Looking at the early research and tools within DfSB this division of behaviour strategies show to be 
DSSOLFDEOH DV ZHOO. (.J. /LOOH\¶V (FR-IHHGEDFN (2009) EHORQJV WR HQOLJKWHQ DQG /RFNWRQ HW DO.¶V 
architectual lense (2009) is a steering strategy as it is directing the user to make the sustainable choice.  
A common trait among these DfSB frameworks is that not any of them are directly linked to the 
development nor redesign of PSS. Lockton et al. (2009) mention that the framework is especially 
UHOHYDQW IRU V\VWHPV ³ZKHUH XVHUV' EHKDYLRXU LV LPSRUWDQW WR LWV RSHUDWLRQ«´ ZKLFK ZRXOG LQFlude PSS, 
yet this may just as well refer to products that operate with a high-energy consumption. Therefore, a 
broader search of what exists within the field is necessary.  
 
3 
RESEARCH METHODOLOGY 
The main objective of this study is to systematise methods and tools for the development of PSS based 
on DfSB. Furthermore, to identify barriers and success factors in the same field of research as the 
stepping-stone to evaluate methods and tools for future work. A systematic review was conducted to 
reach the objectives. The review protocol is structured based on PRISMA-P with the inclusion of 
elements from Biolchini et al. (2007).  
Studies was collected through Scopus, based on the following search string: 
( TITLE-ABS-KEY ( "Product Service System"  OR  "PSS"  OR  "Product Service Solution"  
OR  "Product as a service"  OR  "Serviti?ation"  OR  "Product Design"  OR  "Service 
Design"  OR  "Product Development"  OR  "Sustainable Products" )  AND  TITLE-ABS-KEY 
( "Sustainable Behavi*r"  OR  "Sustainable Consumer Behavi*r"  OR  "Sustainable 
Consumption"  OR  "Sustainable Use"  OR  "Green Behavi*r"  OR  "Green Consumer 
Behavi*r"  OR  "Green Consumption" )  AND  ( ALL ( "Challenge"  OR  "Barrier"  OR  
"Obstacle"  OR  "Limitation"  OR  "Success factor"  OR  "Best practice"  OR  "Achievement"  
OR  "Accomplishment"  OR  "Successes"  OR  "Reali?ation" )  OR  TITLE-ABS-KEY ( "Tool"  
OR  "Method*"  OR  "Principle"  OR  "Intervention"  OR  "Guide*"  OR  "Approach"  OR  
"Rule"  OR  "Technique"  OR  "Design process"  OR  "Framework" ) ) ) 
The relevant studies were selected according to the following criteria reflecting the objectives of the 
study: (1) studies must describe observed barriers in designing for sustainable consumer behaviour; (2) 
studies must describe success factors in designing for sustainable consumer behaviour; (3) studies must 
include methods and/or tools targeted towards designing for sustainable consumer behaviour. The initial 
set of studies were selected with an abstract, keyword and title only search; barriers and success factors 
where sought for in full articles. An evaluation of the systematic review execution was performed, 
investigating potential limitations of web search engines utilized in the study.  
The search returned 263 unique studies that were first screened using the abstract according to the three 
criteria, which resulted in 168 articles left for eligibility. These were sorted by reading the full text of 
the study. The execution of systematic review included 51 studies in total for further analysis. The 51, 
initially 53, was re-iterated to clean the data.  
==PAGE:115==

ICED 
Figure 1. Systematic review process and result of screening and eligibility 
The 51 studies were analysed by identifying the following parameters: 
x 
General information: type of system (product, service, PSS, system); and type of product 
(consumables, mobility, energy, etc.) 
x 
Methods and tools: type (framework, method, tool, approach, business model, guideline, initiative, 
model, procedure, process, strategy, toolbox, toolkit, typology); name; description; goal; design 
phase (discover, define, develop, deliver (Design Council, 2019)) and performance  
x 
Success factors and Barriers 
x 
Behavioural intervention strategies 
Methods and tools were also denoted as framework, process, etc. as the terminology used is based on 
that stated in the study, and thus not critically assessed whether the use of definitions have been used 
similarly. Thus, the use of the word method will hereinafter entail all the terms stated previously. A 
success factor is identified as an entity that is necessary to reach a successful outcome of the method. A 
barrier is closely related to success factors. However, a barrier describes an entity that prevents a 
successful outcome of a method. The parameters were further analysed and split into groups. The groups 
were defined from a bottom-up approach. Behaviour principles were grouped in accordance with 
Selvefors & Renström (2018) as these categories could grasp the wide variety of principles found, see 
Table 1.

4 
RESULTS 
The studies included in the analysis started being published in 2002 and the amount of studies have since 
steadily grown each year as seen on Figure 2. The findings indicate that research concerning DfSB is 
increasingly becoming of interest. However, with a total amount of 51 articles included in the analysis, 
the field is still quite new and premature. The research has also developed in different directions over 
the years. Some of the first studies published were focused on modular upgradability (Briceno & Stagl, 
2006) and user-centered design (Wever, van Kuijk, & Boks, 2008) until the integration of psychology 
(Gulden et al., 2010; Cheng et al., 2014).  
Figure 2. Number of articles published per year that is included in the analysis 
 
In the 51 studies, a total of 45 methods were presented. An overview of the methods is found in Figure 
3, with an overview of the distribution of methods focused at product design (28), product or service 
design (5), service design (1), circular economy design (5), PSS design (5) and system design (1). Thus, 
the DfSB methods are mainly thought to be applied to products. 
4.1 Methods and tools for DfSB 
The methods have been analysed according to which design phase(s) (discover, define, develop, deliver) 
they are targeted towards. 18 methods targeted discover, 19 targeted define, 41 targeted develop and 11 
targeted deliver. This shows a clear focus on development of behaviour strategies and interventions. As 
the focus of the study is founded within behaviour interventions and change, the studies included will 
naturally be focusing on redesigning to avoid unsustainable behaviours since a prerequisite for change 
is an existing behaviour. Thereby the design process is initiated with an existing problem, which may 
be the reason for a majority of methods focusing in the solution space rather than the problem space. 
Only six methods are targeted towards all four design phases, indicating a gap of holistic DfSB 
approaches grasping the full design process. 
The type of product suggested or applied in the method have been indicated with a colour in Figure 3. 
This figure shows that 14 studies target different consumables, while 15 target consumer electronics. 
Including products such as lamp, washing machine, interfaces in building, mobility and energy, a total 
of 21 articles target behaviours to reduce energy usage. This may be caused by the fact that high energy 
usage of products can be directly related to the behaviour users have around a product in its use phase 
which clearly has an environmental impact. The complete list of the identified methods, with their 
respective 
codes, 
descriptions 
and 
references 
can 
be 
found 
in 
the 
following 
link 
(https://cutt.ly/ihIR7fW).

4.2 Behavioural intervention strategies 
To gain an understanding of which behavioural intervention strategies were applied within the DfSB 
field, all strategies were listed for each method included in the analysis (Table 2). Enlighten and steer is 
the most applied strategies with 56 and 58 occurrences, respectively. Spur strategies are also frequently 
applied with 34 occurrences, especially within consumer electronics and energy-related products with 
28 occurrences as seen in Table 3. This table also shows a vast majority of the behaviour strategies occur 
in articles focusing on consumer electronics (99), this despite of an even distribution between articles 
regarding consumer electronics (15) and consumables (14). This may be an indication of behaviour 
strategies mostly target consumer electronics as they may be easier or cheaper to implement in consumer 
electronics compared to other consumables.
