A total of 166 strategies are mentioned across the 45 methods. Generally, enlighten, spur and steer 
categories have the highest recurrence which can be due to their low-cost high-gain condition as 
compared to force mechanisms which may risk scaring away customers, and match strategies that can 
be resource intensive to incorporate. 
4.3 Barriers and success factors 
In total, 31 barriers and 18 success factors were identified within the 51 studies. They were grouped 
based on description (see Appendix A) and whether they were general or specific to the method as seen 
(Figure 4).  
 
Figure 4: Barriers and success factors grouped together across articles. Parenthesis 
indicate number of success factors or barriers mentioned in the study within a certain group. 
In total, 14 were general barriers for achieving sustainable behaviour and 17 were method-specific 
barriers. Knowledge gap is by far the most prominent barrier (12) indicating the general prematurity of 
the field. Regarding success factors, nine were method-specific, while the remaining nine were general 
towards the success of behavioural interventions. User satisfaction is the type of success factor 
mentioned most in the study (5) underlining the importance of satisfied users in order to gain successful 
behaviour change followed by need for user inclusion (3) (highly related to user satisfaction) and 
accompanied by other theory (3).  
5 
DISCUSSION 
The analysis of the 51 eligible studies resulted in 45 methods, 31 barriers and 18 success factors. 
However, only 11 studies addressed PSS, and just six of these were targeted PSS directly. These six 
methods indicated all different types of limitations ranging from the lack of testing the methods on real 
life cases or the limited amount of products/ PSS they were tested on to the lack of thorough prescriptions 
of how to apply one or multiple steps into a design process. Additionally, none of these six methods 
targeted to change the unsustainable behaviours stemming from the transformed ownership embedded 
in the PSS. All of which suggest that the field is unexplored and needs further development.  
Interestingly, three studies suggested that DfSB methods could be applied on both products and 
product/service-systems. The studies did however not touch upon the fundamental differences between 
the two in terms of the relationship between user and provider as well as the motivation behind 
behaviours. The presence of these differences is supported by Catulli et al. (2017), who argue that 
==PAGE:119==
 
 
 
112 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ICED 
RZQHUVKLS RI D SURGXFW LV FXOWXUDOO\ LPSRUWDQW IRU XVHU¶V LGHQWLW\ DQG VRFLDO VWDWXV, DQG WKDW PSS FDQnot 
fulfil these needs. Thus, PSS cannot obtain the same attractiveness even with additional functional 
offerings as ownership of products. We argue that the differences stretch beyond the purchase decision 
and into the use phase, where the same social attachments to the product will lack in a PSS and result in 
a changed (and unsustainable) behaviour. 
Common for most methods in the analysis is the process of selecting the most effective and relevant 
behavioural intervention strategies. This is a complex task as it depends on type of product (Chamberlin 
& Boks, 2018), attitude of user (Zachrisson & Boks, 2010) and cultural context ((Santamaria et al., 
2016)). This indicate that there are several context specific factors that are critical to achieve sustainable 
behaviour through the methods. The question is whether the conditions of PSS should be considered as 
a variation of such factors thereby complicating the choice of intervention strategies to be used in a PSS 
context. This can be an explanation for the prematurity of the methods within the field. 
As the complexity of selecting intervention strategies is rather high, they are either tackled by and 
extensive amount of knowledge as it is the case for Chamberlin & Boks (2018). Shih et al. (2017) suggest 
a data-driven approach to address the complexity yet this is a highly resource intensive approach. 
Thereby this complexity of the field somewhat serves as a high entry-barrier that can only be overcome 
by extensive knowledge or resources in order to successfully utilise and apply DfSB. Shih et al. (2017) 
suggest a data-driven approach to combat the complexity yet this is a highly resource intensive approach. 
Thereby this complexity of the field somewhat serves as a high entry-barrier in order to successfully 
utilise and apply DfSB. 
In total, 21 out of the 51 studies included in the analysis focused on the design of energy-consuming 
products such as consumer electronics, interfaces in buildings, washing machines, etc. This distribution 
suggests that either the potential of changing energy-consuming behaviours is significant (Liedtke et al., 
2015) or it is less resource demanding to incorporate behavioural interventions for these products. As 
digitalisation has also become very popular over the years, this focus on consumer electronics could also 
be caused by the trend. However, consumer electronic products are not alone when it comes to being 
used unsustainably, as seen for the case of Bugaboo. Non-electronic products may just as well be treated 
unsustainably, yet the relevance for prolonging lifetime and preserving the products in use remains the 
same whether products are electronic or not. Therefore, there is a need for extension of the research, 
investigating DfSB without the use of intervention strategies focused on electronics. 
 
6 
CONCLUSION 
This research aimed at systematising methods and tools for the development of PSS based on DfSB and 
to identify barriers and success factors with the long-term objective of supporting designers to create or 
redesign PSS so that users will use them with more sustainable behaviour. This review shows a gap in 
the current research within the field of DfSB targeting PSS. Methods with the potential to achieve 
sustainable behaviour in PSS do exist, but the limited amount of studies together with the amount of 
success factors and barriers indicate that the methods have yet to be proven. Furthermore, in the analysis 
of the methods we found that a majority of methods have a limited prescribed procedure. Applying the 
methods on the redesign of a PSS would thus provide essential knowledge for the further development 
of DfSB in PSS. Thus, further work will include an evaluation of the different methods currently 
available, and application of the one that show most potential in the redesign of a PSS. Future work 
includes an evaluation of the methods found in terms of usability for designers. Selected methods should 
be applied in the redesign of different PSS with varying product offerings. Furthermore, it could be 
interesting to test DfSB methods for products on PSS to test their applicability.

Jeg kender til flere der ikke længere tør lade deres egne biler offentligt, 
uden selv at være i bilen, af frygt for at respektløse delebils chauffører 
skal skade bilen. 
“ Min bror og jeg fik en bøde på 5000kr for noget skade der ikke var 
os. Det var sensoren der var i stykker og hvordan skulle man kunne se 
det før man har kørt de første 10 min. Da vi fandt ud af sensoren var i 
stykker skiftede vi bil for det gad vi ikke.”  
” Første tur med GreenMobility startede ikke super godt ud. Fandt 
bilen på p-pladsen i Stavtrup, vinduerne var rullet ned og der var blade 
og brugt slikpapir i bilen.”  
” Vi købte (desværre) en stor minutpakke og har nu prøvet dem nogle 
gange. Vi har bl.a. oplevet […] at biler flyder med skrald fra tidligere 
bruger (særlig en var utrolig slem. Deres svar: det er noget man som 
kunde må forvente kan ske - det vil de ikke gøre noget ved. Ikke super 
fedt, særligt hvis man skal i byen eller har travlt).”  
” Baseret på mine - indtil videre - 6 kørte ture, lander vi et sted lidt 
over middel. […] Tre gange har jeg oplevet at komme hen til en bil, 
der er i ringe forfatning og endnu har jeg ikke oplevet at der var nogle 
skader registreret i app'en. […] Det gør det pludselig unconvenient at 
køre i bybil, når man skal bruge de første 5-7 min. på at dokumentere, 
at man ikke er dén der har smadret bilen, og det tror jeg ikke er 
hensigten.”

Appendix VII: Unsustainable behaviours database 
 
Link to view and download full version: https://www.dropbox.com/scl/fi/4l8awvm7k3747b2yk8b94/Unsustainable-behaviours-
database.xlsx?dl=0&rlkey=njd04ph0pofodo7qx9v0n40eg

Appendix VIII: Analysed unsustainable 
behaviours 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users expect new looking and aesthetic products  
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 9 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Deselect PSS offering 
When: Pre 
How:  
Option 1: Some users have high expectations for shared cars, when they only pay per use and may deselect the PSS based 
on bad experiences. Additionally, users may not trust the digital technology that is part of enabling the PSS. 
Option 2: Some users have high expectations for shared cars, when the car is considered a personal product that to some 
extent expresses identity and may deselect the PSS if expectations are not met. 
 
Why:  
Option 1: The reason for the behaviour to happen is because users do not feel ownership nor responsibility over the product 
thereby giving users high expectations for the condition as it is handled professionally. This might be a result of an existing 
normative institution where users desire new-looking cars to obtain social status, which shared car providers uses to be 
attractive. 
When there is high anonymity in the PSS users are concerned, they will be held responsible for damages created by others. 
This might be a result of an attempt to create a regulatory institution, that in some instances instead makes people deselect 
the offering. Also, there is a conflict between convenience and the time-consuming requirement to check and report 
damages to avoid consequences from regulations within shared car company. 
Option 2: The feeling of the product being personal may be supported by users considering the product to be their own. 
The deselection of PSS might be a result of an existing normative institution where users desire new-looking cars to obtain 
social status, which shared car providers uses to be attractive.  
 
Whom: User & provider (as the provider feeds the desire by taking cars out of cycle prematurely) 
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users brake rules despite knowing them (derived from: Users smoke cigarettes in the car) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 9 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Misuse products in PSS 
When: During 
How: Users may brake known rules in the PSS when they see the value of it as a transfer of responsibility. When the 
ownership of the product is never transferred to the user despite it being in their possession, and they have little 
responsibility it may contribute to the unsustainable behaviour. The car is only fulfilling a basic need (transport) therefore 
the user has little or no attachment to the product.  
 
Why: The reason for the behaviour to happen is that users in general do not feel ownership over the cars. This is due to 
the fact that the products often shift between different users, that they do not come face to face, and do not feel connected 
to other users in the community.  
Also as the PSS offers little responsibility for the users it is making them treat the product with less care, as they perceive it 
not to be within their responsibility. This behaviour may be a result of a weak institution, that does not prescribe how to 
behave around shared cars. Attempts of implementing a strong regulatory institution has not been successful. 
 
Whom: User 
 
Unsustainable behaviour: PSS vandalism (derived from: Users or passers-by throw bikes into canals and trash bins) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 1 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Misuse products in PSS 
When: Post 
How: This behaviour was not found to be connected to any PSS strategic characteristics.     
 
Why: The reason for the behaviour to happen may be a lack of institutions for how to treat shared products, PSSs, or even 
bicycles as the cycling culture in Manchester is quite sparsely disseminated.  
 
Whom: User or passers-by 
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users claim ineligeble possession over PSS products (derived from: Users put own lock on 
bikes) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 6 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Misuse products in PSS  
When: Post 
How: When PSS offerings based on availability does not live up to user expectations, users might claim possession over 
the products ineligibly. Additionally, when the ownership of the product is never transferred to the user despite it being 
their possession it may contribute to the unsustainable behaviour.  
 
==PAGE:136==
 
 
 
129 
 
Why: The reason for the behaviour to happen is that users in general do not feel ownership over the bicycles. This is due 
to the fact that the products often shift between different users, is used in a safe environment of their back-yards, is shared 
among many, that they do not come face to face, and do not feel connected to other users in the community. It may be 
result of a weak institution prescribing how to share in PSS. 
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users print but do not use or need  
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 4 
PSS category: Product-oriented PSS 
What: Overconsume PSS offering  
When: During  
How: This behaviour was not found to be connected to any PSS strategic characteristics. 
 
Why: This behaviour is happening mainly due to the fact that the users of the printers often are not the ones who are 
responsible for the consumption costs and do not feel an ownership over the product. Additionally, the behaviour is 
generally accepted in some communities why users cannot conceive any other convenient way of working. Hence it might 
be a result of a weak institution prescribing how to consume sustainably.  
 
Whom: User 
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users reserve products without using it decreasing availability for others (derived from: 
Washing machines or cars) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 7 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Overconsume PSS offering 
When: Pre 
How: The PSS is offering an expansion of benefits to encourage the onboarding of new users, yet this can easily create a 
need that did not exist before. Simply offering availability as part of the valueproposition can encourage users’ desire to use 
the offering more than needed. On the other hand, if PSS offerings based on availability does not live up to user 
expectations, it can also make users reserve the products unreasonably. This is further enabled by the level of digitalisation 
that increases the convenience of reserving.  
 
Why: The option to reserve the PSS products can lead to a shared understanding among users that you must reserve the 
product in order to conveniently gain access to use it. The feeling of it being inacceptable to reserve is not established as an 
institution.  
Additionally, the fact that the products often shift between different users, is shared among many, and do not feel connected 
to other users in the community can make users be less considerate and reserve the product unrestrained. 
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users replace more sustainable means of transportation with PSS offering (found in: Shared-
cars, e-scooters and Uber) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 5-6 
PSS category: Use-oriented and Result-oriented PSS  
What: Overconsume PSS offering 
When: Pre 
How: When buying acces to a PSS is much cheaper than buying the actual product in can increase consumption. Users 
having less responsibility will make it even more favourable for them. Simply offering availability as part of the 
valueproposition can encourage users’ desire to use the offering more than before using the PSS. This is further enabled by 
the level of digitalisation that increases the convenience of using the PSS. 
 
Why: This behaviour might be a result of a stabilised cognitive institution, where the use of shared transportation means 
has become so normalised for users that they cannot conceive other convenient ways of getting from A to B in certain 
situations. Additionally when users feel little responsibility over the PSS, it may make them want to use is much more 
because it is so convenient for them.  
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users swap and buy accessories often 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 7 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Overconsume PSS offering 
When: Post 
How: The PSS is offering an expansion of benefits to encourage the onboarding of new users, yet this can easily create a 
need that did not exist before. If that expansion of benefit is offering the option to add special meaning to the PSS product 
it may contribute to the overconsumption. Simply offering availability as part of the valueproposition can also encourage 
users’ desire to use the offering more than needed.  
When users have difficulties determining the value they get with pay per use, it can create unsustainable behaviours as they 
will want to get their money's worth.  
 
==PAGE:137==
 
 
 
130 
 
 
 
Why: This behaviour might be a result of an existing normative institution in the Western world, where users desire new 
products to obtain social status, which Bugaboo uses to be attractive. If the period of ownership is relatively long it can 
increase the desire to overconsume offerings in the PSS giving a sense of newness.  
This behaviour might also be a result of weak institutions that does not prescribe how to behave in the PSS.  
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users swap stroller every year  
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 6 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Overconsume PSS offering 
When: Post 
How: The PSS is offering an expansion of benefits to encourage the onboarding of new users, yet this can easily create a 
need that did not exist before. Simply offering availability as part of the valueproposition can also encourage users’ desire 
to use the offering more than needed. 
When users have difficulties determining the value they get with pay per use, it can create unsustainable behaviours as they 
will want to get their money's worth.  
 
Why: This behaviour might be a result of an existing normative institution in the Western world, where users desire new 
products to obtain social status, which Bugaboo uses to be attractive. If the period of ownership is relatively long it can 
increase the desire to overconsume offerings in the PSS giving a sense of newness.  
This behaviour might also be a result of weak institutions that does not prescribe how to behave in the PSS.  
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users over-charge phone 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 4 
PSS category: Product-oriented PSS 
What: Treat products in PSS carelessly  
When: Post 
How: When the PSS is offering to manage life cycle extionsion services some users perceive the value as a transfer of 
responsibility to the provider, which can make users treat the product with less care. 
 
Why: This behaviour is happening because users own the product and consider it within their right to treat the product 
carelessly if it feels most convenient to them. If users have already paid for life cycle extension services, they will be more 
inclined to treat the product carelessly. 
This behaviour might be a result of weak institutions that does not prescribe how to use insurance services for personal 
products. 
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users use products inexpediently (derived from: Stroller) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 9 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
 What: Treat products in PSS carelessly  
When: During 
How: When the ownership of the product is never transferred to the user despite it being in their possession and they have 
little responsibility it may contribute to the unsustainable behaviour. As the provider is also managing life cycle activities 
users have even less responsibility during use, it can be difficult to perceive what value they get and make users treat the 
strollers with less care.  
 
Why: The reason for the behaviour to happen is because users do not feel ownership nor responsibility over the product 
thereby making users treat the product carelessly if it is most convenient to them. Also, the fact that the product will only 
be in the possession of the user once decouples the users from others and the community. As the period of ownership is 
relatively long, the product is used frequently, and in a safe environment it can contribute to users treating products with 
less care or even form a habit of careless behaviour.  
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users damage PSS products (derived from: Shared-car services) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 10 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Treat products in PSS carelessly  
When: During, post  
How: Users may damage PSS products when they see the value of it as a transfer of responsibility. When the ownership 
of the product is never transferred to the user despite it being in their possession and they have little responsibility it may 
contribute to the unsustainable behaviour. The car is only fulfilling a basic need (transport) therefore the user has little or 
no attachment to the product. Lastly the PSS is offering high availability enabling the use of it in spontaneous contexts 
which can lead to unsustainable behaviour.  
 
Why: The reason for the behaviour to happen is that users do not feel ownership nor responsibility over the product 
thereby making users treat the product carelessly if it is most convenient to them. Also the fact that the period of ownership 
is relatively short and users do not come face to face with others they will be more inclined to treat the product carelessly. 
==PAGE:138==
 
 
 
131 
 
This behaviour might be a result of a weak institution, that does not prescribe how to behave around shared cars. Attempts 
of implementing a strong regulatory institution has not been successful. 
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users leave trash in cars 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 10 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Treat products in PSS carelessly  
When: Post 
 How: Users may leave trash in shared cars when they see the value of it as a transfer of responsibility. When the ownership 
of the product is never transferred to the user despite it being in their possession and they have little responsibility it may 
contribute to the unsustainable behaviour. The car is only fulfilling a basic need (transport) therefore the user has little or 
no attachment to the product.  
 
Why: The reason for the behaviour to happen is because users do not feel ownership nor responsibility over the product 
thereby making users treat the product carelessly if it is most convenient to them. This behaviour might be a result of weak 
institutions that does not prescribe how to behave around shared cars. 
Additionally, the fact that the products is used in a safe environment, often shift between different users, do not come face 
to face with others, and do not feel connected to other users in the community can make users be less considerate and leave 
trash in the cars. 
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users park recklessly (derived from: Voi e-scooters) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 10 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Treat products in PSS carelessly  
When: Post 
How: When the user cheaply gains access to the product in pay per use users may feel that the product has low value. The 
product is only fulfilling a basic need (transport) therefore the user has little or no attachment to it. When the ownership of 
the product is never transferred to the user despite it being in their possession and the provider has partial responsibility it 
may contribute to the unsustainable behaviour. As the PSS provides high availability it can be used in spontaneours contexts 
and thus it may be treated recklessly. 
 
Why: The reason for the behaviour to happen is because users do not feel ownership nor responsibility over the product 
thereby making users treat the product carelessly if it is most convenient to them. This is further enhanced by the period of 
ownership being relatively short. This behaviour might be a result of weak institutions that does not prescribe how to 
behave around shared e-scooters 
 
Whom: User  
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users use the products undesireably (derived from: Users close washing machine door after 
use)  
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 4 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Treat products in PSS carelessly  
When: Post 
How: When the PSS offers low responsibility during use it may contribute to users treating the products carelessly.     
 
Why: The reason for this behaviour to happen is that users do not feel ownership over the products. As they additionally 
do not come face to face with other users they will treat the products carelessly if it feels most convenient. This behaviour 
might be a result of weak institutions that does not prescribe how to operate washing machines and tumble dryers. 
Whom: User 
 
Unsustainable behaviour: Users do not conduct performance enhancing maintenance (derived from: Users do not 
remove lint from dryer) 
Parameters that influence the behaviour: 5 
PSS category: Use-oriented PSS 
What: Treat products in PSS carelessly  
When: Post 
How: When the PSS offers low responsibility during use it may contribute to users treating the products carelessly.     
 
Why: The reason for this behaviour to happen is that users do not feel responsible for the product making users treat the 
product carelessly if it is most convenient to them. As the product additionally shared amongst many others users they may 
treat the products carelessly if it feels most convenient. This behaviour might be a result of weak institutions that does not 
prescribe how to operate washing machines and tumble dryers. 
 
Whom: User

Appendix XII: Workshop analysis of 
observations 
General description 
One participant had the PSS course this semester and has just handed in his final report 
for the course whose project served as case for the workshop. The project facilitated a take-
back system for fast-fashion brands in a B2B context yet also with B2C touchpoints. He 
describes it himself as: “Cloop a unified clothing take-back system where we handle the take back process 
of clothing and facilitate responsible recycling of clothing if our partners do not have established recycling 
partners/infrastructure.” The other participant had taken the course in a previous semester. 
This meant that this participant in general asked a lot of probing questions about the Cloop 
project as he had not himself been part of developing it. Worth mentioning is that the 
second participant has an above average knowledge basis of behavioural design tools in 
general compared to students in the D&I programme at DTU. 
 
Step 0: Create persona 
Notes from observing: 
- 
Chooses end-customer instead of business-customer in the B2B PSS 
- 
B: Asks about ”Likes” for persona and if it is only regarding clothes?  
- 
A: Mentions normative challenges such as fitting in 
- 
Exercise took 1 minute longer than the 5 minutes given 
 
Findings from output of workshop: 
- 
Only focused on the aspects of the persona related to their PSS, in this case buying 
clothes. E.g. in “Pains”, participants write “planning to shop” 
 
Step 1: Identify unsustainable behaviours 
Notes from observing: 
- 
Used 2 minutes to read exercise guide, and then goes straight to the tool (and not 
inspirational examples) 
o This can be due to the time limit as indicated in the evaluation form: “More 
time for reading and digesting the examples could be helpful.” 
- 
Approached the exercise by thinking of traditional rebound effects 
o B: Encourage users to change wardrobe or buy clothes more often  
o B: Are there incentives to take better care of the clothes? 
§ A: the condition of the returned items is not a dealbreaker, and it 
will still be taken back 
- 
Assess whether a behaviour is sustainable or not by comparing it to alternatives, e.g. 
if clothes end up in incineration or landfill with scarce materials.  
- 
Have slight difficulty in understanding cycle phases in terms of when the behaviour 
occurs, especially the distinction between post and pre! 
- 
Put their previous UAC in after 4 minutes 
- 
Uses ecosystem map from previous work to keep track of when user does what as 
they did not have an updated UAC for their developed solution. 
- 
Uses inspirational examples of unsustainable behaviours in PSS after finding 6 
unsustainable behaviours in their own PSS 
- 
The 12 minutes duration of the exercise works well as interesting discussions arise 
after longer time of identifying the “obvious” unsustainable behaviours 
- 
Had difficulty in only thinking of the end-user (in B2B PSS) 
==PAGE:145==
 
 
 
138 
 
 
 
o E.g. discussed that the shop owner can have unsustainable behaviours by 
not promoting the initiative.  
- 
Thinks of “behaviours”/rebound effects first, then tries to place them in the UAC 
- 
Note: Does the UAC even help identifying behaviours (together with 
inspirational examples) or are they just an obstacle? 
- 
Practical suggestions for the tool from participants: 
o Be able to move persona over to the tool 
o Possibility to have more than one persona (both end-user, business, etc.) 
 
Findings from output of workshop: 
- 
The “behaviours” identified using the tool was not all formulated as a behaviour, 
but more general problems or reasons for an unsustainable behaviour without 
making it clear what the behaviour is. Examples are: 
o “No incentive to take good care of clothes while using” 
o “Makes the illusion that fast fashion is sustainable. Easier to choose over 
actual sustainable clothing” 
o “Perception of donation to a NGO is better, e.g. RedCross” 
- 
Suggestion: We need to make it clearer how to formulate behaviours 
- 
Found “behaviours” for not only the persona (end-user), but also the business, who 
is the customer of the PSS (B2B). Examples are: 
o “Stores not making the [recycling] bin accessible” 
- 
Suggestion: Move persona creation to after selecting target behaviour 
- 
The placement of the “behaviours” is not aligned throughout the UAC. However, 
did not impact their ideation 
 
Step 2: Select target behaviour 
Notes from observing: 
- 
Reads instructions in approximately 30 seconds 
o Uses time to understand the scale from 1-5 
- 
B: Disagrees that the easier the behaviour is to change, the better it would be to 
change. Instead suggest the opposite! 
- 
Only one participant (B) suggests to copy in the behaviours from previous exercise 
o Participant says: “Let’s move on” from re-writing behaviours 
- 
Their understanding of unsustainable and how unsustainable seems narrow 
- 
When discussing the question “How easy is behaviour to change?” 
o Participants begin ideating 
o Both from the perspective of whether other designers have been successful 
in changing the behaviour and in relation to their persona 
- 
Took 5 minutes longer than anticipated (had 7 behaviours to evaluate) 
- 
Talked a lot with each other during this exercise 
- 
Practical suggestions for the tool from participants: 
o Automatic sum function to calculate points 
 
Findings from output of workshop: 
- 
The behaviours were not rated in relation to each other, e.g. 
o “More clothing is bought due to rebates (incentives)” rated 5 in occurrence 
o “No incentive to take good care of cloths while using” rated 4 in occurrence 
o Here it is obvious that you would wear a piece of clothing more often than 
you would buy a new piece of clothing due to discounts 
- 
Note: Could be due to time limitation 
==PAGE:146==
 
 
 
139 
 
- 
Suggestions: Should consider what alternative would be and compare 
environmental impact categories across the different behaviours. E.g. 
change wording to: How severe is the environmental impact from the 
behaviour compared to what the alternative would be (1-5)? 
- 
Suggestion: add weighting to severity category to increase importance.  
 
Step 3: Identify what affects the behaviour 
Notes from observing: 
- 
Not formulated as a behaviour initially 
o Facilitators interrupted participants as they needed to formulate the 
“behaviours” as actual actions by their persona, yet they still had difficulties 
- 
Talk about ideas for how to solve unsustainable behaviours as they identify reasons 
- 
Do not speak much together for this exercise 
- 
Did not know how to formulate desired behaviour after 5 whys 
o Started choosing between whys or branches of whys 
o Facilitators interrupted the participants to make it clear that they needed to 
formulate a desired behaviour, not “select their whys” 
- 
Suggestions: Have the identification of desired behaviour in additional 
exercise to not confuse participants 
- 
Went overtime, about 5-10 minutes 
- 
A: Really good method and first time to try it 
 
Findings from output of workshop: 
- 
Not all statements are formulated as whys leading to the other e.g.: 
o Behaviour: I will return my old clothing, get rewards and use them to buy 
more clothing, now that I know it is not an environmental burden | instead 
of buying fewer long lasting clothing. Why: It is marketed as such. Why: 
They say they can reuse it sustainably. Why: We need structural changes 
(sustainability minded) 
- 
Formulation of behaviour is crucial here as well, in order to not get off track 
 
Step 4: Select strategies 
Notes from observing: 
- 
B: Looks difficult/complicated! 
- 
After 3 minutes they have an impression of how to start, yet still confusion of how 
to reply.  
o Difficult distinction between desired and undesired behaviour, why 
facilitators give a hint and behaviours are copied onto the frame. 
- 
Instructions of how to use the tool is given by the facilitators after 5 minutes 
- 
Confusion relates to: 
o Many steps and things to go through 
o “Recreation” of a box corresponding to what is shown in the illustrations. 
§ A: The word “Imitate” confuses! 
§ A: Bulletins on the axes to copy the corresponding figures 
o The question regarding importance. Does it relate to desired or undesired 
behaviour? à stated under headline so it should make sense after reading 
- 
Finish 1 minute early 
- 
They are able to decode the findings from the tool correctly  
 
==PAGE:147==
 
 
 
140 
 
 
 
Findings from output of workshop: 
- 
Suggestion: Would be nice to have a clearer indication of what the 
desired and undesired behaviour is! 
- 
Note: Is the complexity of the tool worth the outcome that users get 
from using it?  
 
Step 5: Create ideas 
Notes from observing: 
- 
Discusses how to use the tool for a couple of minutes 
o Especially whether the orange boxes scoping obtrusiveness and principles 
should be moved in relation to previous exercise 
- 
Starts ideating with the first card (level of control) displaying all behaviour 
intervention strategies/principles 
o Uses 5 minutes on this card 
o Uses the concrete strategies to identify ideas 
- 
Moves on the orange box in obtrusiveness-card 
o Ideates on obtrusive ideas that are not recommended 
o After getting idea with high obtrusiveness, participant suggests to move on 
- 
B: Would rather promote a behaviour than discourage the opposite behaviour 
o Personal preference considered during ideation 
- 
B: Is it about it being trendy or that it feels new to you?  
- 
Some ideas go beyond the initial behaviour change wanted idea (desired behaviour): 
o “Choosing a material where it is not an issue(unsustainable) that it is only 
used very few times” 
- 
Goes through the tool card by card 
- 
Works parallel to each other after about 15 minutes ideating by themselves 
- 
Meaning: participants discuss who and why users consume clothes frequently 
- 
Shift to talking about the undesired behaviour during exposure 
o B: Timing is the same as exposure 
§ The ideas identified here show to be quite different from each other 
indicating that the test persons understand the difference  
- 
Do not work through direction (intentionally?) 
- 
Bring their own existing examples into the discussion for ideas to change behaviour 
- 
Practical suggestions for the tool from participants: 
o B: Do not know if it is annoying that examples are repeated (know that you 
didn’t make them yourselves) 
 
Findings from output of workshop: 
- 
For each card, ideas were generated during the 30 minutes set aside for the exercise 
- 
The ideas were quite broad, and more described general directions associated with 
the cards, such as “inform about impact”, but not how 
- 
A possible limitation of the tool was thought to be whether the tool would be too 
product-oriented, but most ideas were connected to service. However, ideas were 
within product (2.5), service (12.5), infrastructure (2) and network (1). Furthermore, 
ideas were also concerning normative (3), cognitive (1) and regulatory (2) 
- 
By placing ideas into the control/obtrusiveness-matrix, it was seen that ideas were 
mostly placed in with unobtrusive are and where user is in control. Only two ideas 
had product in control, although these were both oriented towards changing 
regulatory structures

Appendix XXII: Case study analysis of 
ideas according to PSS elements 
 
Behaviour 
change card 
Idea 
Elements from Mont 
(2004a) 
Control 
Shower turns off automatically after certain 
amount of minutes, e.g. 5 minutes, and it is not 
possible to turn on again within a timespan 
Product 
Users must set a fixed time for the number of 
minutes they wish to shower. Countdown clock in 
shower. 
Product 
After pushing soap dispenser, shower will turn off 
automatically 
Product 
Shower turns off automatically, but you can decide 
to turn it on again 
Product 
Cheaper rent for one of the users using least 
minutes per shower on average 
Customer satisfaction 
Provide user with feedback on water usage and 
consequences of high water usage 
Service 
Monthly amount of minutes available that you 
yourself must distribute. Must pay extra for 
additional time 
Business viability 
Will stop taking in new water after certain amount 
of time resulting in colder water 
Product 
Obtrusiveness 
Automatically charges from rent if using more 
water than within payment agreement 
Regulatory 
Responsibility 
Collective amount of water that must be 
distributed to all users creating shared 
responsibility 
Infrastructure/normative 
Showers only open in limited hours, forcing more 
people to shower at the same time 
Normative/regulatory 
Campaign: would your like your younger family 
members to be able to take clean showers? 
Cognitive 
Display of average shower time of all users 
Normative 
If you have leftover water on your “account” you 
donate clean drinking water to Africa 
Service 
Poster with person in lack of clean drinking water: 
Not everyone has the privilige of having clean 
water 
Normative 
Meaning 
Show how much drinking water have been used 
during shower 
Service 
Set common goal for how long you should shower 
between inhabitants in dormatory, and track 
whether user reach that goal or not 
Service 
If users reach common goal for water usage in 
dormatory, they get funds to actitivies such as 
parties, etc. 
Customer satisfaction 
Sticker in shower: “We know you save water using 
our product, but help us save more by taking 
shorter showers” 
Environmental soundness 
Poster: “There is no good excuse for taking a long 
shower!” 
Cognitive 
Encouragement 
People can save up points to get a free coffee every 
time thay take a short shower 
Customer satisfaction 
Applause sound effects every time you walk out of 
a short shower 
Product/normative 
Empathy 
Competitions between the different CPH villages 
Network/service
